STAFF APPRAISAL REPORT
RUSSIAN FEDERATION
ENVIRONMENTAL MANAGEMENT PROJEC r
LOAN AND PROJECT SUMMARY
BORROWER:           Russian Federation.
BENEFICIARIES:      Ministry of Environment Protection and Natural Resources (MEPNR);
Ministry of Finance (MOF); Regional Governments in the Urals, Upper
Volga and North Caucasus; and private and state enterprises.
AMOUNT:             US$110 million equivalent.
TERMS:              17 years, including five years of grace, at the Bank's standard variable
interest rate.
ON-LENDING
TERMS:             The proceeds of the Bank loan (US$57.7 million) would be onlent to the sub-
borrowers under the National Pollution Abatement Facility (NPAF) and
Investment Project Preparation Advance (IPPA) facility in US dollars, with
an interest rate spread satisfactory to the Bank (initially 400 basis points) and
with maturities generally up to eight years, including up to three years of
grace. The sub-borrowers will bear the US dollar exchange risk.
PROJECT
OBJEcnvEs:         The Russian Federation has requested the World Bank to assist in establishing
an Environmental Framework Program (EFP) whose objectives are to (a)
incorporate environmental and natural resource management concerns directly
into the economic, social and political adjustment process at the federal and
regional levels of government, including the executive and legislative
branches; (b) strengthen and streamline institutional and organizational
structures for environmental and natural resource management; (c) improve
environmental and natural resource policy and strategy formulation and
implementation; (d) upgrade environmental and natural resource management
systems; (e) strengthen financial delivery mechanisms to address priority
environmental management investment needs through the setting up and initial
capitalization of the NPAF; and (f) facilitate the flow of donor and
multilateral funds and resources to the environmental protection sector. The
project would provide critical support to the EFP.
PROJECT
DESCREMON:         The project consists of the following five main components: (1) Institutional
and Policy Strengthening, including the following two subcomponents: (a)
Policy and Regulatory Support; and (b) Environmental Epidemiology; (2)
Water Quality and Water Resource Management; (3) Hazardous Waste
Management; (4) the NPAF for funding economically and financially viable
pollution abatement projects; and (5) the Center for Project Preparation and
Implementation under MEPNR.



Loan and Project Summary                                                             vi,
JUSTICATION
AND RISKS:          The specific benefits that are expected to accrue as a result of this project are
as follows:
(1) the establishment of national databases and standardized methodologies
and systems for the collection and verification of environmental health and
hazardous waste generation data for policymakers at the national and regional
levels;
(2) the upgrading and adoption of overall environmental action plans based
on environmental health priorities at the national level and in the Upper
Volga and Urals regions, and the first phase implementation of these plans to
test and demonstrate their application;
(3) the development and Phase I implementation and adoption of specific
action plans for water quality and resource management in the Upper Volga,
Urals and North Caucasus Regions, and sectoral environmental action and
Phase I implementation plans for: (a) iron and steel; (b) non-ferrous metals;
(c) oil refining and petrochemicals; (d) basic organic and inorganic chemicals;
and (e) agriculture with particular attention to intensive livestock operations;
(4) the formulation and adoption of improved regulatory systems and
enforcement mechanisms for water quality and water resource management
in the Upper Volga and North Caucasus regions; for hazardous waste
management in the Upper Volga region and for environmental assessment at
the national level;
(5) the dissemination of the experience and the results of project financed
ativities to other regions in the Russian Federation through a national
outreach program;
(6) the establishment and management of a national facility to fund private
sector investment in pollution abatement and improved resource utilization and
recovery. The effectiveness of this facility will be judged on the basis of the
amount of private loan and equity funds it is able to leverage for pollution
abatement, the extent of pollution reduction achieved through its investments
and its ability to address pollution priorities identified under items (2) and (3)
above;
(7) the improved organization, management and training of staff of
environmental regulatory agencies at the federal level and in the Upper
Volga, the Urals, and the North Caucasus regions; and
(8) facilitating donor participation in the environmental sector in Russia
through the establishment of a Center for Project Preparation and
Implementation and donor involvement in project workshops.
The main risks that the project may face include: (1) the fragmented decision
making process for environmental management at the federal and regional



viii                                                       Environmental Management Project
levels; (2) the shortage of experienced and qualified Russian specialists in
environmental economics and law; (3) the poor environmental regulation
enforcement capability of the legal system in Russia; (4) conflicting mandates
and lack of cooperation between government agencies and even between
departments within the same agency; (5) possible delays in project
implementation due to a lack of familiarity with Bank procedures and the
requirements of project management in general; (6) difficulties in arranging
local cost financing for pollution abatement; and (7) potential adverse impacts
of high inflation on projects financed by the NPAF. The project will build
on initiatives already being introduced by the Government in order to address
these issues. Firstly, at the federal level, the project will be under the
direction of the recently formed Federal Commission on the Environment
(with MEPNR acting as the Secretariat of this Commission), which has been
set up to improve inter-agency cooperation and decision making for
environmental management. The regional project areas were selected on the
basis of already existing arrangements for promoting inter-agency cooperation
for environmental management and their government's strong commitments
to environmental quality improvement (usually because of strong local
community  pressures).   The project proposes an early  start to a
comprehensive training program to improve skills in environmental policy and
regulation, as well as in the technical areas of environmental epidemiology,
hazardous waste, water quality management and project management. The
use of twinning arrangements with foreign institutes will ensure practical
application in the development of these skills. Trained personnel will also be
delegated to work at regional, as well federal level to transfer their skills. In
the participating regions, cooperative arrangements for pollution abatement at
major polluting enterprises will be negotiated with appropriate incentive
arrangements for compliance with agreed permit schedules.
To minimize risks of project implementation delays, framework agreements
have been reached between federal and regional agencies that will be
responsible for project implementation laying out in detail the responsibilities
and arrangements for component implementation. These agreements will be
formalized as binding resolutions by the respective parties after loan
effectiveness. The early setting up and staffing of the Center for Project
Preparation and Implementation (CPPI) and recruitment of a Project
Management Advisory Firm to assist the CPPI with the setting up of
procurement, financial and information management systems has been
designated to reduce risks of implementation delays. The NPAF will be
equipped to assist borrowers to put together packages for project financing
including a full array of financing arrangements from local and foreign sources.
One of the criteria on which the NPAF operations will be judged will be its
ability to leverage other sources of financing. IPPAs would enable enterprises
to develop high quality projects to the stage where they meet the requirement
for NPAF project financing. The pollution abatement projects that will be
targeted by the NPAF have a number of inherent characteristics that will
protect them from the impacts of high inflation, namely: (i) recovery of
valuable resources from waste streams with zero or negative costs; and (ii) end
products that have a high economic value in world markets and are exportable.

STAFF APPRAISAL REPORT
RussiAN FEDERATION
ENVIRONMENTAL MANAGEMENT PROJECT
I. BACKGROUND
A.    Introduction
1.1          In the past 70 years, the Russian Federation has inherited an enormously costly
environmental legacy from decades of inefficient economic development that basically failed to include
environmental factors in macroeconomic and sector development strategies and national investment
plans. For the Chernobyl disaster alone, the cost of environmental mismanagement has been estimated
at tens of billions of dollars. There are also ongoing costs across the economy from, for example,
natural resource loss and pollution, that are not stated explicitly in the national accounting process or
sector budgets but are obviously a significant percentage of GNP. The Russian Federation is not
unique in this respect, most industrializing countries followed a similar development path, although
in many cases, made more dramatic structural adjustments at an earlier stage of development.
1.2          In the past, many countries in transition from a centralized state economy to a more
market orientated system, adopted a policy of economic efficiency in its limited traditional sense of
ignoring many environmental issue, to pursue the adjustment process. Currently, however, many
reasons can be found for incorporating environmental and natural resource management concerns
directly into the economic adjustment process-as is being done in Russia and some other countries
of the FSU. The following arguments will illustrate the point.
1.3          First, as the country searches for new sources of growth and comparative advantage,
it may (as in the case of Russia) use natural resources such as forests, lands, oil, gas, minerals, water,
and vegetation much more intensively. It is plausible that natural resource-intensive exports would
be expanded and promoted to replace the old traditional exports. In such a case, decision-makers have
to cost existing natural resources used in trade at their full opportunity cost, to avoid giving a false
sense of comparative advantage in the short and medium term. If these resources are not adequately
costed at the macro level, use rates of those resources will be much higher, and serious misallocation
of resources, environmental degradation, or depletion may occur. In some cases, irreversible damages
may also occur in the country (for example, destruction of biodiversity resources and fragile
ecosystems).
1.4          An important general point regarding adjustments during such transition periods is that
any set of reforms implemented at the national level (such as privatization, industrial restructuring,
monetary and market liberalization) will have a great influence and impact on the allocation of
resources, on the environment and the use and management of natural resources ("natural capital" for
short). These policies are not at all neutral with regard to natural resources and the environment.
1.5          The second argument for including environmental concerns in macro-planning is that
the solution to many environmental quality and resource management problems rests on major
institutional and organizational changes (for example, regarding property rights, taxes and subsidies,
regulations, laws, and private sector participation). If the reform process does not take into account
these institutional realities at the beginning, some of the most important sources of national capital
depletion and degradation will be very difficult to correct at the end of the process. For example, if
property rights, user rights, or management rights over a significant amount of forests, for instance,



2                                                          Environmental Management Project
are given to an economic agent during the reform process, without appropriate environmental
assessment it will be almost impossible to reverse those rights after the reform process; or if they are
reversible, the reassignment will be at very high costs.
1.6          An increasing number of government decision-makers in the executive and legislative
branches at the federal, regional and local levels in the Russian Federation are searching for ways to
restructure and reform the economy to promote efficient and more sustainable economic growth-an
impossible goal without taking environmental quality and natural resource management factors into
consideration. Some important initial steps have been taken, but much more needs to be done at all
levels of legislative and executive government, or this initial momentum will be lost, along with the
enormous opportunities that currently exist for influencing the reform process. The EFP and the
Environmental Management Project are designed to help the Government of Russia to meet this urgent
need and to take advantage of these opportunities.
B.    Economic Context
1.7          In the centrally planned economy of the FSU the standard success criteria of a market
economy-efficiency and profitability-were often ignored and an industrial culture evolved where
waste and disregard for efficiency were normal when the end objective was "pure production".
Industries in the FSU consumed more than twice as much energy and raw materials to produce the
same amount of final product as in a typical market economy. Industrial development under the Soviet
system emphasized heavy industry with extensively developed and polluting iron and steel industries,
nonferrous metal smelters, large oil refineries and petrochemical complexes, etc. In the past 20 years
major investments have been made in the military/industrial complex, thereby greatly restraining
modernization of the industrial base of the country.
1.8          In 1991 there were 22,900 industrial enterprises in Russia accounting for over 30%
of total employment. However, since the mid-1980s, industry's share of both NMP and employment
has fallen from 48.6% to 42.2% and 32.4% to 30.9% respectively. Over the period, agriculture and
construction have expanded as a percentage of NMP from 14.1% to 19.9% and 10.5% to 12.7%
respectively. As in the other sectors of the economy, agriculture is inefficient in the use of agricultural
inputs, as well as experiencing high losses in the storage, transport and marketing of the final
products.
1.9          Russia is also a major producer and exporter of timber, producing 228 million cubic
meters (m3) per year. However, unsustainable natural resource policies have resulted in over-
exploitation of the forests in some regions. Logging has consequently had to move steadily eastward
into Siberia, which accounted for 34% of Russia's sawn timber production in 1989 compared with
25% in 1970.
1.10         Russia has the fourth largest fishery industry in the world (behind Japan, the United
States and China). However, habitat loss and pollution from industrial and urban development as well
as over-exploitation, have had a disastrous effect on inland fisheries. Prior to World War II, the catch
from inland water bodies exceeded that from the ocean shelf, but today the ratio is 10 to 1 in favor
of the ocean. Catches of fish have decreased in the Sea of Azov and the Caspian Sea by over 90%.
1.11         The FSU was the world's leading producer of all major fuels with large proven
deposits of oil, gas and coal. Oil and gas currently account for 45% of Russia's foreign exchange
earnings. This industry is also characterized by low efficiency and waste, as readily evidenced by



Background                                                                             3
extensive oil contamination of land and water resources in oil-producing regions of the Urals and
Western Siberia and the widespread flaring of associated gas. Russia is also the second largest
generator of hydro-electricity (after the United States), producing 224 billion kilowatt hours (kwh),
or 13% of the country's total electrical output.
1.12         The FSU was also a major producer of many types of minerals for example; gold,
chromium, iron, magnesium, manganese, mercury, nickel, tungsten, and asbestos. The Russian
Federation is particularly well endowed with these deposits (in the mineral-rich Ural mountains,
Siberia and the Russian Far East).
C.    Geographic Context
1.13         The Russian Federation covers 17,075,400 square kilometers (kin2), almost twice the
size of the United States and 70% of the territory of the FSU. Its population in 1991, 148.5 million,
was exceeded only by China, India, the United States, Indonesia and Brazil. Even though its
population density is low on average by world standards, it is characterized by wide regional
variations from as high as 52-77 per km2 in the economically well developed regions in Central and
European Russia, to less than 1 per km2 in parts of Siberia.
1.14         Extending halfway around the northern hemisphere and covering much of eastern and
northeastern Europe and all of northern Asia, the territory of Russia displays an enormous variety of
landforms and ecological systems. The major ecological/climatic lowland zones stretch east to west
across the country, and are made up of various tundra subzones in the extreme northern areas, mostly
above the Arctic Circle, that give way to a vast forest belt covering approximately two-thirds of the
entire country (see attached map, IRBD 25119). The forest zone may be further divided into two
subzones: taiga forests and mixed forests. The taiga is characterized by the preponderance of
coniferous forests of spruce, larch, fir and siberian stone pine. Deciduous species-birch, aspen and
alder-are of secondary importance. In the mixed forests, so called "broad-leaved" species (for
example, the oak) appear together with the conifers. Further south the land turns to open steppes, and
finally to hot drylands and semi-deserts. Massive mountain ranges such as the Ural, the Caucasus and
the mountain areas of Siberia (for example, the Altay, the Sayans, Lake Baikal and the Trans-Baikal
regions, and the mountains of Far Eastern Siberia) interrupt these lowland features.
1.15         Positioned in latitudes where precipitation mostly exceeds evaporation, Russia contains
many long rivers, lakes and wetlands. Rivers such as the Volga in European Russia and the Ob in
Western Siberia are among the world's longest. Lake Baikal is the second deepest freshwater lake in
the world and contains 20% of the world's total freshwater-more than all of the Great Lakes of North
America combined.
1.16          "Russia" derives not from "russkaya" (pertaining to the Russian nationality) but from
"rossiyskaya" (pertaining to the country of Russia), and this vast and diverse territory is in fact the
home of about 120 nationalities or ethnic groups of which 82% represent ethnic Russians. This
situation is reflected in the Republics' complex system of administration. There are 20 autonomous
republics within the Russian Federation, as well as five autonomous regions and 10 national districts.
The remainder of the Russian Federation is administratively divided into 6 territories (krais) and 49
regions (oblasts).
1.17         Throughout Soviet history, the size of cities grew  rapidly in response to
industrialization; in 1959, 48% of the population in the Soviet Union lived in cities and 52% in rural



4                                                          Environmental Management Project
areas, but by 1990 the figures were 66% and 34% respectively. In Russia where the population
increased by 10 million in the past two decades, the share of city inhabitants in the total rose from
69% in 1979 to 74% in 1991. However, majority of the population and the industrial activity is
concentrated on the territory west of the Ural mountains. On the other hand, the bulk of the nation's
natural resources are now located east of the Ural mountains.



STAFF APPRAISAL REPORT
RUSSIAN FEDERATION
ENVIRONMENTAL MANAGEMENT PROJECT
II.  THE ENVIRONMENT SECTOR
A.      Overview
2.1             The development of the system of environmental management in the FSU has been
characterized as fragmented and uncoordinated with more than ten government agencies having some
responsibility for environmental concerns. The long-standing problem has been that the implementing
ministries and overseeing government bodies have a built-in conflict of interest in being responsible
for both economic targets and environmental protection. Only in 1988 was a Union-Republican State
Environmental Protection Committee (Goskompriroda) formed with a mandate to strengthen direct
regulation and enforcement of environmental norms and standards and to coordinate the work of
ministries and committees responsible for environmental management and nature protection. Since that
time, several reorganization attempts to better integrate environmental management under the present
MEPNR have failed to achieve their objectives in full. This is mainly explained by the legacy of a
system of centralized management of the economy that emphasized production over efficiency,
concentrated pollution-intensive industries in enormous complexes, compartmentalized decision-
making, and treated natural resources as free goods.
2.2             The failure of the previous, centrally planned Soviet system  to efficiently and
effectively manage regional economic development and environmental issues was implicitly recognized
in the Russian Federal Treaty of March 14, 1992,1' that formally devolves much of the former powers
and resources of the central ministries and committees to regional and local level agencies. The trend
toward decentralization has major implications for solving environmental and natural resource
management problems. These changes, while creating much uncertainty and confusion, also present
unprecedented opportunities for providing strategically targeted support and assistance, based on sound
economic, social, environmental, and natural resource management principles. The Environmental
Framework Program (EFP) and the Environmental Management Project (EMP) have been designed
to support these changes.
B.     Enviromental and Natural Resource Management Issues
1.     Pollution and Wastes
Air Pollution
2.3             The industry and energy sectors are the major sources of atmospheric pollutant
emissions in the Russia Federation, although road transport is becoming an increasingly important
source of low-level emissions in urban areas. Forty-three cities- in Russia have been identified under

million metric ton (mmt) of noxious air pollutants in 1992 (31.8 mint in 1991), including 8.2 mrnt of

reporting by enterprises and the biased methods used to make these estimates. Moreover; there is
considerable regional variation in the distribution of these emissions according to the type of pollutant
(see Figure 2.1). The main sources of industrial air pollution in Russia are the nonferrous metal
smelters, iron and steel mills, coal-burning power plants, cement plants, oil refineries, and
petrochemical and chemical plants.

2.4           Because of the inefficient combustion of fuels, the large system loss of natural gas,
and the extensive flaring of associated gas (8.2 bcm, in 1991), the FSU was the second largest
generator of greenhouse gases in the world in 1989, discharging some 4,480 mmt of CO2 equivalent,
compared with 5,610 mmt of CO2 equivalent in United States.
2.5           The extent of the acid rain problem in Russia has not been well documented, although
Russian enterprises alone discharge more S02 than Belgium, France, Holland, the United Kingdom,
and West Germany, combined. Moreover, these emissions are concentrated mainly in the Urals and
in east Siberia (as shown in Figure 2.1), and potentially threaten sensitive lake ecosystems in the
northern areas, as well as forests, wildlife habitat and agriculture crops.
Water Pollution
2.6          In 1992, the total volume of water withdrawn in the Russia Federation was
approximately 111 cubic kilometers (km3), with a consumption loss of 9 km3. Industry (including
power stations) used about 48.5 krn3; irrigation, 15.4 kin3; urban households, 14.7 km3; rural
population and livestock needs, 13 kin3; and other users, about 7.4 km3.
2.7           About 70% of the urban population in the Russian Federation is supplied with water
from surface sources. Groundwater is primarily used in the rural regions (mostly shallow wells).
Although the amount of water withdrawn in the whole country does not exceed 3-4% of the mean
annual runoff, in a number of watersheds (the Don, Kuban, Terek, Ural, Iset, Miass, and others),
about 50% of available water is withdrawn, causing rapid deterioration of local aquatic ecosystems.
2.8           Effluent discharges into surface water bodies in the Russian Federation totalled about
70.6 km3 in 1992, including 27.1 km3 of polluted wastewater, 42 km3 of untreated cooling water, and
about 3 km3 of standard treated water. The bulk of polluted wastewater was discharged by ferrous and
nonferrous metals, chemical, petrochemical, petroleum, coal, and pulp and paper industries, as well
as by municipalities insufficiently treating their wastewater (containing as a rule large volumes of
untreated industrial wastewater). The non-point discharges of agricultural origin (contaminated
primarily by toxic chemicals and pesticides), were in the order of 8 km3.
2.9          In 1992, the most widespread toxic pollutants discharged to surface water, were
petroleum products (about 39.4 thousand tons discharged in 1992 to surface water), phenols (0.2
thousand tons), copper compounds (0.9 thousand tons), zinc (1.6 thousand tons); and in some regions,
ammonia nitrogen (187.0 thousand tons), phosphorous (59.8 thousand tons), and iron (57.2 thousand
tons). Large amounts of biogenic substances are washed down to surface water bodies from farm
fields. The current level of domestic wastewater treatment is such that even in the case of biological
treatment, the effluent contains sufficient amounts of nitrates, phosphates and phosphorus to cause
extensive eutrophication of water bodies.
2.10          The quality of surface water resources is monitored in the Russian Federation at about
2700 sites, covering 1159 rivers, 80 lakes and 67 storage reservoirs, through a national monitoring
network established about 50 years ago. Its main purpose was to detect the trends and long-term
changes in surface water quality, but there were too few observation sites and observations were too
infrequent for water quality management purposes.
2.11          The Maximum Allowable Concentrations (MACs) were violated many times in 1991
for most of the polluting substances that were analyzed. For petroleum products they have been
violated in 40-45 % of the samples taken. Similar numbers for MAC violations for other pollutants



8                                                         Environmental Management Project
are: 30-35% for organic substances; 45-60% for phenols; 25-40% for ammonium nitrogen; 70-75%
for copper compounds; and 30-35% for zinc compounds.
2.12         Bacteriological contamination of rivers and lakes below large urban centers is
common. On the basis of public health indices, surface water bodies are divided into two categories:
Category I is water fit for drinking, and Category II is recreational water. In 1991, for Russia as a
whole, about 25% of the samples from both Category I and Category II water sources did not meet
public health standards on the bacteriological indices. Sources of infectious disease were found in
1.2% and 5% of samples from Category I and II waters respectively.
2.13         The situation is particularly alarming in a number of the country's oblasts and cities.
For example, about 60% of samples from Category I water sources do not meet bacteriological
standards in Kostroma Oblast. In Bryansk Oblast, this figure is 91%; in Rostov Oblast, 51%; in
Kemerovo Oblast, 52 %; in Khabarovsk Krai, 52 %; and in Omsk, 51 %. Sources of infectious diseases
were found in approximately 40% of samples from Vladimir Oblast and 13% of samples from
Novosibirsk Oblast. Category II water bodies do not meet bacteriological standards in 60-80 % of the
samples taken.
2.14         Regarding groundwater, in the vicinity of major industrial enterprises and agricultural
regions, approximately 500 sites with polluted groundwater have been identified, including 70
operating water intakes. The most severe groundwater pollution problems occur in the Moscow Oblast,
in the area of lignite coal mines in Tula Oblast, at oilfields in Tatarstan, Perm Oblast and
Bashkortostan, and in the vicinity of major industrial cities such Volgograd, Magnitogorsk and
Kemerevo. Groundwater is contaminated mostly by sulfates, chlorides, nitrogen compounds, petroleum
products, phenols and heavy metals. In the majority of polluted groundwater aquifers, MACs are
exceeded 10 times for at least 1 of the polluting substances.
Hazardous Waste
2.15         The first nationwide inventory of industrial hazardous waste in the Russian Federation
was published in 1990, based on data collected in 1989 by the State Committee for Statistics. At that
time, there were 80 ministries and committees at national and regional levels that had information on
hazardous waste, but the information was not generally shared or made available to the public,
especially information from the ministries concerned with the military-industrial complex.
2.16         The 1990 inventory was carried out using a questionnaire form that was filled out and
collected from almost all enterprises in the country. The reporting form included information on the
volume of waste generated (self reported) by the enterprise, the amount used, the amount stored, the
amount transferred to other units, and the amount buried, based on four standard classes of toxicity
used to characterize hazardous waste in the FSU. The concerned authorities recognize that the results
are not sufficiently reliable because of under-reporting. Even so, the results suggest an alarming
situation. Summary data on production of toxic wastes in the Russian Federation, from the 1990 waste
inventory, are presented in Table 2.1.
2.17         Since 1990, work has continued within MEPNR and associated institutes on refinement
and implementation of the basic national system for hazardous waste management including
improvement of the waste classification system. However, it is apparent that progress on this is not
proceeding expeditiously due to lack of resources. More specifically, it has not been developed to the
point of implementation at the level of detail required to support an effective regulatory management



The Environment Sector                                                                   9
system. This is viewed as a prerequisite for compliance with the Basel Convention on Transboundary
Movement of Hazardous Waste which is considered a bench mark in assessing progress toward
meeting international standards in the control of hazardous waste. The Russian Federation has signed
but not yet ratified this convention, largely because these basic standards are still to be put in place.
2.18          Continuing attempts are being made to maintain and update the original 1990
inventory.  Under MEPNR direction, regional and municipal environmental committees have
established regular reporting requirements for major enterprises in the last several years and the
availability of this information to and within the federal level on a consistent basis is sporadic and
incomplete. Within MEPNR, resources available to handle, interpret and transfer the data received
remain limited. Plans to consolidate locally supplied data into a national inventory on a regular basis
have not been implemented, although a state program on "Waste" has been prepared, this program
has progressed slowly. Similarly, several industry specific organizations, either Ministries or
Committees for particular industrial sectors or associated Institutes, have data on wastes in their
particular industrial sectors. However, a general lack of coordination and consolidation of this
information has been noted and conflicts respecting valuations and access of these data are apparent.
2.19          It has been observed that current industrial waste management practices depend
predominantly on direct land disposal, utilizing large scale lagoons and landfills, or direct discharge
of liquid effluent to surface water. Only limited discrimination among these industrial wastes is
utilized in employing these disposal practices.  These practices are resulting in significant
environmental impact and in many cases have exhausted available land. A large scale development
program will be required to provide more effective and environmentally sound facilities, something
that should involve a nationally based plan, utilizing accurate data. In the absence of a consolidated
picture of industrial waste generation, tabulated on a consistent basis at the national level, it is difficult
for coordinated planning of actual waste management infrastructure to occur. As a result, initiatives
currently being proposed to MEPNR from the local and regional levels are largely being made on an
ad hoc basis.
2.20          The amount of toxic waste generated annually by the organic chemical industry is
890,000 tons, of which less than 10 % is reportedly destroyed and 35 % is recycled or re-used (mainly
petroleum waste).
2.21          The Russian Federation has one of the world's largest metallurgical industries.
Consequently, metal-containing waste is a major portion of the total 75 million tons of solid industrial
waste produced every year. Approximately 27 million tons is metal-containing waste including Cu,
Cd, Co, As, Ni, Pb, Mg, and Cr. Most are Ni-containing wastes, accounting for 25 million tons.
However, only a part of this waste is considered to be hazardous. Approximately 450,000 tons per
year of dust-containing fluorine and cathode residues are produced by the aluminum and fertilizer
industries.

2.22            The engineering and machine building industry in Russia is also a major sector.
Twenty major machine building complexes exist in Russia, and there are, in addition, about 3,500
individual enterprises. The electroplating industry, which is associated with the engineering and
machine building sector, is a major source of hazardous wastes in Russia, generating some 750,000
tons per year. Electroplating baths are often discharged directly to surface waters or to sewage
treatment plants without any treatment. This action results in a reduction in the treatment efficiency
of the biological treatment process of the sewage treatment plants. Metals, for the most part, end up
in the biosludge and consequently, the sludge cannot be used in agricultural applications. The sludge,
depending on where it is stored, can pose a long-term problem as a result of heavy metal leaching,
resulting in groundwater contamination. There are approximately 5,000 electroplating shops in Russia,
of which 1,000 are located in Moscow. Half of the electroplating plants have treatment facilities for
waste baths and rinsing water but only 25% of the total plants are using their treatment facilities.
2.23            Russia has committed to phase out PCBs, although plans to achieve this have not yet
been finalized. Residues of banned pesticides are also a growing problem, with approximately 30,000
tons of pesticides currently being stored awaiting destruction. Radiological contamination and nuclear
waste disposal are also problems to be addressed on a large scale.



The Environment Sector                                                               11
2.    Natural Resource Management Issues
Forests
2.24         The forest products industries of Russia do not make a contribution to the national
economy commensurate with the size of the forest resource, nor do they, as a whole, contribute a
large share to the export base, except in select cases, such as log exports to Japan. Forest industries
make up about 5% of gross industrial output, and 7% of the value of exports from Russia. The
amount of industrial product derived from each tree is significantly lower in Russia than is the case
in Scandinavia and North America. Much of the wood is wasted in the production process, and a large
share of national harvest still goes for fuelwood (20-22% of the yearly cut). Improvements in the
industrial use of wood could be an important first step toward better conservation of Russian forests.
2.25         Shortcomings in forest products industries tend to have a negative impact on the
environment of Russia, in four ways:
*     Poor use of deciduous trees in industrial processes. In the pulp and paper industry,
for example, deciduous species make up only 9% of the raw material base, versus
30% in the United States and 58% in Japan. Improved management and consumption
of deciduous species under a sustained yield forestry system in the European regions
of Russia could reduce the need for shifting timber harvesting operations to Siberian
stands or over-cutting accessible coniferous forests.
*     Poor use of wood by-products. More than 50 million cubic meters of leftover wood
materials are produced yearly in Russia during industrial processes, but only half of
it is used to make composition boards or contribute to the wood chemicals sector.
Twenty percent of all wood harvested in Russia is used directly to make packaging
materials, such as paperboard, whereas much of the material needed could come from
the woodchip by-products of sawmilling and other processes.
*     Forest industries as direct sources of enviromnental damage. Deforestation has
contributed to soil erosion and water resources have suffered due to effluent
discharges from pulp and paper mills, with only 19% of the pulp and paper industry's
wastewater receiving effective treatment.
*     Low level of capital investment in forest industries. One approach to improving
both the environmental record and output performance of the forest sector is in
creating new and cleaner technologies. Such processes would make better use of trees,
allow selective cutting, enhance and expand replanting efforts, raise productivity in
industry, and reduce levels of environmental pollution. Unfortunately, such
technological shifts come at a high price, and investments levels in forest products as
a whole have not grown since the 1950s. In 1970, for example, forest industries were
allocated a 5.1% share of national basic investment in industry; in 1980 this share was
4.5%, and in 1986, 4.2%. In fact, forest products activities, especially logging and
sawmilling, are probably some of the most severely under-capitalized of any industry
in the country.
2.26         The Russian forest products industry thus faces the formidable three-fold task of
improving production efficiency to satisfy consumer demand, offering more wood products on world
markets to provide export income, and improving wood utilization practices that have already had



12                                                                    Environmental Management Project
severe consequences for the environment. All this is to be accomplished in what has been treated as
a backwater part of a troubled economy.
Land
2.27            Land resources. Table 2.2 provides a breakdown of land categories in the FSU by
management or use and by natural zones. However, due to the generally northern location of the
country, much of that land resource is rather limited in terms of its possible uses and potential
habitability. Aridity and mountainous terrain are two other widespread constraints. Some of the largest
natural zones found in the FSU, such as the Arctic tundra and the Central Asian deserts, not only have
limited economic possibilities but also possess fragile and easily destroyed ecosystems. Even the
largest natural zone, the vast forest belt or taiga, has important limitations on the extent and nature
of its use, and has suffered from organizational and environmental mismanagement.
