2.28            On the other hand, some of the most threatened ecosystems in the Soviet Union are
relatively small, and have already undergone a high degree of adverse transformation. Such
ecosystems include the narrow but very important vegetated corridors along rivers (particularly those
in arid areas), as well as the rivers themselves. They also include coastlines, both tidal and freshwater
wetlands, and isolated areas of rare or unique vegetation.



The Environment Sector                                                                  1 3
2.29          The long-standing problem has been that the implementing ministries and overseeing
governmental bodies have a built-in conflict of interest in being responsible for both economic targets,
environmental protection and resource use. The agricultural sector, for example, has pursued policies
that have sacrificed soil fertility to crop productivity. Over Russia as a whole, 13 million hectares of
land suffer from soil erosion; 12 million hectares have been lost to construction and roads, and 3
million hectares to irrigation systems; 10 million hectares have been flooded for reservoirs; and 2
million hectares have been damaged by open-pit mining. As has been previously noted, the economic
mandate has in general prevailed over conservation goals. As a result, many land use management
problems, such as erosion and reclamation, have had a rather slow pace of improvement.
2.30          Land protection. Unfortunately, many areas in Russia are highly vulnerable to soil
erosion. Much of its arable land is either on sloping terrain that is susceptible to water erosion or is
in semi-arid regions that are subject to recurring drought. These concerns are exacerbated by the
extensive occurrence of fine-grained, wind-deposited soils (called loess) over much of the fertile steppe
and forested steppe regions of the country. Past farming practices, going back into the nineteenth
century, were often inadequate to protect these highly erodible topsoils, and losses in the pre-Soviet
period were great. In the twentieth century, sub-optimal agricultural practices (for instance, plowing
marginal lands, soil salinization in arid areas, improper use of fertilizers, downslope plowing patterns,
and use of excessively heavy machinery) have also often been conducive to topsoil degradation and
accelerated erosion.
Inland fisheries
2.31          The causes of the decline in the fish harvest from lakes, rivers, and estuaries includes
such recurring themes as over-fishing, urban and industrial effluents (in numerous rivers), dams and
reservoirs (on the Volga and Ob), increased salinity (in the Sea of Azov), and diversion of water for
irrigation (the Don and other rivers). These factors, singly or in combination, have greatly diminished
fish stocks in a number of water bodies that historically were very productive.
2.32          The impact of direct discharge and leakage of industrial effluents and hazardous wastes
from land disposal sites near major rivers is becoming measurable in the freshwater fish population.
Bioaccumulation of metals and chemicals, as well as biological changes have been noted in the Upper
Volga region for example. At the same time fish remain a major food source in this region.
2.33          The FSU responded to these problems in a variety of ways, including the introduction
of new species, constructing new fish breeding facilities and spawning areas, creating a vast network
of small stock ponds, and occasionally instituting fishing moratoria where appropriate. In response to
the perceived seriousness of the problem, special resolutions on fisheries conservation and management
were passed in 1974 and 1981. Such actions have partly alleviated the problem, but the take of many
desirable species (most notably sturgeon) continue to be well below former levels, and the Ministry
of Fisheries acknowledged in 1984 that serious problems remain.
Biodiversity and Nature Protection
2.34          Overview. The 8 biogeographic zones of the Russian Federation encompass 54
ecological zones that contain unique and endemic species. In the FSU, there were 35,000 species of
higher plants, with 553 in the Russian "Red Book" of endangered species, of which 49% are endemic
and 23 % are at the limit of their ranges. There are approximately 130,000 species of fauna, with 463
species appearing in the Red Data Book, of which 70 are in the most critical category of "endangered"
(IUCN Category I).



14                                                            Environmental Management Project
2.35          Although much of Russia's biodiversity is outside of protected areas, the protected area
system is one of the most important, and until recently one of the best organized systems in the world.
It consists of (1) 79 Nature Reserves or Zapovedniki (strictly protected and used for research with 16
classified as UNESCO Biosphere reserves; 1.2% of Russian land area); (2) 22 National Parks or
Natsional'nyi Park (protected, although with tourism in specified zones); (3) 71 Special Purpose
Reserves or Zakazniks (established to safeguard certain flora and fauna, protected status is occasionally
seasonal; 3 % of land area); and (4) National Monuments. The 76 Zapovedniki comprise 45% of the
total of all global strict scientific reserves. The Russian scientists have an extremely distinguished
history of research in these reserves, 16 of which are part of the Biosphere program; and an enormous
amount of scientific data has been collected on natural areas. Advantage has also been taken of the
opportunity presented by changes in land tenure and administration during the transitional phase, and
additional protected sites have been established by the government.
2.36          Due to economic and social pressures, the nature protection system is under threat.
This threat is further compounded by the fragmented institutional system that is uniformly and
simultaneously beset by a lack of coordination, efficiency and funding. For instance, protected areas
are administered by a plethora of organizations: the majority (71) are managed directly by the small
Department of Nature Reserves (within the MEPNR), but seven are managed by other institutions.
National Parks are managed by one of the departments of the Forest Service Agency. Most of the
Zakazniks are controlled by the local governments, although 71 are under federal administration with
the local Committee of Environmental Protection responsible for the increasingly sporadic inspections.
2.37          Protected areas are now severely under-financed, with national inflation at 20-30%
per month. Current appropriations do not cover salaries for the 20,000 personnel, and essential
activities are being cancelled. Also, the increasing need for local communities to exploit protected area
resources has meant that 19 of the 76 Zapoovedniki are already in a critical state, with another 19
rapidly deteriorating. Furthermore, the difficulties of the transition period are encouraging
inappropriate resource use that should be corrected promptly.
2.38          Global importance of biodiversity in the Russian Federation. Approximately 10% of
the world's biodiversity is represented by the vast range of species (many of which are endemic) found
in Russia. Loss, depletion or even degradation to this natural resource treasure would have profound
biological and philosophical impact on all of life-as well as decreasing the tangible benefits accruing
from sustainable use of the resources. Additionally, the expanse of forests and tundra acts as a
significant carbon sink. Destruction of these areas, combined with the swift eradication of tropical
forests (the other arboreal sink) would have a profound effect on world climate change.
2.39          The Canadian Parks Service, at the request of the Bank, has recently identified the
protected areas system in Russia as being in immediate danger of disintegration. Given its global
importance to biodiversity conservation, an attempt must be made to save it. An area of particular
importance, and one under serious threat, is Lake Baikal. This area requires immediate assistance in
biodiversity protection; and because it is a microcosm of the country as a whole, can be used also as
a pilot project (see project description in Annex 2). Specifically, this area shows the need for sound
management of both in-situ and ex-situ efforts, and provides considerable opportunity for consensus
building in the consideration of available conservation options.



The Environment Sector                                                               15
3.    Institutional and Legislative Framework for the Environment
Environmental Institutions
2.40         Prior to 1988, the FSU had no central administrative and management bodies involved
in environmental protection; and a number of ministries and departments performed different
management and monitoring functions in natural resources conservation and environmental protection,
including the USSR State Agro-industrial Committee, State Forestry Committee, USSR Ministry of
Water Management and Reclamation, USSR Ministry of Fishing Industry, USSR State Committee of
Hydrometeorology and Environmental Monitoring, USSR Ministry of Public Health, USSR Ministry
of Geology, USSR State Mining Safety Inspectorate, and USSR Ministry of Foreign Affairs. These
ministries and departments often operated as powerful monopolies, ignoring environmental concerns
and the interests of local populations, as the centrally set production targets served as the primary
incentives.
2.41          Organizational Structure of Environmental Quality Management in the Russian
Federation. On January 8, 1988, the Communist Party Central Committee and Soviet Government
adopted a resolution by which a central state management entity-Goskompriroda-was established
in order to modernize the conservation and regulation of natural resource use. In 1990,
Goskompriroda was transformed into the Ministry for Nature Use and Environmental Protection of
the USSR (USSR "Minpriroda"). Subordinate ministries or departments were established in the
Russian Federation and its autonomous republics and at the krais (provinces, districts), oblasts
(regions), and local levels. These commonly take the form of regional or municipal environmental
or ecological committees which in practice should undertake implementation of regulatory actions.
2.42         Despite USSR Minpriroda's status as a Ministry, implementation of its policies was
slow and inconsistent, and the Ministry and its territorial bodies met strong resistance from other
ministries previously responsible for environmental issues within their respective sectors. Many of the
ministries managed to hold on to their traditional powers with respect to environmental concerns, often
meaning that they were given little attention in relation to main line-ministry production functions.
2.43         One aspect of environmental policy-making particularly impeded by bureaucratic
division was information gathering and analysis. Research on the environment in the former Soviet
Union was conducted by over 1,000 institutions under the auspices of 70 different ministries and
agencies. The authors of the USSR 1989 National Report on Environment, prepared under
Goskompriroda coordination, described serious bureaucratic challenges they encountered in gathering
available information, not to mention assessing the information's reliability, because each agency had
its own methodology for collecting and analyzing data. This in itself represents a major barrier to
effective and coordinated environmental planning and policy making.
2.44         Ministry of Environmental Protection and Natural Resources of the Russian
Federation. In Russia, the recently renamed Ministry of Environmental Protection and Natural
Resources (MEPNR) is, in many ways, similar to that of the previous the USSR Minpriroda. MEPNR
is the central governmental organ responsible for environmental protection and natural resource
utilization in Russia, in conjunction with the executive authorities of the legal subjects of the Russian
Federation. Its functions include regulation of land utilization and conservation, and the use of surface
and ground water, air, vegetation, wildlife, and natural resources, including those of the territorial
waters, the continental shelf, and the exclusive economic zone.



16                                                         Environmental Management Project
2.45          Within the Russian Federation, the governments of the subjects of the Russian
Federation have received greater powers in formulating policy, including environmental policy. At
the same time, the earlier system of ministries and state committees charged with environmental
responsibilities essentially remains.  Due to rapid, ongoing changes in the Russian system of
government, the institutional framework for environmental protection is still evolving and devolving
to lower levels in the government structure.
2.46          The structure of the MEPNR is illustrated in Annex 1. Territorial bodies of the
MEPNR work with executive authorities and industries to achieve standards, conduct inspections and
assess fines. The territorial bodies of MEPNR are empowered to carry out inspections for local
environmental protection and elaboration of environmental programs in their areas, accounting and
assessing of natural resources, volumes of production, waste accumulation, and pollution. They are
also responsible for State Environmental Impact Assessment Review of projects and for issuing of
permits, making decisions on restriction, suspension and cessation of environmentally harmful
activities, settlement of disputes, protection of natural monuments and protected areas, ecological
training and education, and information dissemination.
2.47          The present environmental administration is going through a period of substantial
change. The overlapping of duties and fields of responsibility of different organizations is a common
feature, as is the constant shortage of resources. Efforts are underway within the Government and
MEPNR to define and resolve possible areas of overlap, conflict, or gaps. An important recent
initiative in this regard has been the formation of a Federal Commission on the Environment under
the Chairmanship of the Deputy Prime Minister responsible for Environment, Natural Resources and
Agricultural issues which is responsible for settling overall environmental policy and resolving
interagency conflicts. Increasing cooperation and coordination of environmental management activities
by regional governments is being achieved through the formation of Inter-Oblast Environmental
Councils.
Environmental Law and Policy
2.48          In formal terms, the conservation and rational use of natural resources were
constitutionally guaranteed in the FSU. Principal laws and codes, such as "On Air Protection" (1960),
"Land Code" (1970), "Code on Mineral Resources" (1976), "Water Code" (1972), and "Fundamentals
of the Russian Federation On Forestry" (1977) were adopted. In addition, there have been Communist
party and government resolutions that have covered general environmental protection and wildlife
utilization.
2.49          Protection of the environment in Russia was carried out by legislative, administrative
and economic means. The constitution of the country imposed an obligation on the state to ensure the
protection and rational use of the environment. From the legal point of view environmental protection
was declared one of the basic functions of the state.
2.50          During the Soviet era, the Russian system consisted of a number of separate laws that
addressed the following areas: (1) conservation and protection of "landscapes"; (2) protection of
natural and environmental resources; and (3) protection of the human environment against pollution
(this included protection of drinking water, urban air, and agricultural soils).
2.51          While environmental protection legislation and institutions were being developed
during the Soviet era, both at the central level and in the republics, environmental objectives in
general remained secondary to near-term industrial production objectives. The limited financial and



The Environment Sector                                                                17
other resources earmarked for environmental protection, the lack of enforcement of environmental
norms and regulations, and the use of censorship and other means to control public information on
the environment contributed to weak implementation of these policies. Generally, the central and the
Russian governments largely ignored the growing environmental problems and public environmental
concerns. However, there were some notable exceptions to the practice during the Soviet era,
particularly with protection of wildlife and forests, and efforts to ensure safe drinking water.
2.52          A shift in Soviet policy occurred in 1986 and 1988, when efforts were increased to
rationalize the use of natural resources and protect the environment. Incentives for decreasing material
and energy inputs per unit of output were introduced. While economic imperatives were the primary
driving force behind the new policy, the growing costs to the environment were also increasingly
being appreciated.
2.53          The USSR "Law on the State Enterprises" (passed in 1987) had special value in this
regard, by containing legal provisions for taking account of environmental impacts. This law contained
the first preventive measures aimed at curbing pollution and limiting excessive and wasteful extraction
and production methods in the former Soviet Union. Its Articles 17 and 20 specifically referred to
environmental aspects, such as payments for the use of natural resources, environmental protection
measures, and compensation for environmental damage (to be paid from the enterprises' own budgets).
Introduction of cleaner technologies became mandatory for certain types of production.
2.54          This law was founded on the premise that state enterprises should and could become
self-financing and profit-making entities, with a stake in their own profitability. However, it proved
difficult to implement the new policy, to some extent because the efforts to realize enterprise self-
financing faltered. The realization of plans envisaged by this and other laws during the Gorbachev
presidency, turned out to be unrealistic, and suggested that a deeper restructuring of the whole
economic, political, and social order should be done before any individual innovations of particular
production sectors.
2.55          The Russian Federation Law on Environmental Protection. Based on the Soviet-
inspired antecedent passed in 1961, this 1991 law sets out noble principles-for example, every citizen
has the right to a healthy and safe environment. To this end, the law establishes the areas of
responsibility of the various levels of government within the Russian Federation. The federal
government, for example, is tasked with establishing environmental standards and procedures for
setting fees for natural resources use. Governments at the republic level set fees and issue licenses for
natural resource use. Regional (krai and oblast) governments issue permits for pollution emissions.
Regional and local agencies are responsible for monitoring emissions; and republic- and regional-level
agents enforce compliance with federal standards. On this point, the law also stipulates a positive role
for individuals and nongovernmental organizations (NGOs) to assist with monitoring and enforcement
and guarantees their right to association, their right to information, and their right to seek legal redress
for environmental degradation.
2.56          The Russian law  mandates State Environmental Impact Assessment Review
("Gosudarstvennaya Ecologicheskaya Ekspertiza") for all development activities. The law sets out the
obligations of enterprises with regard to compliance with standards concerning waste management,
handling of hazardous materials, air and water emissions, noise pollution, land use and reclamation.
Of note, the law extends all regulations to include all military operations on Russian territory, ending
the Soviet tradition whereby the military could act independently of the law. In what signals a dramatic
departure from the past, the law mandates criminal liability for "environmental damage", as well as
"obligation for full compensation" for damages to the environment and also to public health. Finally,



18                                                          Environmental Management Project
the law also contains several novelties, such as mandating "environmental insurance" against natural
or manmade disasters and establishing the concept of a "zone of environmental disaster" where all but
essential economic activities must be halted in order to restore the environment.
2.57          Environmental Funds. The system of local, regional and federal Environmental
Funds, formed in June 1992, is critical to the funding of nature protection activities of the MEPNR
and the territorial and local environmental agencies, as well as for polluting enterprises. These Funds
receive the revenue from pollution fees applied to air emissions, water discharges, waste disposal, and,
in some cases, to the extraction of natural resources. Ten percent of this revenue goes directly into
the federal budget. Of the remaining 90 percent, amounts are allocated as follows: 10 percent to the
Federal Environment Fund, 30 percent to the relevant Oblast/Republic Environment Fund, and 60
percent to the relevant Municipal/Local Environment Fund.
2.58          Estimates of Federal Environmental Fund revenues provided by MEPNR were
approximately 10-25 billion rubles for 1992. The bulk of their funds (86 percent) were provided to
support research and development on resource-saving and nature-saving types of technologies and
equipment. Of the balance: 3.6 percent was provided to nature reserves and national parks; 3.6
percent was allotted to environmental impact statements and environmental expert review; 2.7 percent
to education and information dissemination; 0.8 percent to international cooperation; and the balance
to other undefined activities.
2.59          At the federal level, decision about allocating the central Ministry's share of the
Federal Fund are made by a National Board. Similar boards exist at territorial and local levels. The
law requires that the funds be spent on environmental protection activities. Beyond this, there appears
to be wide discretion in allocating the funds. Examples of uses of the Territorial Funds include such
activities as: scientific support for natural parks and environmental monitoring; storage facilities for
pesticides and agricultural chemicals; systems requirements for wastewater treatment; instruments for
environmental quality monitoring; recycling of hazardous wastes; and restoration of sites of historic
value. Allocations within geographical areas are based on the amounts collected within those areas.
This has the effect of raising the most money in those places where the environmental damages are
the greatest. However, problems arise, because enterprises withhold payment of these fees for various
reasons. As a result, the amount collected is well below the potential level. Moreover, local and
regional environment funds are often used to find non-environment related activities, further reducing
the potential for environmental improvement.
Summary of Key Crosscutting Issues
2.60          The Russian Federation has inherited an economic structure and obsolete technologies
that encourage pollution and inefficient resource use, as well as a large stock of accumulated
environmental problems.3' The capital requirements for addressing those problems and launching
mitigation measures are high by international standards. Therefore, it is imperative to carefully set
priorities and develop policies and strategies that will allow these priorities to be addressed over time
in a least-cost way.
2.61          However, the current environmental quality management system at all levels of
government is in a state restructuring and adaptation to the new economic and political realities in
Russia. Key issues that need to be addressed during the transition to a more effective system of
environmental management for pollution abatement and nature protection that can meet the special
3/ A number of regions have requested that the Federal Government designate them as Environrmental Emergency or Disaster Zones.



The Environment Sector                                                                19
demands of a new economic, political and social system include: (1) unreliability of the data; (2)
ineffectiveness of the legal and regulatory system; (3) poorly defined management and organizational
responsibilities; (4) inadequate budgetary allocations; (5) breakdown of the "command and control"
system and deficiencies in the oeconomic incentive structure for pollution abatement and nature
protection; (6) deficiencies in the methodologies and techniques used to derive emission limits for air
and water quality management and management of solid and hazardous wastes, including the ambient
environmental quality standards that have been adopted; and (7) insufficient financial incentives and
resources for companies to invest in environmental mitigation efforts.
2.62          Unreliability of environmental data. The old system of central planning and control
has left a legacy of inefficiency and mismanagement resulting from: unreliable basic information;
narrow segregation of responsibility; poor information dissemination and analysis; incomplete
accountability for performance or results; and, in certain cases, deliberate misreporting of
environmental data. In some cases Bank missions have found that the data provided was in
considerable error (i.e, by factors of 2 to 5 times). In many situations it is simply impossible to judge
by how much the data are in error. Due to insufficient budgets and lack of staff, the air and water
inspectorate in the MEPNR and its committees at oblast and local levels have to rely on the industrial
enterprises and municipal sewerage organizations to provide data on pollution emissions from their
facilities. In short, the current situation is such that no data generated by the system can be taken at
face value. Since the generation of reliable environmental information and its timely dissemination and
effective analysis are fundamental to efficient environmental management, this is clearly a priority area
for upgrading and improvement in any strategy for assistance to this sector.
2.63          Ineffective environmental protection laws and regulations. Another important issue
that adversely affects the ability of the MEPNR and its territorial branches to perform their regulatory
functions is the state of development of the legal system in the FSU. Environmental law was slow
to develop and environmental lawyers and law schools teaching environmental law are, therefore, rare
in Russia.
2.64          Poorly defined management and organizational responsibilities. Responsibilities for
environmental quality management are dispersed among many governmental agencies. They are
poorly coordinated leading to conflicts between agencies and inefficient use of the limited human and
financial resources available. The MEPNR is one of the more recently formed governmental
organizations, and has had great difficulty establishing its own leadership role in environmental
management. Further complicating the situation, a new Constitution has provided greater autonomy
to the regional and local governments.
2.65          Inadequate budget allocations. Even before the break-up of the FSU, the financial
resources available for environmental quality management were extremely limited. Some relief was
provided with the introduction of the pollution fee system and allocation of a proportion of these funds
to support environmental regulatory activities. However, the downturn in the economy, the pressing
social demands on limited government funds, and the effects of inflation have greatly eroded these
small gains. As a result the environmental management system is under severe financial stress. There
is a danger that well-trained and experienced personnel in pollution abatement and nature protection
will seek other occupations in order to survive the current economic crisis.
2.66          Breakdown of command-and-control system. With the breakdown of the centrally
planned economy, and the supporting administrative control system, there is an urgent need to rebuild
a new and more appropriate form of command-and-control system for pollution abatement and nature
protection compatible with the new economic structure.



20                                                          Environmental Management Project
2.67          Inappropriate criteria for emission standards. Some of the current ambient standards
in Russia for air, water and soil quality, are very stringent by international standards. Most industries
in the Russian Federation do not currently have the capacity to be able to comply and thereby call into
question the credibility of environmental policy. According to Bank missions, the methodologies and
techniques used to set emission limits also are out of date, and lead to inefficient and ineffective
pollution abatement and control mechanisms. The problems are further compounded by the lack of
EIA requirements in respect of investment projects.
2.68          Inadequate medium term investmentfunds. Enterprises are facing great pressure to
restructure their operations at a time when their financial resources are being cut through lower
government subsidies and soft credits. At the same time, the commercial banks are reluctant to
provide medium-term project investment financing due to a shortage of such funds and political risk
factors. There is, therefore, an urgent need to provide a source of medium-term loan funds to
enterprises so that they can also implement pollution mitigation measures.
C.    Government's Objectives and Strategy
2.69          Central and regional government officials are aware of the issues raised above, but
a clear and viable policy and strategy for dealing with continual decline in environmental management
for pollution abatement and nature protection are being realized very slowly. For instance, a list of
some forty laws and regulations currently being drafted by the MEPNR are not necessarily of high
priority and yet tie up the services of the very few environmental protection law specialists employed
by the MEPNR. The MEPNR and other government agencies with overlapping environmental
responsibilities have been the subject of several reorganizations that have not fully resolved the issue.
2.70          The one relatively clear government strategy that offers scope for interventions with
considerable likelihood of immediate success is that of devolution of administrative and legislative
powers to regional and local governments. Since local residents are becoming increasingly vocal in
their opposition to facilities that contaminate the environment, there is increasing pressures on these
local and regional governments to find solutions to environmental problems. Unfortunately the same
is not true of nature protection. Local pressures for economic development tend to minimize the social
value of protecting nature, focusing instead on the potential economic value of the natural resources
found in them. However, there are beginning to be more opportunities in Russia for satisfying regional
goals in both nature protection and economic growth, through sustainable and environmentally
acceptable development options (such as in forestry and agriculture).
2.71          Another important recent government policy initiative has been the introduction of
pollution fee system for industrial, agricultural and domestic discharges into air and water and onto
land. These fees are paid into environmental funds at the local, regional and federal level for
investments in pollution control, and to a lesser extent for research and for environmental management
purposes. While there are a number of improvements which need to be made in relation to the level
of fees assessed and in the efficiency of collection and utilization, there is already a sound basis for
an environmental economic incentive system in the Russian Federation.
2.72          The severe budget constraints being faced by government agencies and enterprises
alike are severely restricting their ability to address even the most urgent environmental quality and
natural resource depletion problems. Much scope, therefore, exists for short to medium term strategic
support from multilateral and bilateral financial sources to assist the Government in dealing with these
concerns.



The Environment Sector                                                             21
D.    Assistance Strategy for the Sector
2.73         Based on Bankwide experience, the best approach is to lead in to the sector through
a project to strengthen environmental management institutional capacity, and to assist Governments
in developing environmental policies, incorporating a balanced use of economic instruments, and
command and control approaches to promote the most efficient and effective management of
environmental quality and natural resources. Moreover, in order to assist Governments to demonstrate
some immediate environmental benefits to its citizens from such a program, an associated program
of investments in pollution control is needed. This should initially focus on the many possible
economic/environment "win-win" investments that improve economic performance and reduce
pollution at the same time. It is widely recognized among experienced practitioners in the field that
even for the "win-win" investments, certain economic/financial incentives may be necessary to
encourage enterprise managements to undertake such investments. This is particularly the case for
countries in transition to a market economy, where price distortions and other market imperfections
give signals to investors that are often contrary to the achievement of environmental goals.
E.    World Bank Role and Strategy
2.74         The most recent discussion of the Country Assistance Strategy (CAS) for Russia was
on April 28, 1994 in the context of the Financial Institutions Development Project. At that time, the
Bank proposed to operate under an Intermediate Case scenario that would include a core investment
program of projects that would be relatively unaffected by changes in the macroeconomic
environment. The proposed project forms part of the core program. It meets all three priority
objectives for the Bank's assistance program contained in the CAS by: (i) supporting the development
of a market-oriented economy by demonstrating through the National Pollution Abatement Facility
(NPAF) how to involve the private sector in projects with tangible environmental benefits; (ii)
redirecting public sector involvement in the economy by strengthening the policy-making and
regulatory activities of key institutions involved in environmental management and establishing a sound
technical and policy framework for future environmental activities; and (iii) establishing a key role for
the Bank in project design and helping to mobilize donor funds that might not otherwise be available
to support priority activities in environmental management. The GOR and Bank recognize the vital
importance of bilateral and multilateral grant funds in support of the environmental sector (including
biodiversity conservation and sustainable management of natural resources) at this critical stage in
Russia's economic transformation. A donors meeting hosted by the Bank on April 13, 1994, resulted
in pledges in support of the EFP amount to $90 million from Canada, the European Union, Finland,
Norway, Switzerland, the United States and the GEF.
1.    Framework of an Environmental Strategy
2.75         In the next two years, the Bank will assist the Government in improving its policy,
regulatory and institutional framework for environmental management at the federal level and in
selected regions to preserve and strengthen the existing environmental management infrastructure
during the period when the Government is trying to stabilize and restructure the nation's economy.
Because of its external debt problems, the Russian Federation will be only able to borrow Bank funds
for projects in the environmental field that are essentially self financing, and which meet the basic
economic/environment "win-win" criteria outlined above. In order to overcome the Government's
debt constraints, the proposed EMP links investments in such projects through a special pollution
abatement fund with assistance to improve and sustain the environmental management infrastructure.



22                                                        Environmental Management Project
2.76         In the medium term, the Bank expects to broaden the regional and sectorial focus of
its environmental operations while still meeting the above Government priorities. This could include
expanding the operations of the NPAF drawing on the experience of its first phase operations, as well
as on the results of regional and industrial subsector environmental studies and action plans being
developed under the environmental management components of EMP.
2.77         In the longer term, as the Government's restructuring efforts lead to greater capacity
for public financing and management of infrastructure investments at the regional and municipal
levels, and as privatization succeeds to a point where enterprises can self finance more environmental
investments, the Bank would foresee an expanded program in the environmental area based on
geographically focussed infrastructure investments for: (1)improved water supply and sewage
treatment; (2) urban air pollution control; and (3) solid and hazardous waste management, etc.
2.     Role of Bank in Relation to Other Donors
2.78         Since the beginning of the Bank's involvement in the environmental sector in Russia,
it has worked closely with the Government and bilateral donors in formulating an Environmental
Framework Program (EFP) for the Federation. The Bank sees its role as a facilitator (not as a
coordinator) assisting bilateral and multilateral donors to identify and meet the highest priority needs
of the country in this sector. The EFP seeks to identify some of these needs, but is not intended to
be fully comprehensive. For instance, the program does not address issues such as radiological
protection, management of nuclear wastes, or nuclear safety issues; neither does it address in detail
the natural resource and environmental management issues in the forestry, fisheries, agriculture, or
mining sectors. Some of these issues are being addressed by bilateral and multilateral agencies under
other programs. More details of the EFP are given in Chapter 3. Chapter 4 describes the EFP
Components that will be financed under the Environmental Management Project.
2.79         An important source of grant funds is the Global Environment Facility (GEF) which
has already approved a $3.2 million grant to the Russian Government to identify the major sources
of greenhouse gas emissions from the gas sector, which accounts for some 60% of the energy use in
Russia. In addition, a $500,000 project preparation advance from the GEF has also been approved
to prepare some 20-30 investment projects for the phase out of ozone-depleting substances (ODS) in
the aerosol and refrigeration industry sub-sectors, as well as processes for the conversion of ODS to
non-ODS substitutes. Currently the Danish Government is providing technical assistance to MEPNR
in supporting the development of the Country's ODS phase-out strategy. Application is being made
to the GEF for a US$25 million grant to support the Biodiversity Conservation program that has been
developed under EFP. The Canadian Government's NIS Task Force has financed the work of Parks
Canada, which has led to the formulation of a priority capital expenditure program for immediate
assistance to the Nature Protection areas in Russia. The McArthur Foundation (US) has also financed
World Wide Fund for Nature (WWF) assistance in the preliminary work on a countrywide biodiversity
strategy.
2.80         In addition to these global activities, the UK Know-How Fund approved a $450,000
grant to prepare and set up the NPAF that will lend foreign exchange to enterprises for economically
and financially viable, export-oriented, pollution abatement projects.
3. Role of Bank in Relation to NGOs
2.81         Both national and international NGOs are playing an increasingly important role in
monitoring and raising awareness of the environmental quality and natural resource management issues



The Environment Sector                                                               23
which are of daily concern to Russian citizens. Especially, significant are the multitude of "grass
roots" NGOs that have sprung up throughout the Russian Federation, even dating back to the Soviet
period. Many of these organizations are loosely associated with two major national NGOs: the Socio-
Ecology Union (SEU) and the NGO Center under MEPNR. Because of their activism on issues of
critical concern to local communities and their frustrations in dealing with entrenched bureaucracies,
members of these organizations have become active in local politics and many have been elected to
local legislatures. These groups have now moved into positions of influence, not only at the local
level, but also at the regional and federal levels as well.
2.82         The Russian NGOs are also receiving considerable assistance from international
NGOs, which have linked up with them through an extensive E-mail network. In addition,
international NGOs are providing direct assistance through regional and local projects, particularly in
relation to sustainable agriculture and forestry practices, protection of watersheds and other small scale
community activities. Others have focussed on pollution issues, in particular nuclear waste disposal,
and marine pollution.
2.83          Under the direction of MEPNR, both the EFP and the EMP seek to foster the work
of the local NGOs through direct participation as members of subcomponent management committees,
as well as members of subcomponent implementation teams and as local experts assisting these teams.
Others will be involved as members of legislatures drafting and debating regulations which will put
into effect the results of the EMP. The MEPNR and the Bank are also in frequent contact with the
international NGOs that are active in Russia, and are working as partners with them, especially in
relation to biodiversity conservation and the conservation and management of natural resources (i.e.,
WWF) and International Union for the Conservation of Nature (IUCN), under funding from the GEF
and the McArthur Foundation.
F.    Relevant World Bank Experience in Other Environmental Projects and in Russia
1.    Environmental Management Projects
2.84         Three Bank assisted Environmental Management Projects in Indonesia, Chile and
Poland have provided valuable lessons for the preparation and implementation of this project. This
experience suggests that: limited absorptive capacity may exist in the federal Ministry of Environment;
responsibility for environmental and natural resource management may be dispersed across a number
of government ministries and agencies; confusion and frustration may result from uncoordinated donor
and multilateral agency support programs; full commitment to the program by the beneficiaries is
needed; strengthening of project implementation capacity prior to the loan avoids later delays; effective
supervision and strong support by Bank's Resident Mission is desirable; and a well-designed
monitoring and evaluation system to provide early warning of implementation delays is important.
2.85         These lessons have been incorporated into the design of the proposed project. Federal
Ministries in Russia are being down-sized and reorganized and budgets are being severely cut. For
this reason more than 80% of this project's funds will be directed to regional activities in three regions
west of the Urals. The problem of inter-ministerial and inter-agency coordination and cooperation
in Russia is more difficult at the federal than at the regional levels, where common problems and
public pressure have resulted in greater collaboration. Pilot programs have been identified in those
regions where there is strong support by the heads of executive and legislative branches of
government, as well as the local population to ensure effective coordination. At the federal level heads
of different executive and legislative agencies and committees have been consulted at every stage of
the development of the project to ensure their cooperation. With severe budget cuts for all government



24                                                         Environmental Management Project
agencies, injection of funds through this project is a strong incentive for collaboration. Uncoordinated
donor programs for the environment sector in Russia have not presented a problem to date because
of MEPNR and donor support for a World Bank-lead role among the donors, and the collaboration
of Canada, Finland, the United Kingdom, and the United States in assisting the GOR to formulate the
EFP.
2.86          The main criteria for selecting pilot project sites for this program have been the
commitment and capacity of the regional executive and legislative authorities to address environmental
and natural resource management issues. The selected beneficiaries have all expressed their full
commitment to participating in the project and will be signatories to Framework Agreements setting
out their responsibilities and obligations under the project. The Government's commitment to the
project has been expressed in its request for a Project Preparation Facility (PPF) advance of
US$460,000. The demand for the EFP far exceeds the supply of resources that can be committed.
Because of experience in other Bank projects in Russia and Eastern Europe, preparation for the setting
up of the Center for Project Preparation and Implementation (CPPI) began during the first Bank
mission. A PPF advance was approved (in December 1992) to provide funding for the CPPI prior to
loan effectiveness and most of the legal, management and organizational issues have been resolved
during project preparation. The Director of the CPPI has already been selected. A General Consultant
Firm will be hired by the time of loan effectiveness to assist in project start-up activities and the
training of subcomponent project managers. This firm will also play a key role throughout the project
to independently evaluate the project's performance and progress on a tri-yearly basis. Development
impact indicators are defined in detail in Chapter IV and will be the focus of Bank supervision
missions. A permanent environmental coordinator is employed at the Moscow Resident Mission and
he will play an important role during supervision (as he has during preparation). An additional
Russian environmental specialist has been hired in the Resident Mission to reinforce this effort.
2.     Credit Lines for Pollution Abatement
2.87          The success of these operations in other countries can be judged by the fact that
Governments have requested follow up projects. In the case of Brazil, there have been three such
operations and in India, the first pollution control credit line of US$160 million was fully committed
within two years and the Government requested that the second pollution control project in the lending
pipeline be brought forward to meet the high demand for such investments. Initially, in the case of
Brazil, commitments were slow because of excessive requirements for information and cumbersome
appraisal procedures. Once these were simplified and streamlined and the geographical coverage of
the project extended beyond the City of Sao Paulo, the loan was committed very rapidly, especially
for projects in the heavily polluted industrial area of Cubatao. The second pollution control project
was also for the State of Sao Paulo, while the third project was extended to cover the entire country.
For the first two loans, mass emission rate criteria were used to identify high priority investments for
pollution control. While this was found to be a useful technique for SO2 reduction, because of the
predominance of industrial sources of SO2, in the case of dust there was little correlation between
investment in pollution control and environmental improvement because of the predominance of urban
pollution sources (mainly traffic). This experience illustrates the importance of identifying the major
pollution sources in an area or region across economic sectors when deciding priority investments for
pollution control.
2.88          The first credit line for pollution control in India funded seventy-six industrial
pollution abatement projects and nine common industrial liquid effluent treatment plants collecting
discharges from some 2,500 small scale industries. The average loan size was about US$2 million.
This loan also provided US$10 million in technical assistance to strengthen State Pollution Control



The Environment Sector                                                                  25
Boards in three states. The success of this project was attributed to a balanced use of economic
incentives and command and control measures (i.e., "carrots" and "sticks"). Economic incentives
included: (i) onlending rate set at 2% below market rate; (ii) 100% grant for preinvestment studies;
(iii) government grant contributions for the establishment of common treatment plants; (iv) government
grants to support the introduction of innovative pollution control technologies; and (v) accelerated
depreciation of pollution control investments, i.e., 100% depreciation allowance in the first year.
From the command and control perspective, there was strong state Government commitment to force
polluting industries into court actions for pollution violations, and even to plant closures. In both
India and Brazil, special units were set up in the onlending banks or pollution control agencies to assist
in project preparation and appraisal.
2.89          The NPAF proposed for Russia is of more modest scale and has more limited
objectives than described above, because of the focus on commercially viable resource recovery
pollution abatement projects. However, there are many similarities which make the lessons gained
from the previous Bank projects of particular relevance. These include: (i) setting up a technical
assistance unit under the Fund to assist in preparation and appraisal of subprojects; (ii) providing
financial assistance as part grants and loans for carrying out preinvestment studies; (iii) setting the
onlending rate slightly below the "market" rates and providing loans with longer term maturities; (iv)
assisting regional governments in three regions under the TA component of EMP to identify priority
pollution control investments and to strengthen pollution permitting, fee collection and enforcement
capacity; (v) carrying out environmental sector studies and development of pollution abatement action
plans in the major polluting industry sectors to assist in identifying investment priorities; and (vi) not
restricting investments to any particular region at this stage, although subprojects in European Russia
are likely to predominate. Moreover, financial projections for the NPAF are based on rather
conservative assumptions as indicated by the India and Brazil experiences. These include an average
loan size of $1.6 million and a three year commitment period for the Bank loan.
3.     Lessons from Other Bank Operations in Russia
2.90          Lessons learned from the first Oil Rehabilitation Project and other operations in Russia
highlight the importance of: (i) identifying a consistent committed counterpart team with sufficient
authority to move the project forward; (ii) coordinating among key interested parties at the federal and
regional levels on critical or controversial project issues; (iii) early detailed attention to procurement
and other implementation issues and the benefits of a PPF in this regard; and (iv) involving local
consultants and institutes in the process.  The design and preparation of the proposed project
anticipates all these issues, with the early setting up of the CPPI with a PPF advance; the setting up
of an interministerial working group to resolve critical project issues; and the drafting of Framework
Agreements between MEPNR and the regional committees responsible for subcomponent project
implementation.






STAFF APPRAISAL REPORT
RusSIAN FEDERATION
ENVIRONMENTAL MANAGEMENT PROJECT
III. ENVIRONMENTAL FRAMEWORK PROGRAM (EFP)
A.    Framework Program Objectives
3.1          The Russian Federation has requested the World Bank to assist in establishing an
Environmental Framework Program (EFP) whose objectives are to (a) incorporate environmental and
natural resource management concerns directly into the economic, social and political adjustment
process at the federal and regional levels of government, including the executive and legislative
branches; (b) strengthen and streamline institutional and organizational structures for environmental
and natural resourze management; (c) improve environmental and natural resource policy and strategy
formulation and implementation; (d) upgrade environmental and natural resource management systems;
(e) strengthen financial delivery mechanisms to address priority environmental management investment
needs through the setting up and initial capitalization of a National Pollution Abatement Facility
(NPAF); and (f) facilitate the flow of donor and multilateral funds and resources to the environmental
protection sector.
B.    Framework Program Description
3.2          The EFP is estimated to cost a total of US$ 282 million over a period of
approximately four to five years. It addresses environmental and natural resource management issues
at federal, regional and local levels in demonstration areas across a wide spectra of natural areas and
from the North Caucasus in the southwest and Murmansk in the northwest to Khabarovsk in the east.
The EFP consists of eight principal components:
(1)   Institutional and Policy Strengthening:
(a) Policy and Regulatory Support;
(b) Environmental Epidemiology;
(c) Human Resources Development and Training;
(d) Regional and Local Pilot Demonstration Projects;
(2)    Air Quality Management;
(3)    Water Quality and Water Resource Management;
(4)    Hazardous Waste Management;
(5)    Biodiversity Conservation and Natural Resources Management;
(6)    Conservation and Management of Cultural and Natural Heritage;
(7)    Setting Up and Capitalization of a National Pollution Abatement Facility; and
(8)    Center for Project Preparation and Implementation
1.    Institutional and Policy Strengthening Component (US$47.0 milion)
3.3          Activities planned within the institutional  strengthening and policy upgrading
component fall into two categories. The first are activities for the federal and regional levels of
government, that will address institutional and policy issues that are critical for strengthening
organizational and management structures and to improving financial management for environmental



Framework Program                                                                  27
and nature protection within the MEPNR and other federal and regional agencies. The second are
activities that address specific environmental issues at selected regional and local sites. These
activities focus on developing local action plans to address priority environmental concerns in a least-
cost way. These local environmental actions plans will include assistance to strengthen local
enforcement measures and upgrade local environment management capabilities. However, the World
Bank project described in Chapter IV focusses only on activities at the federal and regional levels.
3.4          The federal and regional activities have been divided into three subcomponents and
reflect the priorities identified for assistance by the MEPNR, the World Bank and bilateral donors
(a)    Policy and Regulatory Support;
(b)    Environmental Epidemiology;
(c)    Human Resources Development and Training.
1(a)         Policy and Regulatory Support (US$21.1 million)
3.5          Responsibility for policy on environmental issues is currently dispersed across MEPNR
as well as other Ministries and State Committees. There is a need to consolidate these activities and
to develop the capacity to formulate and implement policies designed to achieve clear environmental
goals at both federal and regional levels. As outlined in Annex 3. 1, the key elements of support to
achieve this objective are:
3     establish a Federal Environmental Policy Unit responsible to the Federal Commission
on the Environment;
3     agree on a clear set of environmental priorities and of measures designed to address
these priorities based on an assessment of the extent of and trends in environmental
damage across sectors and regions;
3     develop the economic, legal and institutional framework for the implementation of
these measures;
-     prepare and implement a small number of Sectoral Environmental Action Plans with
the target of reducing the environmental damage to human health and ecosystems
caused by these sectors by 25-50 percent over 3-5 years;
*     prepare and implement a small number of Regional Environmental Action Plans
designed to achieve significant improvements in environmental conditions in some
heavily polluted regions;
*     negotiate agreements with major emission sources in the sectors and regions covered
by the project to achieve phased reductions in emissions over 3-5 years; and
*     develop the capacity of regional environmental authorities to implement and monitor
targeted programs of environmental improvement.
1(b)   Environmental Epidemiology Component (US$9.0 million)
3.6           As outlined in Annex 3.2, this component will focus on strengthening the national and
regional environmental epidemiology management system for: (i) the collection and management of



28                                                        Environmental Management Project
data relevant to environmental health issues; (ii) the identification of the most urgent problems and
an analysis of the causes responsible for damage to health most relevant for policy making; and (iii)
the development of policies to address such problems and to monitor their success. Building upon
existing efforts to prepare a national program for, and regular review of environmental health in
Russia, the environmental epidemiology component would focus on the following activities at national
and regional levels:
*     establish a Federal Environmental Epidemiology Center and strengthen national
facilities and institutes in Moscow;
*     establish two Regional Environmental Epidemiology Centers in the Urals and the
Upper Volga regions;
*     establish a regional capacity for incorporating environmental health data into
environmental management decision making;
*     improve the existing system for collecting, managing and analyzing data on infant and
child health as the most sensitive indicator of environmental health;
*     develop and incorporate occupational health data systems into environmental
management decision making at the federal and regional levels;
*     develop a series of training courses for environmental health; and
*     develop twinning relationships with foreign environmental health institutes.
1(c)   Human Resources Development and Training Component (US$3.8 million)
3.7          As discussed in Annex 2, the focus of this component is to develop suitable training
courses to train trainers and to develop the capacity of existing Russian institutions to continue ongoing
training courses for environmental management. The subject areas that have been identified as the
highest priority are:
*     policy, programming and decision making at the federal and regional levels;
*     operation proficiency and managerial capabilities within the Ministry;
*     public education, participation and conflict resolution; and
*     environmental regulatory enforcement capabilities of the MEPNR and regional
committees.
I(d)   Regional and Local Pilot Demonstration Projects (US$13.1 million)
3.8          Four regional and local pilot demonstration institutional development projects (see
Annex 2) are proposed under the framework program in the following regions and cities:
(i)    Khabarovsk Krai
(ii)    Nizhnii Tagil
(iii)   Novokuznetsk
(iv)   Murmansk Oblast



Framework Program                                                                  29
These pilot projects will focus on:
*     developing local and regional environmental strategies and action plans;
*     strengthening institutional arrangements for environmental management;
*     testing and implementing new environmental policies and regulations for adoption
nationwide;
improving training and education in environmental issues and concerns; and
*     improving environmental management at the enterprise level.
2.    Air Quality Management Component (US$17.8 million)
3.9          Many cities in Russia have significant air pollution problems with pollutant
concentrations over 10 times the Maximum Pollutant Concentration (MPC) level. However, in order
to develop the Air Quality Management system (AQM) the region of Volgograd was selected as the
pilot site for this component. The objective of the project are to: (i) characterize air quality in
Volgograd using modern monitoring methodologies; (ii) improve AQM principles, policies, and
compliance methods; (iii) improve the Russian Federation's technical approach to solving air quality
problems and develop a preliminary least cost strategy in Volgograd as a demonstration; and (iv) to
disseminate the results of this pilot project throughout Russia as an improved AQM system nationwide.
The project (see Annex 2) would involve the following activities:
*     evaluation of existing methodologies and measurement methods for air emission
estimation and ambient air quality;
*     assessment of the current system of defining air quality sources;
*     improving emission inventory methods, source test methods and emission factors;
*     reviewing and revising atmospheric dispersion modelling, air quality monitoring and
siting of monitors, quality assurance methods, and development of nationwide data
information systems;
*     improving and revising the system for regulating new and existing air emission
sources and modifications of sources;
*     developing  methods for assessing  and  managing  pollutant transport and
transboundary/global air pollution problems through local and regional AQM
programs;
*     upgrading laboratory and field measurement capabilities and monitoring procedures
in Volgograd for ambient air and source emissions;
*     demonstration of source testing, saturation monitoring, receptor and airshed
modelling;



30                                                        Environmental Management Project
*     development of least cost AQM strategies for Volgograd;
*     improving AQM regulations; and
*     improving AQM enforcement capabilities.
3.     Water Quality and Water Resource Management Component (US$28 million)
3.10         The priorities for environmental management in the water sector in Russia (see
Annexes 2 and 3.3) have been identified as follows: (i) ensure the availability and supply of safe
drinking water; (ii) restore and maintain the ecological health of rivers and other water bodies; and
(iii) promote water conservation and strengthen integrated water quality and quantity management.
These priorities are reflected in the program of fourteen pilot projects in five water sheds or regions
(see Table A2.1 of Annex 2) that have been identified for funding under this component. The
objectives of these pilot projects are to:
*     reduce human health risks from unsafe drinking water supplies;
*     develop more effective regulatory policies from point and non-point sources of
pollution;
*     improve discharge permitting systems and compliance;
*     improve efficiency and operation of existing and new drinking water treatment plants;
*     develop basin and water body specific ambient water quality standards more closely
related to actual local water uses;
*     develop capacity to carry out integrated, short-term water quality assessments and
identify priorities;
*     develop basin scale integrated water quality management plans to achieve ambient
standards;
*     improve industrial pre-treatment pollution control;
*     develop economic incentives for water conservation and efficient water use;
*     develop institutional arrangements to enable integrated water quality and water
resource management; and
*     increase public awareness and understanding of the value of water.
4.    Hazardous Waste Management Component (US$16.9 million)
3.11         The effective management of industrial waste generally and hazardous waste in
particular represents a major environmental priority in Russia. The current policy objectives in this
area are: (i) reduction of hazardous waste production; (ii) increasing the reuse and recovery of
resources from these wastes; (iii) mitigation of impacts of past management practices; and (iv)
developing effective controls over their generation and management.  The Hazardous Waste



Framework Program                                                                     31
Management Component (see Annexes 2 and 3.4) is intended to assist the country in achieving these
objectives through the three following strategic subcomponent projects targeted at the national,
regional and enterprise specific levels respectively:
*     Development of a national hazardous waste data management system, providing a
capability within the federal government to collect, consolidate and evaluate waste
generation and disposition information, such that problems and issues can be
prioritized, policies established, and resources efficiently allocated;
*     Development and demonstration of a regulatory management system at the regional
level in Vologda and Yaroslavl Oblasts, which cover the classification, tracking,
permitting and measurement capacity required for "cradle to grave" management of
these wastes and which can be emulated elsewhere in the country; and
*     Undertaking enterprise specific waste management related environmental management
studies in six strategic industrial sectors: oil refining; steel making; metal fabrication;
printing; paint production; and chemicals, with the objective of demonstrating current
waste management practices as part of modernization and conversion initiatives.
5.     Biodiversity Conservation and National Resource Management Component (US$27.2
million)
3.12          The first two subcomponents would be supported under the project and the third
subcomponent would be financed through a Swiss grant ($3 million).
3.13          The objective of this component (see Annex 2) is to strengthen government capacity
to manage its natural resources, including biodiversity in an sustainable way. The project will assist
the government in three major areas:
*     Formulation of a biodiversity strategy, and through it generation of a national
consensus on priorities regarding the allocation and use of natural resources. Given
the multi-dimensional and multi-sectoral nature of such a strategy, its formulation will
draw decision-makers from different sectors and agencies and will help Government
to incorporate environmental concerns into its macro-reform agenda.
*     Protection of areas that are central to the conservation of an adequate level of
biodiversity, with special emphasis on the national park and nature reserve systems.
Protection of these areas is now critical, as the deteriorating economic situation is
increasing pressure for the use of protected area resources, both by industry and by
neighboring communities, who are seeking to exploit these resources for their
livelihood.
*     Management of natural resources located in the Lake Baikal Region-an area still
abundant in natural resources and with a major resource stock of vital interest to the
Russian Federation and to the world at large. Regional approaches to natural resource
management will be put in place under the project.



32                                                      Environmental Management Project
6.    Conservation and Management of Cultural and Natural Heritage (US$2.7 milion)
3.14         The Conservation and Management of Cultural and National Heritage component (see
Annex 2) consists of three main activities: (i) Policy and Regulatory Support; (ii) Institution Building
and Strengthening of Management and Implementation Mechanisms; and (iii) Development of a
National Program for the Conservation and Management of Cultural and Natural Heritage.
3.15         In addition to improving the management of cultural heritage, this component will:
*     provide necessary financial and technical assistance to improve the protection of the
nation's heritage;
*     assist the Government of Russia to develop its national cultural and natural heritage
strategy within the context of current macro policy changes;
*     strengthen institutional capacity to ensure the coordinated and effective administration
and implementation of the policy, particularly in regard to the linkage between
environmental, economic, natural resource, and cultural heritage policies;
*     support legislative and administrative reform pertaining to cultural and natural heritage
conservation; and
*     promote the training of staff and encourage the participation of local communities.
7.    National Pollution Abatement Facility (US$137 million)
3.16         Discussions on this component are in Chapter IV and Annex 4.
8.    Center for Project Preparation and Implementation (US$5.5 million)
3.17         Discussions on this component are in Chapter IV and Annex 7.
C.    EFP Cost and Financing Plan
3.18         The estimated cost of the Environmental Framework Program is summarized in Table
3.1. The total cost of the proposed program is estimated at US$282.1 million (including taxes and
duties, as well as physical and price contingencies) with a foreign exchange component of US$149.6
million, or 53% of the total program cost. The Bank loan would finance US$110 million, or 39%
of the total program. As agreed at a Donors Meeting on April 13, 1994, the current EFP financing
package includes grant funds from the following countries and organizations: Canada; EU; Finland;
Norway; Switzerland; USA; and the GEF. The total amount pledged by these agencies was
approximately US$90 million. This financing will be achieved through parallel cofinancing and will
be coordinated by the CPPI. The balance of the financing (US$92 million) would be provided by the
GOR, as well as Russian enterprises and commercial banks.
