STAFF APPRAISAL REPORT
RuSsIAN FEDERATION
ENVIRONMENTAL MANAGEMENT PROJECT
IV. THE PROJECT
A.    Project Objectives
4.1          The Environmental Management Project (EMP) would provide critical support to the
EFP. The main objectives of the project are to assist the Russian authorities to: (i) strengthen and
streamline federal and regional institutional structures for environmental and natural resource
management; (ii) improve federal and regional environmental policy and strategy formulation and
implementation; (iii) upgrade environmental and natural resource management systems; (iv) assist in
the financing of economically viable, high priority resource recovery/pollution abatement projects in
the country; and (v) facilitate the flow of donor funds and resources to the environmental protection
sector.
B.    Project Description
4.2          The EMP consists of the following five main components:
1.    Institutional and Policy Strengthening:
(a)    Policy and Regulatory Support; and
(b)    Environmental Epidemiology;
2.    Core Elements of the Water Quality and Water Resource Management Component;
3.    Core Elements of the Hazardous Waste Management Component;
4.    National Pollution Abatement Facility; and
5.    Center for Project Preparation and Implementation.
4.3          The first three technical assistance subcomponents of the EMP will focus on
environmental management and institutional strengthening at the federal and regional levels in three
regions of European Russia: the Upper Volga, the Urals and North Caucasus. An integrated
approach to regional environmental management will be achieved through the Policy and Regulatory
Support Component, which will closely link with the Environmental Epidemiology, Water Quality and
Water Resource Management, and Hazardous Waste Components in identifying, developing and
setting policies and regulations for implementation by regional environmental regulatory agencies, and
at the federal level.
4.4          Activities under the Policy and Regulatory Support Component will provide the
integrating framework for the whole EMP. The Environmental Epidemiology component has been
designed to provide the basis for setting environmental priorities at both the federal and the regional
levels. Drawing upon the assessment of the impact of environmental problems on human health, the



The Project                                                                                               35
goal is to ensure that the environmental targets are directed to achieving the best feasible reductions
in mortality and morbidity linked to environmental causes. Similarly, the Water Quality Management
and Hazardous Waste Management components (as well as the Air Quality Management Project under
the EFP) will draw upon the environmental assessments and priorities, policy and regulatory
development, sectoral studies and regional action plans, which form the core elements of the Policy
and Regulatory component. Finally, the National Pollution Abatement Facility (NPAF) will follow
the environmental damage assessments and priorities in determining which proposals should receive
priority funding. Where its project criteria are met, the resources of the NPAF can also be used to
support the implementation of the Sectoral Environmental Action Plans.
I(a)    Policy and Regulatory Support Component (US$21.1 million)
4.5              The overall objective of this component (for more details see Annex 3.1) is to help
accelerate a reduction in environmental damage in the Urals and Upper Volga regions within a period
of 3-5 years with a particular focus on mitigating the impact of pollution on human health-'. To meet
this goal there will be a need to:
*       develop the capacity at federal and regional levels to set environmental priorities, to
formulate, implement and enforce environmental policies, and to monitor
environmental conditions; and
*       establish  the  administrative, economic, legal and  technical basis for reaching
negotiated agreements to reduce emissions from specific industries in a particular
region.
4.6              The successful achievement of these subsidiary objectives at federal level and in two
demonstration regions will provide a foundation for extending the approach to other regions in Russia,
as well as for ensuring continued progress in reducing environmental damage in the Urals and the
Upper Volga regions. Thus, the component will contribute to the ultimate objective of ensuring that
economic recovery and development in Russia over the next 5-10 years is accompanied by
improvements in environmental conditions rather than by a deterioration as in the past.
4.7              The component will be implemented by establishing a Federal Environmental Policy
Unit in Moscow and Two Regional Environmental Policy Units in Ekaterinburg (Urals region covering
the Chelyabinsk, Perm and Sverdlovsk oblasts) and in Yaroslavl (Upper Volga region covering the
Yaroslavl and Vologda oblasts). The Project Implementation Manual contains draft framework
agreements outlining the implementation arrangements.
4.8              The Federal Environmental Policy Unit will be responsible to the newly established
Federal Commission on the Environment which oversees environmental issues across a range of
ministries and state committees on behalf of the Council of Ministers (i.e., the Government). The key
activities of the Federal Environmental Policy Unit will include:
4/ The economic and social returns from the successful implementation of this component will be extremely high. A very modest target
would be to accelerate reductions of 10 percent in ambient levels of particulates, heavy metals, sulfur dioxide and toxic hydrocarbons in
the main cities of the two regions by five years relative to what would have happened in the absence of the project. Experience in preparing
the Environmental Action Program for Central and Eastem Europe for the 1993 Luceme conference suggests that this can be achieved by
.win-win" measures which involve zero net economic costs other than the policy work and associated technical assistance to be financed
under this component. On this basis the economic rate of return for the component in terms of the reduction in environmental damage to
human health will exceed 200 percent even on the most conservative assumptions for valuing the costs of excess mortality and morbidity.



36                                                                    Environmental Management Project
*       preparing assessments of the extent of and trends in environmental damage across
sectors and regions and recommending priorities for federal environmental policies
based on damage to human health and to ecosystems caused by pollution, natural
resource use and other environmental causes;
*       developing the economic, legal and institutional framework and capacity for the
implementation of federal environmental policies and regulations;
*       working  with  non-environmental ministries  and  agencies to  ensure  that the
environmental costs or benefits of measures adopted by those bodies are taken fully
into account when decisions on economic policies, investments and other matters are
made;
*       preparing and implementing a set of Sectoral Environmental Action Plans for five
sectors: (a) iron and steel; (b) non-ferrous metals; (c) oil refining and petrochemicals;
(d) basic organic and inorganic chemicals; and (e) agriculture with particular attention
to intensive livestock operations-';
*       reviewing and updating the National Environmental Action Plan at regular intervals
in the light of changing environmental conditions and priorities;
*       coordinating and supporting the work carried out in the Regional Units with a
particular emphasis on assisting them in the negotiation of emission reduction
agreements with the major emission sources within the sectors covered in the Sectoral
Environmental Action Plans; and
*       providing technical assistance support to the NPAF for project feasibility studies and
project appraisals (see para 4.66).
4.9             The Regional Environmnental Policy Units will be responsible to the inter-oblast
environmental councils which have been, or are being, established in the two regions and will work
closely with the oblast authorities with environmental responsibilities -- including the oblast branches
of federal ministries and state committees, oblast administrations and the environment committees of
oblast parliaments. The advantage of working through the inter-oblast environmental councils is that
for the same investment, activities can be initiated simultaneously in two or three adjacent oblasts.
However, the regional unit will be located in one of the oblasts that will provide all the necessary
basic support infrastructure for the unit. The work program of each Regional Environmental Policy
Unit will include:
*       the preparation and updating of Oblast Environmental Action Plans which will provide
the basis for setting specific targets for mitigating environmental damage within the
framework of the project drawing upon the work to be undertaken under the
Environmental Epidemiology Component;
51 Parallel sectoral activities are underway, or planned for two other sectors. The preparation of a Sectoral Environmental Action Plan
for the pulp and paper industry is being funded by the Finnish Goverment. An extensive review of the forestry sector - funded as part
of the Bank's sector work program with additional assistance from GEF and bilateral donors - should start later in 1994 and may lead to
a project which could follow up the environmental recommendations that emerge from the sector work.



The Project                                                                               37
*      applying federal environmental policies and regulations at oblast level in a manner that
is consistent with the priorities and targets established in the Oblast Environmental
Action Plans;
*      providing policy and regulatory support for the Water Quality Management and
Hazardous Waste Management components of the EMP;
*      negotiating agreements with major emission sources to achieve phased reductions in
emissions over the period of the project by drawing upon technical advice provided
by the sectoral studies and teams, as well as the economic and regulatory incentives
and penalties available to the provincial authorities; and
*      monitoring the effectiveness of environmental measures and recommending changes
designed to enhance their impact, or to take account of new information or changing
circumstances.
4.10          The assistance will be provided over a period of 4 years starting in November 1994.
The first 4 months of the project will be primarily devoted to providing specialized training courses
in environmental law and environmental policy for the prospective staff of the Federal and Regional
Units, as well as on establishing offices and procuring equipment. Thus, the Federal and Regional
Units' are expected to start full operation in April 1995.
4.11          A Policy and Regulatory Management Committee will be established by the Federal
Commission on the Environment to supervise the implementation of the component.  This
Management Committee will include representatives from the Ministries and State Committees
represented on the Commission, as well as representatives from other economic Ministries such as the
Ministries of Fuel and Energy and of Industry which will either be affected by, or should work with
the Federal and Regional Units. Subject to the agreement of the Bank, the Management Committee
will appoint a Project Manager for the Policy and Regulatory Component who will also serve as the
Director of the Federal Environmental Policy Unit.
4.12          The Federal and Regional Units will be staffed by Russian experts. The Federal Unit
will have a Director (the Project Manager), a Deputy Director who will be a qualified environmental
lawyer-' and will also act as the Unit's Chief Legal Adviser, and three team leaders for the federal,
regional and sectoral teams. At least 5 of the staff of the Federal Unit will be qualified environmental
lawyers, and a further 5 will have postgraduate qualifications in economics plus training in
environmental economics. It will be condition of employment in the professional grades of the
Federal and Regional Units, except for the Director, that individuals must pass either a intensive 2-3
month training course in environmental law or one in environmental policy analysis before they join
the staff.
4.13          The Urals Regional Unit will have a Director (at the same level as the team leaders
in the Federal Unit), at least 5 qualified environmental lawyers and 3 staff with postgraduate
qualifications in economics plus training in environmental economics. The Upper Volga Regional Unit
will have a Director, at least 3 qualified environmental lawyers and 2 staff with postgraduate
qualifications in economics plus training in environmental economics.
6/ In this context a qualified environmental lawyer is someone with a recognized law degree involving at least 112] semesters of higher
education plus a satisfactory performance in a full-time one semester course on environmental law such as that provided in the training
program for prospective staff.



38                                                        Environmental Management Project
4.14         A Policy and Regulatory Consultant will be appointed to provide policy advice and
to undertake specific studies during the course of the project. The terms of reference for this contract
will specify an initial set of studies and policy advice, as well as an overall level of effort and set of
skills for the remainder of the work to be undertaken. In selecting the consultant a strong preference
will be given to proposals which are based on a team drawing on experience with environmental
policymaking and implementation in several countries with differing legal systems and approaches to
economic incentives and environmental regulations.
4.15         A Training Consultant will be appointed to design and organize the formal training
courses to be provided at the beginning of the project and the program of secondments. The
consultant should include a Russian training institution in its team, which can be responsible for taking
over and providing the formal training courses after the first year. The program of secondments
proposed by the consultant should cover more than one country, so that preference will be given to
consultants which can put together consortia of policy institutes and other appropriate agencies in
several countries.
4.16         In addition to the Policy and Regulatory Consultant and the Training Consultant, there
will be sector consultancy contracts, one for each of the sectors to be covered by the Federal Sectoral
Team. Each of these consultants will be responsible for drawing up a Sectoral Environmental Action
Plan in cooperation with the Sectoral Team in the Federal Unit and with the relevant Regional Units.
It will then be expected to provide support and advice for the process of implementing the Action
Plan, especially with respect to the development of technical and economic proposals to underpin the
negotiation of emission reduction agreements with major polluters in the sector. Some of these
sectoral activities may be funded by bilateral donors in a manner similar to the development of an
Environmental Action Plan for the Pulp and Paper Sector which is being funded by the Finnish
Government.
1(b)   Environmental Epidemiology Component (US$9.0 million)
4.17         A large body of work on environmental health is being undertaken at both the national
and oblast levels in the Russian Federation. However, the quality of the data and methods used are
inconsistent and there has been no coordinated attempt to develop a national strategy to identify,
evaluate and address threats to human health from environmental pollution. In the past, horizontal
flows of information were very limited, so that local and regional officials were unable to pool
knowledge and experience in dealing with environmental health issues. Even now they find it difficult
to evaluate local concerns in the context of national problems and priorities.
4.18         The objective of Environmental Epidemiology Component (Annex 3.2) will be to
strengthen the national and regional environmental epidemiology management system for: (i) the
collection and management of data relevant to environmental health issues; (ii) the identification of
the most urgent problems and an analysis of the causes responsible for damage to health most relevant
for policy making; and (iii) the development of policies to address such problems and to monitor their
success. In summary, the environmental epidemiology component will focus on the following
activities which will be incorporated into three interlinked subcomponent projects: one at the federal
level; and two at the regional level (in the Urals and Upper Volga regions).
(a)    Establish a Federal Environmental Epidemiology Center and strengthen federal
facilities and institutes in Moscow with particular emphasis on developing the capacity
to provide assessments of environmental health priorities relevant for national
legislation and policy.



The Project                                                                        39
(b)    Establish two Regional Environmental Epidemiology Centers to provide the essential
expertise for the collection and analysis of data, and support for environmental and
health officials in regional and local governments. Undertake specific investigations
of the impact that emissions from individual plants have on the health of the
population at risk.
(c)    Develop the capacity to enable regional authorities in the Urals region and the region
of the Upper Volga to develop their own policy priorities based on regionai
assessments of environmental health conditions.
(d)   Improve the existing system for generating, collecting, managing and analyzing data
on infant and child health as the basis for monitoring the consequences of changes in
population exposures to various pollutants. Data on variables such as the birth
weight, birth defects, infant diseases and child development can be sensitive indicators
for the impact of environmental conditions on the population in general.
(e)   Improve the existing Russian system to design and manage a program to collect and
analyze data on the health status and environmental conditions of workers in different
industrial sectors of the economy and populations in industrial sanitary zones, who are
at greatest risk due to environmental health impacts.
(f)    Develop and provide a series of policy orientated training courses in environmental
epidemiology.
(g)    Promote twinning relationships with foreign institutes responsible for policy orientated
environmental epidemiology work.
4.19         The responsible department in MEPNR for this component is the Department of
Ecological Safety.  The Project Implementation Manual contains draft framework agreements
describing the implementation arrangements for the component.  A Component Management
Commnittee consisting of representatives of MEPNR,  the State Committee for Sanitary and
Epidemiological Surveillance (GOSKOMSANEPIDNADZOR), the State Committee for
Hydrometereology (ROSGIDROMET), the Ministry of Health, the Ministry of Economic Affairs, the
Russian Academy of Sciences, and the Russian Academy of Medical Sciences has been set up to
advise and supervise the work of the Subcomponent Project Managers and their teams at federal and
regional levels. The Federal Program Manager will be responsible for the management of the federal
level subcomponent, and the coordination of the two regional initiatives. He will be responsible for
the federal subcomponent technical subject areas such as the annual federal report, federal databases
and policy oriented analyses. He will be an ex-officio member of the subcomponent Management
Committee and will be assisted in his work by the General Consultant Firm (see para 4.84), as
outlined in this firm's Terms of Reference.
4.20         The federal Subcomponent Project Manager will lead a team of Russian experts who
have been seconded to work full time on the project. A Foreign Consultant Firm will provide foreign
advisors to work with this team, as well as the two regional implementation teams in the Urals and
Upper Volga under regional Subcomponent Project Managers. The two regional Subcomponent
Project Managers will report to Regional Subcomponent Management Committees under the Upper
Volga and Urals Inter-regional Environmental Councils, respectively. The foreign contract for this
component is expected to be awarded to one general contractor, representing a consortium of research
institutes and academia from at least two continents. The assistance will extend over a period of 4



40                                                       Environmental Management Project
years starting in November 1994. Approximately two-thirds of the Consultant's inputs are expected
to be provided in the second and third year of the contract. The Consultant would liaise closely with
other teams undertaking work for other components of the Environmental Management Project.
Training is a crucial element. Much of it will take place through close and continuing collaboration
between foreign advisors and their local counterparts. A part of the formal training program is
designed to precede implementation of the project.
2     Core Elements of the Water Quality Management and Water Resource Management
Component (US$12.4 million)
4.21         The core program of the Water Quality and Water Resources Management Component
(Annex 3.3) focuses on developing and testing solutions to the major water quality management
problems in three key regions: (a) the Upper Volga River basin; (b) the Urals region; and (c) the
North Caucasus region. The subcomponent in each region will consist of an integrated planning and
regulatory reform program aimed at improving drinking water supplies, developing a prioritized
investment and action program, and addressing key sectoral policy issues. Each subcomponent will
also support existing regional organizations and improved regional inter-agency management of
priority issues. In addition, the component will support a Federal Program to provide mechanisms
within the project to disseminate the results of subcomponent projects to others who are not directly
participating in project activities, and to create the opportunity for those working on various
subcomponent projects to meet periodically to exchange experiences. This federal program will be
executed by a Subcomponent Implementation Team under the MEPNR's Department of Surface Water
Protection. The regional programs will be executed by subcomponent implementation teams reporting
to oblast or inter-oblast councils or committees. The Project Implementation Manual contains draft
framework agreements describing these arrangements. It is planned to implement this program over
a period of four years.
(a)    Upper Volga Rver Basin This is a vast region of forests and farms with a modest
population concentrated in an number of large industrial cities and important tourist areas located in
the riverain environment of the Volga River. The river is the main source of water supply for the
region's population, is an important fishery, navigation and transportation link, and source of
hydroelectric power. The subcomponent will support the newly formed Inter-regional Council of the
Upper Volga formed by the five concerned Oblasts with the goal of improving and restoring the
ecological condition of the Upper Volga River. The objectives of the subcomponent include the
identification of strategies and priority investment programs to improve the quality and efficiency of
water supply systems in selected cities, assess alternative region economic policy options to address
management of water use and quality, and develop regulatory reform programs for industrial and
municipal water management.
4.22         In response to a growing regional concern for and recognition of these issues, the five
oblasts which constitute the Upper Volga River basin - Vologda, Ivanovo, Kostroma, Yaroslavl, and
Tver - have formed the Interregional Ecological Council of Upper Volga with the goal of improving
and restoring the ecological condition of the Upper Volga River. The Council consists of six voting
members including the Chairman of the respective Oblast Environmental Committees and the
Chairman of the Council. Representatives of regional NGOs and environmental organizations,
academic and technical organizations in the region, and others may participate in the work of the
Council and have the right of an advisory vote. The current Chairman is the Director of the Institute
of Inland Water Biology in Borok. The members have agreed to form an Interregional Environmental
fund to support the Council's activities financed by contributions from the respective Oblast
Environmental Funds.



The Project                                                                         41
4.23         The competence of the Council includes formulating and making recommendations on
urgent actions for improvement of ecological conditions in the Upper Volga;  determining
mechanisms to finance programs and actions including the determination of Oblast contributions;
adopting joint decisions on disputed intra-regional issues; making recommendations on permanent or
temporary stoppage of industrial discharges; organizing and carrying out regional ecological studies;
organizing international environmental cooperation;  and adopting an annual budget including
allocations of the Upper Volga Ecological Fund to various activities.
4.24         The Council in collaboration with the respective oblast environment committees will
be responsible for supervising the work of the Subcomponent Implementation Team.   Foreign
consultants and regional expertise, including the scientific institutes and universities located in the
region will assist the Subcomponent Implementation Team to carry out the work, and will be
supplemented as necessary by specialized expertise at other Russian institutes. Study tours and
collaboration with foreign specialists will be used to review experience in the OECD countries with
the policy, institutional, and technical issues and options to be considered in this region.
(b)    North Caucasus Region  This subcomponent will focus on the problems of integrated
water management and conservation in the Lower Don River basin (from the Tsimlyansk reservoir
to the Azov sea) in the Rostov Oblast.   The Don River is one of the largest rivers in european
Russia. It has been intensively developed for irrigation, hydropower, and water supply. The water
supply and waste water management problems of the large cities of this region, including Rostov,
include supply deficits characterized by intermittent service, microbiological contamination due to the
lack of treatment capacity and inadequate maintenance of the distribution systems. Reduced flows
brought about by the more intensive irrigation water use, including reservoir storage, have led to
increased salinity in the Azov sea, and pollution in its highly urbanized and industrialized lower basin
have resulted in major fisheries and ecological damage. The objectives of the subcomponent include
the development of prototype monitoring systems, demonstration of small watershed management
practices, development of strategies and prioritized investment programs for improving water supply
and municipal wastewater treatment, and the development of integrated strategies for regional water
management and conservation focusing in particular on improvement in water supply availability and
restoration of the riverain ecosystem in the Lower Don River.
4.25         The Don River Basin Agency (Don BVO), in collaboration with the Rostov Oblast
Committee for the Protection of Nature, will be responsible for implementation of this subcomponent
project. The tasks will be carried out by working groups involving Russian specialists from such
institutions as the North-Caucasus Branch of the Russian Institute for Integrated Utilization and
Protection of Water Resources, the Azov Sea Research Institute of Fishery Problems, Rostov
University, Hydrochemical Institute and others. The work will be coordinated with the international
Black Sea program, coordinated by the World Bank (project results will be directly used for
development of the Black Sea recommendations). Working relationships will be established with the
non-governmental environmental organizations. The activity will include foreign and local training
components, consultations of foreign specialists, and supply of necessary equipment specified
tentatively in the project cost estimates.
(c)    The Urals Region This subcomponent will be located in the major industrial city of
Ekaterinburg, and will focus on the problems of integrated management of the raw and treated water
supply of Ekaterinburg. The large concentration and rapid growth of industry and population
combined with periodic droughts, especially over the last 50 years, has led to the development of
complex water management systems comprising numerous reservoirs and inter-regional transfers.
Public heath officials believe that environmental conditions in the oblast and surrounding areas, which



42                                                      Environmental Management Project
have greatly effected the quality of drinking water supplies, have been a primary factor in the reported
higher than average incidence of disease and worsening morbidity rates. The objectives of this
subcomponent include assessment of the threat to public health from drinking water supplies and
sources of supply for Ekaterinburg; evaluation and prioritization of current infrastructure needs and
the institutional and technical framework for supplying potable water and protecting its quality;
recommend physical, operational, legal, financial, and institutional improvements; demonstrate specific
measures to reduce threats to public health stemming from drinking water; and strengthen local/Oblast
institutions for drinking water management.
4.26         The MEPNR will delegate responsibility for day-to-day technical and administrative
functions for implementation of the regional Water Quality and Water Resource Management
Subcomponents to a Subcomponent Management Committee (SCMC) and a Subcomponent Program
Manager (SCPM). The activities of the SCMC and SCPM will be supported in the following areas
by the CPPI (Annex 7.4) liaison; procurement; disbursement; reporting, accounting, and auditing;
information exchange; and related policies and procedures.
4.27         The SCPM will hire a team of experienced Russian technical personnel (with the
number varying depending on the size and complexity of the program) and administrative and support
personnel to form a Subcomponent Implementation Team (SIT). The SITs will be assisted by local
and foreign experts, hired according to Bank procurement procedures. An overall period of four years
is assumed for the implementation of the Water Quality and Water Resources Management
Component, divided into two phases as outlined below.
4.28         Phase I - Start-up Activities Activities during this initial Phase will establish the
institutional framework for implementation of the Water Quality and Resources Management
Component at the regional level. The EMP loan would finance the establishment and staffing of the
SCPM, provide the specialized training needed by the SCPMs, and the staff of the SITs. The
General Consultant Firm will assist the CPPI in this work. Activities will include:
*     build a national level program team including the SCPMs, staff of the MEPNR, CPPI,
and other key agencies; develop a common concept of the purpose, approach and aims
of the project, establish milestones and specifics goals, and develop regular
mechanisms for communication and coordination;
*     provide training to the SCPMs in CPPI policies and procedures, project management,
work planning and preparation of tender documents for consulting services; develop
mechanisms and approaches for effective working relationships with the CPPI,
donors, and other government agencies at the federal and regional levels;
*     establish the regional SIT Offices including the recruitment of staff, procurement of
equipment, and provide training for SIT staff;
*     build a regional level project team consisting of the SCPM, SIT, as well as Russian
and Foreign experts.
4.29         Phase I(b) - Inception and Work Planning. Activities during this phase, which
follows immediately on the subcomponent team building workshop, would develop a detailed work
plan and plan of implementation for each subcomponent project. Analysis and planning would focus
on sources, availability of data, methodologies, detailed discussion of expected outputs and results
and their relation to the objectives. The work plan includes the description, sequencing, and



The Project                                                                        43
scheduling of work tasks, data requirements, and analytical methods in sufficient detail to prepare the
contractors' TORs and monitor progress of the work. The implementation plan would include a
determination of all inputs required to complete the subcomponent project including contracts to
provide local and foreign technical assistance, equipment, etc. Local technical resources to be used
to implement the pilot project would be identified and evaluated. The implementation plan would
include a schedule of subcontracts and all required Terms of Reference.
4.30         Phase II- Implementation of Subcomponents (3.5years). This phase would consist
of the implementation of each of the subcomponents. The component would provide consultants (local
and foreign) to assist the SCPM and SITs to carry out studies, surveys, and analysis; equipment;
training including workshops and study tours; and demonstration projects and activities.
4.31         Phase II- Federal Program of Outreach and Coordination (3.5 years). This phase
of the project would develop a mechanism to network the various subcomponent projects to insure the
exchange of experience between team working on the same type of pilot project, and a mechanism
such as a annual or semi-annual seminar or workshop to link subcomponent project teams to oblasts
and municipalities that are not participating in the project.  It would run in parallel with the
implementation of each regional subcomponent.
3     Core Elements of the Hazardous Waste Management Component (US$9.8 million)
4.32         MEPNR officials are convinced that abatement and remediation of waste problems in
the Russian Federation cannot proceed successfully without specific hazardous waste legislation along
with a strengthened institutional system for managing hazardous waste according to well-established
"cradle-to-grave" principles. In December 1992, the MEPNR completed a Concept Paper for a major
program entitled simply "Waste. " The Waste program proposes a comprehensive, integrated approach
that will involve all relevant legislative and executive bodies at the federal, regional and local levels.
The program has three strategic objectives: (1) reducing waste production; (2) increasing waste
utilization; and (3) creating ecologically safe waste storage and burial.
4.33         The predominant method of hazardous and industrial waste management in the Russian
Federation is large scale land disposal using unlined lagoons and landfills. Discharge into liquid
industrial wastewater, either directly or through municipal sewers, is also a widespread practice. Few
formal treatment, resource recovery, or waste minimization techniques are applied. There is
widespread illegal and indiscriminate dumping of industrial waste. Waste streams are typically
intermixed, further complicating treatment and disposal. Scarcity of land is putting pressure on
enterprises to find other-than-land disposal methods. Concern for surface water quality and potential
contamination of primary domestic and industrial water supplies is rising.
4.34         Proposed Activities Two subcomponent projects have been identified for World Bank
funding from the hazardous waste component of the Environmental Framework Program: (i)
development of a national industrial waste management data management system (see Annex 3.4.1);
and (ii) development and demonstration of a regulatory management system for hazardous waste
control at the regional level (see Annex 3.4.2). These two core subcomponent projects represent the
development of basic tools required for the implementation of an effective response to the hazardous
waste problem. They have been specifically requested by MEPNR, as being both required and
supportive of current initiatives within the Ministry and its associated agencies at the regional level.
In addition, they offer the opportunity to introduce world wide experience in the field, such that the
country can attain harmonization with international standards and conventions such as the Basel
Convention. The need for these basic tools is also recognized as a prerequisite to being able to
identify, select and justify capital investments in hazardous waste management infrastructure, such as



44                                                         Environmental Management Project
may qualify for domestic and international financing in the future.
4.35          Development of a National Industrial Waste Data Management System. The purpose
of this subcomponent is to develop a comprehensive capability to collect, classify, document, analyze
and report data related to the nature, quantity and disposition of industrial and particularly hazardous
waste generated within the Russian Federation on an ongoing basis. More specifically, the project is
intended to put in place an operational data management and information system which can:
*     provide a uniform national system of waste classification, consistent with international
conventions;
*     aggregate and evaluate waste generation information collected at the enterprise,
regional and national levels as a function of type, industrial sector and region;
*     predict waste generation trends;
*     facilitate the analysis of waste management issues for purposes of legislative and
regulatory action; and
-     allow coordinated planning of improved waste management infrastructure.
4.36          Three defined parts have been identified for the project to be undertaken over a three
year period:  (i)  rapid assessment of existing data; (ii) system design, development and data
verification; and (iii) data evaluation and system implementation.  The first part, targeted for
completion by the end of Year 1, will provide a consolidation of existing national waste information,
a revised national waste classification system consistent with international conventions, and a
preliminary assessment of the data for policy purposes. This will make available an initial information
base for immediate policy planning, as well as providing a basis for subsequent data management
system design. During the second year, Part 2 will be undertaken and encompass the design and
testing of an automated data management system, inclusive of the capability to efficiently collect data
being obtained by regional environment committees as part of mandatory reporting requirements
currently in place. Part 3 will extend over the three year period and involve development of data
analysis capability, specifically directed to its utilization in the planning of waste management
infrastructure, as well as the transfer of the system to MEPNR for ongoing use.
4.37          At the end of the three year period, it is anticipated that the above data management
capability will be operational within MEPNR as a continuing service capability for the development
of improved national policies for the sound management of industrial wastes through an enhanced
ability to prioritize waste management problems and direct resources to their solution.
4.38          The coordinating agency for this subcomponent project will be MEPNR  and
specifically the Hazardous Waste Management Component Management Committee (HWMMC). The
Project Implementation Manual contains the framework agreement outlining the implementation
arrangements for this subcomponent). The HWMMC has been formed to provide overall supervision
of the Hazardous Waste Management Component and, under the Chairmanship of MEPNR, has
representation from:  national industrial committees and ministries; State Institute for Statistics;
Ministry of Economy; and Participating Oblast Environmental Committees.   The project's
implementing team will be located in the Institute of Economic Problems of Nature Management
(IEPNM), which operates as a supporting organization to the MEPNR and which will report to the
HWMMC. The project will be organized under the direction of a Subcomponent Project Manager,
reporting to IEPNM, and responsible for direction of all local and foreign technical resources



The Project                                                                        45
assembled for the project's implementation, and for support resources dedicated to, or contracted by
the project. Under the Subcomponent Project Manger, the Project will be organized in accordance
with its functional parts, namely: Rapid Assessment of Existing Data; System Design, Development
and Data Verification; and Data Evaluation and System Implementation. Each functional part
identified above will be directed by a Task Manager, reporting to the Subcomponent Project Manager,
and who will be responsible for technical and support resources dedicated to the specific part's
implementation. Foreign technical advisors will be assigned to each of the specific parts of the project
under a single contract.
4.39         Development and Demonstration of Regulatory Management Capability for the
Control of Hazardous Waste at the Regional Level The purpose of this subcomponent project is to
develop and demonstrate the basic elements of a functional regulatory capability, necessary to control
the generation and management of hazardous waste at a regional level for emulation elsewhere in the
country. More specifically, it is intended to develop and put in p!ace the necessary tools in the form
of regulations, practices, procedures, and support infrastructure, which can effectively:
*     characterize and classify hazardous wastes for regulatory control purposes;
*     track hazardous wastes from their source of generation to point of disposal;
*     control the management practices applied to them throughout their life cycle; and
*     provide sufficient measurement capability to adequately support the above regulatory
system.
4.40         The functional products of this subcomponent will be the fundamental regulatory
system constituents commonly identified as necessary to effectively control hazardous waste generation
and disposition, namely:
*     detailed hazardous waste classification;
*     hazardous waste generator identification;
*     hazardous waste movement tracking;
*     hazardous waste management facility approval system; and
*     contingency event response planning.
4.41         Each of these will consist of a number of elements, including formal enforceable
regulations, implementation procedures, communication and consultation practice on their
implementation, and training for both regulatory officials and those to whom the system is applied.
The ability to evaluate waste streams analytically is recognized as a basic technical support
requirement and provision for upgrading of laboratory capability is also included in the subcomponent
project's scope.
4.42         Two Oblasts, namely Yaroslavl and Vologda in the Upper Volga region have been
selected for this subcomponent. They have agreed that the Subcomponent Implementation Team will
be located in Yaroslav and will be under the direction of an Inter-regional Environmental Council.
The Project Implementation Manual contains the framework agreement outlining the implementation
arrangement for this component. A high level of commitment and cooperation has been exhibited by



46                                                         Environmental Management Project
the regional and local environment committees, particularly within the major industrial centers of
Yaroslavl and Cherepovetz. Local administrations and elected councils appear willing to take the
necessary action to support a regional regulatory control system. The area offers a wide distribution
of representative industry which will be characteristic of their respective industrial sectors. Significant
industrial and hazardous waste problems have already been identified in the area, some of which are
considered critical in environmental impact terms, particularly in relation to the Volga River and
Ribinsk Reservoir. Finally, several other EMP components which will be complimentary are slated
for implementation in the Region. These include three potential waste related environmental studies
identified as donor funded technical assistance opportunities as part of the Hazardous Waste component
(Cherepovetz Steel Mill, Yaroslavl Paint and Refinery enterprises). A subcomponent of the Water
Quality Management component will be implemented in the Upper Volga Region. A regional
subcomponent project has been identified for Cherepovetz as part of the Environmental Epidemiology
subcomponent.  Finally, the project will be supported with legal resources by the Policy and
Regulatory Support Component.
4.43          The subcomponent project will be undertaken over a four year period with the waste
classification, generator identification and waste movement tracking activities being given priority in
the first two years and the facility licensing and contingency event planning activities being completed
in the latter two years. Two existing regional environmental committee laboratories, one in a major
industrial center in each Oblast, will be upgraded in terms of equipment, operating practice and
environmental monitoring capability.
4.44          A Regional Hazardous Waste Regulatory Project Management Committee will be
established under the auspices of the Inter-Oblast Upper Volga Environmental Council and report to
the overall EMP Hazardous Waste Management Component Management Committee (HWMMC)
established within the MEPNR. Chairmanship of the Committee will be shared on a rotating basis
by the Oblast Environmental Committee Chairman from Vologda and Yaroslavl, or their delegates.
Membership on the Committee shall include but not be limited to representatives of: Oblast
Administrations; Cherepovetz and other City Environmental Committees; Ministry of Environmental
Protection and Natural Resources; Major Regional Industries; Environmental Organization
Representatives; and Regional Technical Institutes.
4.45          This subcomponent project will be organized under the direction of a Subcomponent
Project Manager, reporting to the Committee and be responsible for direction of all local and foreign
technical resources assembled for the Project's implementation, and for support resources dedicated
or contracted to the Project. Under the Project Manger, a Subcomponent Implementation Team will
be organized in accordance with its functional parts, namely:  Waste Classification; Waste
Management Facility Standards; Waste Tracking System; and Measurement Capability.  Each
functional part identified above will be directed by a Task Manager, reporting to the Project Manager,
and who shall be responsible for technical resources dedicated to the specific parts's implementation.
Foreign technical advisors will be assigned to specific parts with an additional senior foreign technical
advisor assigned to assist the Subcomponent Project Manager with both technical and project
management matters and coordinate the activities of other foreign technical advisors assigned to
specific parts of the Project.
4.46          The Project will be headquartered and based in Yaroslavl in office space to be supplied
by the Oblast administration. Specific project tasks may be based at other locations in the region as
determined by mutual agreement among the parties, considering the practicality of effectively
undertaking the specific work requirements at that location, and the availability of suitable office and
living accommodation for project staff and technical advisors during project assignments, as well as
the effective management and organization of the project. Laboratory facilities selected for upgrading

will be those currently operated by the oblast and/or city environmental committees at one location
in each of Vologda and Yaroslavl Oblasts. Final selection of the upgraded laboratory locations will

4. National Pollution Abatement Facility (US$137 million)
4.47          Introduction.  During the central planning period, substantially higher levels of
emissions were tolerated in the Soviet Union than were allowed in the rest of Europe. In addition,
central planning distorted domestic energy and natural resource prices, with the result that there was
no incentive to recover valuable natural resources emitted into the environment. The legacy of these
policies is severe environmental degradation, as well as a capital stock that is inefficient, highly energy
intensive, and pollutes not just the Russian Federation but other parts of the world.
4.48          The failure to invest in resource recovery projects has resulted in a plethora of "win-
win" investment opportunities in which investors can earn a satisfactory rate of return and reduce
noxious pollutants by capturing commercially valuable commodities currently being emitted into the
environment. Such projects are characterized by raw material inputs which have zero or negative
costs and outputs which can be exported. These resource recovery/pollution abatement investments
will not only improve the environment, but will also assist the GOR to implement an economic
transition program.  However, mobilizing funds to invest in such resource recovery/pollution
abatement projects presents major challenges due to various uncertainties, including (i) the difficulty
in encouraging inward investment; (ii) political uncertainties; (iii) lack of project preparation and
financing capabilities; and (iv) unwillingness of both local and foreign banks to provide the medium
to long term loans needed to finance these investments.
4.49          The GOR has recognized that at this critical time in the development of new economic,
political, and social structures, Bank funds can play an essential role by providing: (i) a direct source
of funds which would not otherwise be available; (ii) comfort to other foreign investors by virtue of
their ability to associate their money with Bank funds; and (iii) access to a stock of well-prepared
resource recovery projects with significant environmental external benefits.
4.50          The investments required by these resource recovery/pollution abatement projects are
generally too small and the number of individual projects too large for the Bank to lend to directly.
Consequently, an effective and efficient mechanism needs to be developed which will receive funds
from the Bank and other donors and on-lend them to individual sub-projects.
4.51          To this end, GOR is in the process of establishing a National Pollution Abatement
Facility (NPAF) initially financed from the Bank loan. The NPAF would help to finance a portion of
the investment costs of commercially viable resource recovery/pollution abatement projects with an
identifiable and secure stream of either foreign exchange or ruble denominated earnings that could be
used to repay NPAF loans as well as loans from other lenders. It would be authorized to lend to state
owned enterprises, local or foreign private entities, and joint ventures.
4.52          In addition, to the extent possible, the NPAF would be authorized to syndicate or sell
all or part of its existing loans to local commercial banks on a non-recourse basis, thereby reducing
the Government's exposure to commercial risk and enabling the NPAF to maximize its catalytic
impact and revolve its resources more rapidly. The proceeds of all loan sales will be redeposited in
the NPAF and will be available to finance additional sub-loans. Special efforts will be made to alert
local commercial banks to the lending opportunities associated with NPAF projects. In the event that



48                                                            Environmental Management Project
local commercials banks are interested in financing a large percentage of the total project cost, the
NPAF will step aside and provide only the minimum necessary financial support. The NPAF will not
finance projects whose expected rate of return would indicate that they could pay the interest rates
expected to be charged under the World Bank-supported Enterprise Support Project (ESP), provided
that the project meets the other eligibility criteria for ESP financing and commercial banks are willing
to provide the necessary term financing.2'
4.53          The NPAF will also provide an institutional framework for the GEF, ODS Phase Out
Program, and various multilateral and bilateral agencies to channel their resources to well-prepared,
carefully appraised environment projects in the Russian Federation, including projects developed under
the auspices of the Baltic Initiative and the European Community's carbon tax initiative. Support for
these additional projects could take the form of loans, grants, and debt for environment swaps,
depending on the requirements of the project and the preference of the donor. Financial resources
provided under the auspices of these programs would be processed by the NPAF's existing cadre of
core consultants, although the actual resources to support these sub-projects would be independent of
the NPAF's main activities supported by this loan.
4.54          NPAF Design and Operating Procedures. Organizational arrangements for the NPAF
are shown in Figure 4.1. A core NPAF consultant team will be established under the Center for
Project Preparation and Implementation (CPPI) to manage the appraisal and supervision of investment
projects. This team will also be assisted by technical and legal consultants as required. An NPAF
Supervisory Board (Supervisory Board) will also be established, consisting of high level government
officials, to review and approve sub-project investment proposals and to decide policy matters relating
to the operation of the NPAF. Organization of the CPPI is discussed below (paras. 4.81-4.86).
4.55          The NPAF will be established pursuant to a formal resolution of the Government
which will indicate, inter alia, that (i) the CPPI, assisted by NPAF consultants, will provide
professional services related to the selection, design, appraisal, and supervision of investment projects,
on the basis of specific criteria and procedures outlined in the Operating Instructions; (ii) the NPAF
Supervisory Board consisting of high level representatives of appropriate ministries and agencies of
the Borrower will review and approve investment projects proposed by the CPPI; and (iii) the MOF,
as the sub-loan lender of record, will take decisions related to investment projects considered and
approved by the NPAF Supervisory Board and will enter into appropriate sub-loan agreements. The
MOF will not allocate loan funds to sub-projects that have not passed through the above approval
process. In addition to codifying the organizational arrangements of the NPAF, the Government
resolution will ensure that the NPAF will not be subject to Russian taxation, including VAT and
income and/or profit tax on interest income, nor will it be subject to banking regulations. Finally,
the resolution will stipulate that the NPAF will have the right to analyze proposed environmental
projects, prepare the necessary documentation, perfect security, hire consultants to assist with project
appraisal and other NPAF business, and make recommendations to the NPAF Supervisory Board.
During negotiations, agreement was reached that the adoption by the Borrower of a resolution
and other actions, acceptable to the Bank, necessary to implement the NPAF component, will
be a condition of loan effectiveness. In addition, it was agreed that the Borrower would adopt
legal and regulatory decisions to enable NPAF sub-loans and IPPA to be onlent to sub-borrowers.
8/ The purpose of the ESP is to help put in place an integrated framework for assistance to privatized and new private enterprises including
debt, and equity financing, as well as technical assistance.
2/ At the project screening stage, any projects which would appear to meet ESP requirements will be offered to three ERP participating
banks. If no offer of finance is received in a reasonable time, the NPAF would continue to process the project according to its own
procedures.



The Project                                                                          49
4.56          The MOF will establish a dedicated NPAF revolving account (NPAF Account) into
which all debt service payments of sub-loans (interest, principal and other charges) will be deposited.
Sub-loan principal repayments will be used to fund additional sub-loans and will not be used to
amortize the Bank loan used to fund the NPAF. This will help ensure the sustainability of the NPAF
to finance pollution abatement projects. During negotiations, agreement was reached that the
proceeds from the NPAF Account will be used as follows: (i) to pay the interest on the portion
of the Bank loan used to fund the NPAF; (ii) to pay the operating expenses of the NPAF; and
(iii) of the remaining balance after deducting these charges, 30% will be transferred to the MOF
and 70% will be used to fund additional NPAF sub-loans. It was also agreed that as a condition
of disbursement for NPAF sub-loans and Investment Project Preparation Advances (IPPA), the
Borrower would enter into an arrangement with a bank to manage the NPAF Account who terms
and conditions are satisfactory to the Bank.
4.57         Operating Instructions would provide guidance to the NPAF consultants on detailed
operating procedures, including such matters, inter alia, as: (i) project eligibility criteria; (ii) project
approval and appraisal criteria, including the Bank's right to prior approval; (iii) staff responsibilities;
(iv) eligibility criteria for receiving assistance for project preparation from the Investment Project
Preparation Advance Facility (see para. 4.66); (v) cofinancing procedures; (vi) procurement
modalities; (vii) auditing and reporting requirements; (viii) the general terms and conditions of NPAF
sub-loans; and (ix) environmental guidelines and procedures. During negotiations, it was agreed that
adoption by the Borrower of Operating Instructions for the NPAF, satisfactory to the Bank, will
be a condition of loan disbursement for the NPAF component. In addition, it was agreed that
the Borrower would ensure that NPAF would operate pursuant to the Operating Instructions
satisfactory to the Bank.
4.58         Management and Staffing.  A small professional cadre of NPAF consultants,
including expert consultants with international project finance experience, would be retained by the
CPPI to manage the NPAF's activities, including project preparation and appraisal. The cadre of core
consultants would consist of: (i) a general manager; (ii) a chief financial officer and one professional
assistant who would be responsible for analyzing the financial merits of each proposed sub-project and
overseeing contract/project management and procurement matters; and (iii) a technical manager and
one professional assistant who would be responsible for evaluating and overseeing the technical
engineering and pollution abatement aspects of each proposed sub-loan. The staff would be authorized
to appraise projects and make recommendations to the Board only on the basis of a project's technical
and financial merits, in accordance with the rules and regulations specified in the Operating
Instructions. The General Manager would be expected to submit periodic audited financial statements
and activity reports to the Bank, other cofinanciers, and appropriate GOR authorities.  The
composition of the core consultant team as well as the duties of each team member were agreed
during negotiations. It was also agreed that the hiring of the core team of consultants for the
NPAF will be a condition of loan effectiveness.
4.59         To facilitate its operations, the CPPI would contract with: (i) legal consultants to
prepare draft loan agreements and handle other legal matters; and (ii) technical consultants to help the
core staff appraise the technical engineering and environmental aspects of proposed sub-loans.
4.60         Sub-Project Appraisal and Sub-Loan Approval Procedures. The NPAF will not
finance the installation of additional production equipment, or the expansion of existing production
facilities, but will focus instead on environmental clean-up sub-projects designed to capture
commercially valuable emissions from existing production facilities. Typical project concepts that
would likely qualify for NPAF financing are included in Annex 4.2. These include: (i) the collection
of dust containing high concentrations of copper and other toxic metals, and the recovery of copper



50                                                               Environmental Management Project
for export; (ii) collection and recovery of toxic/flammable hydrocarbon vapors from oil tank farms;
(iii) recovery of a copper concentrate from waste tailings; (iv) oil spill recovery; and (v) recovery of
metals from metal finishing wastes. A pipeline of sub-projects is currently being identified by
consultants who are working on the setting up of the NPAF.
4.61           The NPAF will finance only those sub-projects that have been prepared on the basis
of a comprehensive feasibility study prepared by the sponsors and subjected to a rigorous screening
and appraisal process by the core consultants and technical expert consultants hired from time to time
to assist with specific appraisal issues. A detailed diagram illustrating the sub-project approval cycle
is presented in Annex 4.4. Sub-project sponsors would be expected to submit a feasibility study
carried out in accordance with NPAF guidelines approved by the Bank. The study would indicate,
inter alia,: (i) project design; (ii) equipment specifications; (iii) engineering cost estimates; (iv)
implementation arrangements; (v) the proposed financing plan; (vi) detailed financial projections with
appropriate sensitivity analyses; (vii) the proposed legal structure of the borrowing entity; and (viii)
an environmental impact assessment.
4.62           During the appraisal of the feasibility study, the core consultants will review and
assess the sub-project's financial, environmental, legal and technical aspects.
i      The financial portion of the appraisal will, inter alia, (i) confirm the financial viability
of the proposed project; (ii) assess the portfolio risk posed by the project; (iii) ensure
that the proposed loan maturity is consistent with the projected pay-back period; and
(iv) examine the proposed cofinancing arrangements, including sponsors' equity,
export credits, and internal cash generation; (v) determine if the enterprise will be
able to repay its loan even in a period of high domestic inflation; and (vi) determine
whether the project might be eligible for financing under the ERP.
*      The legal portion of the appraisal will, inter alia, (i) confirm the legal status of the
borrowing entity; (ii) ensure that the legal entity is licensed to borrow and repay
foreign exchange; and (iii) examine the proposed security arrangements.
*      The technical portion of the proposed appraisal will, inter alia, (i) confirm that the
project is technically feasible, cost effective, and that no experimental technologies are
being applied; (ii) confirm that the benefits are achievable; (iii) establish that the
timescales are reasonable; and (iv) confirm that the procurement approach is
acceptable.
*      The environmental portion of the appraisal will, inter alia, (i) ensure that the project
has a beneficial impact on the quality of the natural environment compared with
existing baseline conditions; (ii) confirm that the project complies with prevailing rules
and regulations regarding emissions standards; (iii) determine that the proposed project
is consistent with the NPAF priority pollution abatement criteria; and (iv) ensure that
the project complies with existing Russian EA regulations and meets the Bank's EA
requirements.
4.63           In determining whether the sub-project meets the NPAF's pollution abatement criteria,
NPAF managers will evaluate such considerations as: (i) the location of the sub-project with respect
to population centers, sensitive local land uses including schools and hospitals, and the existing levels
and sources of pollution; (ii) the pollution category (air, surface water, groundwater, hazardous waste,
etc.); (iii) the scale of the pollution impact, including whether there are potential trans-boundary
effects, or only local or regional impacts; and (iv) the effects associated with the pollution, including



The Project                                                                        51
possible toxicity to human health, possible impact on climate change, and damage to natural
ecosystems and habitat; and (v) the environmental benefits of the sub-project under consideration
compared with the environmental benefits expected to accrue from other sub-projects currently in the
project pipeline or under appraisal. Draft feasibility study guidelines, sub-loan appraisal criteria,
environmental priority criteria, and Supervisory Board approval procedures were agreed during
negotiations and will be incorporated in the NPAF Operating Instructions.
4.64         If the appraisal indicates that the proposed sub-project satisfies the NPAF's
environmental, legal, financial, and technical criteria, the core consultants will recommend that the
Supervisory Board approve the sub-loan. Prior to consideration by the NPAF Supervisory Board, the
Advisory Panel for the Federal Environmental Policy Unit will advise the MEPNR as to whether a
proposed loan has acceptable environmental benefits and conforms to the NPAF's environmental
priorities. Their recommendation will constitute part of the official record of each sub-loan, but the
NPAF Supervisory Board will not be bound by these recommendations when deciding whether to
approve a particular sub-loan. Investment sub-projects approved by the NPAF Supervisory Board will
be submitted to the MOF, which will make a decision on sub-loan financing. NPAF core consultants
will monitor procurement and disbursement matters, supervise the sub-borrowers, and ensure that all
reporting and auditing requirements are fulfilled. During negotiations, agreement was reached with
the Borrower that, prior to the disbursement of Bank loan for NPAF sub-loans, it would ensure
that each NPAF sub-loan has been approved in accordance with the requirements agreed with
the Bank, including procedures, eligibility criteria, and terms and conditions for sub-loans.
4.65         Prior Bank Review. Until the Bank loan for the NPAF is fully disbursed, the Bank
will have two opportunities to review proposed sub-loans. First, the Bank will receive a summary of
all projects that have passed the initial screening review conducted by the NPAF consultants. Second,
before sub-loans are presented to the NPAF Supervisory Board, the Bank will have an opportunity to
review the project documents and indicate whether the NPAF should finance the proposed project.
If the Bank believes that the project should not receive a loan from the NPAF, the project would not
be presented to the Supervisory Board. Conversely, the Supervisory Board of Directors would not
be obliged to approve a project merely because the Bank have not expressed its objections. Details
of the Bank's prior review and approval responsibilities were agreed during negotiations.
4.66         Facility for Investment Project Preparation Advances. An IPPA Facility will be set
up with an initial funding of $5 million under the Bank loan. Donors have also expressed interest in
providing grant funds for investment project preparation and identification. Such funds, if available,
will be used prior to the drawdown of the Bank loan for this purpose.
4.67         A sub-borrower seeking an IPPA for a NPAF sub-loan will submit to the NPAF a
proposal, together with an application and a summary of the proposed sub-project. If the NPAF
management determines that the proposal for the IPPA meets the technical, environmental, financial,
and legal criteria, the proposal will be sent to the Bank, together with the initial review summary for
the proposed sub-project. Following the Bank's review and no-objection, and the NPAF Supervisory
Board or its appointee approval, the MOF will, based on the determination of the NPAF Supervisory
Board, make the final decision whether or not to approve the IPPA.  Each IPPA will: (i) be
denominated and be repayable (principle and interest) in US dollars; (ii) carry an interest rate
satisfactory to the Bank; (iii) be no more than $300,000; and (iv) contribute no more than eighty
percent of the total cost of preparation work and feasibility studies related to the proposed sub-project.
During negotiations, agreement was reached that prior to disbursing loan funds for IPPA, the
Borrower would ensure that each IPPA has been approved in accordance with the requirements
agreed with the Bank, including procedures, eligibility criteria, and terms and conditions for
advances.



52                                                          Environmental Management Project
4.68          Potential Cofinancing Arrangements. The NPAF has been designed to support two
distinct cofinancing arrangements. First, in order to reduce the NPAF's cost of funds and also to help
the NPAF obtain additional lending resources, bilateral and multilateral agencies have been asked to
help fund the NPAF with either grants or low interest loans. At a Donors Meeting on April 13, 1994,
the Swiss Government indicated its interest in providing SFI0 million of grant funds to capitalize the
NPAF.
4.69          In addition to capitalizing the NPAF, individual donors, export credit agencies, and
private lenders and investors could also cofinance specific sub-projects. Disbursement, procurement,
and reporting rules and procedures for this cofinancing modality will also be specified in advance. The
Bank is continuing to explore the possibility of mobilizing cofinancing via both modalities. The
federal, regional, and local environmental funds are also potential cofinancing sources for local costs.
4.70          On-lending Terms. Sub-loans from the NPAF will be denominated in US dollars.
Sub-borrowers will bear the US dollar exchange risk. The interest rate on NPAF loans would be
fixed for the life of the sub-loans. For the start-up phase of the NPAF, it has been agreed that the
on-lending rate would be equal to the World Bank lending rate in effect during the semester in which
the sub-loan'L' is approved plus a spread of approximately 400 basis points. In case of excess demand
for NPAF financing, rationing will be on the basis of environmental benefits. The minimum sub-loan
size will be $350,000 and the maximum sub-loan size will be $7,000,000. In order to ensure that
smaller enterprises have an opportunity to apply for and receive financing from the NPAF, efforts will
be made to publicize the existence of the NPAF within Russia. The availability of IPPAs should
enable small and medium enterprises to prepare projects for consideration by the NPAF. The NPAF
will finance no more than 70% of the total project cost and project sponsors will be expected to
finance at least 20% of the total project cost with equity. Where a proposed project has no existing
revenue stream, the NPAF will finance interest during construction, provided that the total loan
including interest during construction does not exceed the maximum loan size and financing percentage
specified above. The maximum loan term, including any grace period on principal repayments during
construction, will generally be eight years, including up to three years of grace.
4.71          In addition to the terms and conditions specified above, commercial projects will pay
an up-front appraisal fee equal to 1 % of the sub-loan amount, which can be financed from the sub-loan
proceeds, and a sub-loan supervision fee equal to 0.25% for the first five years and 0.125% for all
subsequent years.
4.72          Based on the assumptions detailed in Annex 4.1, the NPAF's forecast income
statement for the 17 year life of the Bank loan is summarized below in Table 4.1. The forecast
interest income amounts to $84 million over the 17 year period. Additional income via appraisal and
supervisory fees of $5.2 million should also be generated, bringing the NPAF's gross income during
the seven year period to $89.5 million. Interest and commitment fees on the portion of the Bank loan
used to fund the NPAF are projected at about $31 million over the 17 year period and operating
expenses would an additional $24 million over this period, comprised of operating losses of $1.5
million during the initial two year start-up phase and operating profits of $36 million during the latter
15 years. As agreed during negotiations, 30% of these operating profits will be transferred to the
Ministry of Finance; the remaining 70% will remain in the NPAF and be available to fund additional
sub-loans on the same terms and conditions. Interest on these reinvested earnings would boost the
NPAF's interest revenues by approximately $21 million over the 17 year period. Table A4.1 in
IO/This will be between the rate at which ESP funds are onlent to the commercial banks (i.e., 250 basis points above the weighted average
of EBRD and IBRD lending rates, for a cost of funds to the banks of approximately 9%) and the on-lending rate to their borrowers. The
commercial banks are expected to charge their borrowers between 14 and 15%.



The Project                                                                        53
Annex 4.1 indicates that the expected disbursement rate used in the financial projections is expected
to be close to the IBRD standard profiles for lines of credit.
4.73         Expected additional tax revenues. Commercially viable pollution abatement projects
financed by the NPAF will not only be able to repay the principal and interest on their NPAF loans,
but also will generate additional tax revenues for the Government. Depending on the extent of
cofinancing with other lenders, the amount of additional taxes generated during the life of the Bank
loan could be substantial and in excess of the amount of the loan borrowed from the Bank. An
example of such projects is included in Annex 4.3, in which a copper dust recovery project is analyzed
on the basis of a preliminary feasibility study results. This shows that for an investment of US$8
million (for which the NPAF may lend between US$2-4 million for procurement of foreign equipment
and technology), yearly tax revenues may be of the order of $2 million. Therefore, over the expected
10-15 year life of the project, some US$20-30 million of additional tax revenues would be generated
by this one project alone.
