5.     Center for Project Preparation and Implementation (US$5.5 million)
4.74         The CPPI has already been established as a separate non-commercial legal entity under
the MEPNR, which is responsible for appointing the CPPI's Board of Directors. An initial
organizational arrangement for the CPPI is presented in Annex 7.1. Both the Director and Deputy
Director have been hired using funds from the Project Preparation Facility advance for the project.
The principal role of the CPPI will be to facilitate the implementation of the Bank financed
components of the EFP, as well as GEF funded Project Preparation Advances and other projects
following Bank procurement, disbursement, accounting, auditing and reporting procedures and
requirements. Other important roles of the CPPI will be to: (i) provide overall project management
and coordination; (ii) disseminate information regarding Bank and donor financed EFP components;
(iii) liaise with other funding agencies; and (iv) facilitate training activities under the project. During
negotiations, agreement was reached that CPPI would maintain functions, staffimg, including
consultants, and other resources satisfactory to the Bank.
4.75          During the start up phase of the project, the CPPI will be assisted by a project
Management Advisory firm familiar with Bank procurement and disbursement procedures. The
Project Management Advisory firm will assist the CPPI in setting up and with the daily operation of:
financial and accounting systems; information and donor coordination system; procurement procedures
and systems; contracting management; and for the training of CPPI staff in the operation of these
systems. The services of this firm will be retained as long as it is needed for the efficient management
and operation of the CPPI. As shown in Annex 7.1 and 7.2, the procurement and disbursement
functions fall under the responsibility of the Deputy Director, who has already received Bank training
in these procedures. During the start-up phase, the CPPI will consist of six full time personnel,
increasing to eleven during implementation. The CPPI will be responsible for contracting all advisers
and experts at national and regional levels - both individuals and firms. The subcomponent
implementation team to which the adviser or expert is attached will be responsible for their selection
and technical activities, but contract administration will be by the CPPI. It will also be responsible
for equipment procurement in accordance with World Bank guidelines.
4.76         The CPPI also will be assisted by a General Consultant Firm (GCF), which will assist
and train subcomponent project managers during the start-up phase of the project, and will assist the
CPPI with project supervision and project performance and progress evaluation during implementation.
During the start-up phase, the GCF will assist the subcomponent project managers with the following:
finalizing subcomponent scopes of work; work plans and schedules; budgets; consultant terms of
references; terms of reference for members of subcomponent implementation teams; integration and
coordination of project subcomponents; providing project management training; and developing project
progress benchmarks for future evaluation. During the implementation phase, the GCF will carry out
independent tri-yearly reviews of project performance and progress against the development objectives
and schedules developed during appraisal and refined during the start-up phase. The Terms of
Reference for the General Consultant Firm is given in the Project Implementation Manual.
4.77         The CPPI will be responsible for all the project's financial aspects, including opening
and managing a Special Account, contract administration, contract payments, facilitating Letters of
Credit, arranging for international financing agency disbursements, setting up and managing accounts
and arranging for audits, as and when required. The CPPI will also be responsible for the Project
Completion Reports which will be required by the international financing agencies. Details of the
CPPI's activities in relation to project implementation are given in Section D below.



The Project                                                                                    57
C. Project Cost and Financing Plan
4.78           The estimated cost of the project is summarized in Table 4.3. The total cost of the
project is estimated at $194.8 million equivalent (including taxes and duties totalling about $3 million
equivalent), with a foreign exchange component of $91.9 million, or 47 percent of the total project
cost. A detailed breakdown of the cost estimates for the different components of the project is given
in Tables A.5.1 and A.5.2 of Annex 5.
4.79           As shown in Table 4.4, the Bank loan would finance $110 million, or 56 percent of
the total project cost. It would cover 100 percent of foreign exchange expenditures and 17 percent
of the estimated local costs ($18.1 million equivalent). The local costs that will be financed by the
Bank loan include non-recurrent incremental costs and recurrent incremental costs on a declining basis:
100 percent for expenditures incurred up to November 30, 1997 and 50 percent thereafter. Local
costs include hiring of local experts and institutes, subcomponent project managers, staff for
subcomponent implementation teams, and local expert advisors to these teams. Once the Project has
terminated, some of these teams will continue to function as newly created divisions in their
sponsoring comnmittees or ministries, which have agreed to provide for their continuing financial
support. Other teams, such as the CPPI, will be disbanded, or may spin-off as independent
consultants, as have been the case in other East European countries. The federal and regional agencies
responsible for overseeing project subcomponents will provide office and other facilities for these
teams at no cost to the project. However, these costs have been factored into the Government
contribution as "in-kind"L'' expenses.
4.80           The full financing plan for this project includes funding from the Government of
Russia (GOR) and local Russian commercial banks and enterprises of $84.8 million. These estimates
are based on a GOR contribution of $9.8 million as well as local cost financing for the projects
supported by the NPAF of $75 million, comprised of $25 million as equity contributions from
enterprises and $50 million financed by loans from banks or other sources to enterprises. The
Federal, Regional and Local Environment Funds that have been established as a result of the
introduction of a pollution fee collection system throughout the Russian Federation (see para 2.57)
could provide a part of the loan cofinancing of local costs in some instances. Enterprises may also
choose to finance their equity contributions through joint venture arrangements.
11/ In-kind expenses are estimated at $6.02 million over the expected life of the project, including: office space and facilities; staff of
government agencies (i.e., subcomponent management committees); laboratory and other support facilities; conference rooms, etc.

D.    Project Implementation
1.    Center for Project Preparation and Implementation
4.81         The Government has designated the MEPNR as the lead agency for the management
of the project and the coordination of its implementation. Because of the need to strengthen the project
preparation and implementation capacity of MEPNR, work began at an early stage of preparation of
the project with the setting up of the CPPI as a separate non-commercial legal entity governed by the
MEPNR. The Terms of Reference for the CPPI is provided in Annex 7.3. An advance from the
Bank's Project Preparation Facility was requested by the government to enable the early recruitment
and training of staff for this unit. The Charter of the CPPI is included in the Project Implementation
Manual.
4.82         Recruitment for CPPI has already begun and will take place in two phases as shown
in Annex 7.1 and Annex 7.2. In the first phase, the Director and Deputy Director of the CPPI have
been selected and a Project Management Advisory Firm has been hired using Bank consultant selection
procedures. Other CPPI staff are being hired as the work load increases. A draft copy of the CPPI
Director's contract and Terms of Reference for the Russian Project Management Advisory Firm are
contained in the Project Implementation Manual.
4.83         In the second phase, the remainder of the core CPPI management team would be
recruited, thereby increasing the staffing level to some 11 people, including support staff. This level
of staffing would be achieved by the time of loan effectiveness expected in December 1994.
4.84         As well as providing administrative support for the project as discussed above, the
Director of the CPPI is also designated as the overall project manager for EMP. As shown in the
organizational chart (Figure 4.2), the CPPI not only reports to the Minister (and the designated Deputy
Minister) of MEPNR, but also to a CPPI Supervisory Board made up of representative project
stakeholders from MEPNR and other Federal Government agencies. This is necessary because of the
distributed functions for environmental management across a number of government agencies.
4.85         Each TA subcomponent of EMP will be managed by a Russian subcomponent project
manager who reports to the CPPI Director, as well as to an EMP subcomponent management
committee made up of specific stakeholders relating to that subcomponent. Each subcomponent
project manager directs the work of a subcomponent implementation team of Russian experts. If the
subcomponent is being implemented at the regional level this team reports to a regional subcomponent
management committee, which is responsible for overall technical direction, coordination and
dissemination of the results to other regions.
4.86         A  framework agreement has been drawn up for each Subcomponent, and included
in the Project Implementation Manual, which spells out in detail the roles, responsibilities, and
commitments, as well as management and organizational arrangements that have been agreed between
the parties, which include MEPNR, CPPI and the federal and regional implementing agencies and
committees. These framework agreements will be formalized in the form of a binding legal agreement
among the parties.  Implementing resolutions of the parties to the subcomponent framework
agreements have been initialed by the relevant parties at federal and regional levels and a letter from
the Russian Government indicating that the resolutions will be adopted upon loan effectiveness has
been provided to the Bank.

2.    Project Implementation Schedule
4.87         Individual subcomponent implementation schedules are given in Annex 8. These
schedules indicate that the overall project is expected to be completed in approximately six years,
while some subcomponents may be completed within two to three years.  Since the Project
Management Advisory Firm has been hired and is setting up the procurement, financial and
information management systems in the CPPI, selection, contracting and recruitment of the General
Consultant Firm and the subcomponent project managers could be finalized by March 1995.
4.88         The General Consultant Firm could then begin the work of training and assisting the
Subcomponent Project Managers in initiating the project start-up activities prior to loan effectiveness
with a view to early selection and recruitment of consultants to assist the subcomponent project
implementation teams.
4.89         Prior to loan effectiveness, a Project Launch Workshop would be conducted with the
participation of the subcomponent project managers, the General Consultant Firm, the CPPI, MEPNR
and subcomponent management committee chairpersons, the Project Management Advisory Firmn and
the World Bank team. At this workshop, presentations and discussions would be held on procurement
and contracting processes and procedures, financial management matters, interlinking and coordination
of the technical aspects of the various subcomponents, supervision and reporting requirements, follow-
up workshops and seminars, links with the programs of other donors supporting the Environmental
Framework Program, etc. Follow-up coordination workshops including donor supported activities will
be held subsequently, on at least a yearly basis, so that participants can share experiences and leam
from the success and failures of others in the program.
4.90         A detailed Project Implementation Schedule will be established by CCPI to serve as
a baseline against which the overall project, its individual components and subcomponents, and the
integration across them can be evaluated in terms of the progress in meeting the project's objectives.
An overall project start up schedule will be developed by the CPPI Director and the General
Consultant Firm (GCF), in consultation with the CPPI Supervisory Board, component management
committees and the Bank. The milestones and deliverables envisioned as being appropriate for the
overall project start up implementation schedule are:
*     Subcomponent project manager appointments and training
*     Initial operation of the CPPI
*     Establishment of CPPI operational practices and procedures
Covering procurement, disbursements and administrative functions
*     Procurement of project support equipment (computers, office equipment, etc.
*     Overall project reporting procedures
4.91         Upon appointment of subcomponent project managers, this list of milestones and
deliverables will be expanded with the consultants advice and input to encompass the start-up activities
of component and subcomponent projects. Using the advice and assistance of the GCF, the SCPM's
and the CPPI Director will develop a start up project implementation schedule for each of these
subcomponent projects. The milestones and deliverables conceptually envisioned as being appropriate
for these schedules are:
*     Finalized subcomponent project descriptions and work plans
*     Final subcomponent project organization structures

4.92         For each subcomponent project, a detailed project implementation schedule will be
developed by the SPCMs and CPPI Director, with the advice and assistance of the GCF based on the
detailed work plans and finalized project descriptions noted above. The attached schedules in Annexes
8.1-8.4 list some of the major milestones and deliverables anticipated to be included in detailed
schedules for each subcomponent project. These are based on the conceptual project descriptions
developed by MEPNR and the Bank.
3.    Monitoring of Development Objectives
4.93         A list of specific development objectives has been drawn up for the project and are
included in Table 4.5. The extent to which these objectives will be achieved will be the basis for
judging the progress and performance of the project during implementation, and will be a focus of
Bank supervision missions. It is considered that this list of objectives will provide a sufficiently
comprehensive overview of project performance without being overly cumbersome to monitor and
evaluate. On the other hand, it is sufficiently specific to give a realistic evaluation of performance
and progress. These objectives are also referenced in the Project Benefits section below to identify
the principal project benefits.
E.    Procurement
4.94         General. All Bank-financed items will be procured in accordance with the Bank's
Guidelines. Table 4.6 summarizes the methods of procurement. The Project Procurement Plan
(Annex 9.1) details procurement administration, and contains procurement lists, notes, and schedules.
Donor financing of the EFP will be on a parallel basis and procurement of these items will follow the
donors' procurement procedures.
4.95         Technical Assistance. Implementation of the four technical assistance components of
EMP will require procurement of goods, the selection and employment of consulting firms and
individuals, and the employment of Russian staff. Assisted by the Project Management Advisory
Firm, the CPPI will undertake the procurement of all goods and will assist subcomponent management
committees, subcomponent implementing teams, and subcomponent project managers, with the
selection of consulting firms and individuals. For administrative purposes, all consulting firm
contracts over US$50,000 will be managed by the CPPI.
4.96         TechnicalAssistance Equipment. The equipment (totalling $10.43 million) comprises
mainly: (i) computers, peripherals and software (including computing equipment required for
scientific purposes, and for data base management); (ii) environmental monitoring equipment; and (iii)
office equipment required for the setting up and operation of the SITs. All contracts for goods over
$300,000 (estimated to total $7.60 million) will be awarded through International Competitive Bidding
(ICB) following the Bank's guidelines and using the Bank's standard bidding documents. Domestic
manufacturers competing under ICB would receive a preference in bid evaluation of 15% of the CIF
price, or the prevailing customs duty applicable to non-exempt importers, whichever is less, provided
the value added to the product exceeds 20% of the ex-factory bid price. To the extent possible, items
will be packaged suitable for procurement under ICB. For goods with an individual contract value
of no more than $300,000 up to an aggregate value of $ 2.33 million, procurement would be through
International Shopping; Local Shopping would apply for goods with contract value no more than



The Project                                                                        63
$10,000 each, up to an aggregate value of $0.50 million. All the goods contracts, with value at no
less than of $100,000 each, would be subject to prior review which would cover about 80% of the
total value of Bank-financed goods. The balance of the contracts would be subject to post review on
a selective basis by the Bank.
4.97         Technical Assistance Consulting Serices. Professional services (consulting services)
for technical assistance form a significant portion of project procurement ($39.98 million) and will be
provided by both firms and individual experts. The major consulting assignments and selection
schedules are given in Annex 9.3. A typical consultant selection schedule is given in the Project
Implementation Manual.
4.98         All consultants will be selected and employed following the World Bank's Guidelines
on the Use of Consultants and using the Bank's standard contract. Proposals will be invited from
shortlisted consulting firms for all assignments with an estimated value of no less than $100,000 each.
Individual consultants will be selected from an evaluation of the CVs of at least three candidates.
4.99         The Bank will require for its prior review, the terms of reference (TOR), the method
of selection, advertisements, letters of invitation, the proposed selection, and negotiated contract prior
to award for consultant services, except for contracts less than $100,000 each. However, this
exception to prior Bank review will not apply to TORs; single source selection of firms; employment
of individual consultants; or assignments of a critical nature.
4.100        National Pollution Abatement Facility. Most items and packages to be procured by
sub-borrowers will comprise industrial equipment, although the NPAF will also finance consulting
services for its own management and operation. NPAF financed goods (totalling $52.69 million) will
be procured by sub-borrowers following Bank procurement guidelines. NPAF sub-borrowers will use
ICB and Bank Standard Bidding Documents for all packages with greater than $2 million per contract;
domestic manufacturers would receive the margin of preference subject to the conditions noted above
(para 4.96). ICB contracts are estimated to total $19.0 million (including office equipment). All
packages with no more than $2 million per contract will be procured through International Shopping
on the basis of at least three quotations from suppliers in at least three countries, subject to an
aggregate ceiling of $33,190,000. Local Shopping based on three quotations will be used for packages
of no more than $10,000 each, subject to an aggregate ceiling of $500,000. The selection and
employment of consulting services (estimated at $4.19 million) will also follow the Bank's Consultant
Guidelines.
4.101        For each sub-loan, the Bank would review all documentation for procurement of goods
estimated to cost $300,000 or more per contract, i.e., the Bank would need to review and give its no
objections to the complete bid package prior to bids being invited (including the proposed
advertisement) and the proposed contract award with a detailed report covering bid opening and
evaluation. For consulting assignments, the Bank's review requirements would be as for other project
consulting assignments, as detailed above.

66                                                      Environmental Management Project
F.    Disbursement
4.102        The loan will finance for the NPAF, Technical Assistance Components and the CPPI:
(1) 100% of NPAF sub-loans and investment project preparation advances; (2) 100% of expenditures
of experts' services and training; (3) 100% of the foreign expenditures of imported goods, up to 100%
of the local expenditures (ex-factory costs), and up to 75% of the expenditures for other items
procured locally; (4) 100% of incremental recurrent costs for expenditures incurred up to November
30, 1997, and 50% thereafter; and (5) refinancing of the PPF (See Table 4.7).
4.103        The proceeds of the loan are expected to be disbursed in accordance with the
disbursement profile shown in Annex 6.3, over a period of six years, which is the same as the Bank's
average TA and financial intermediary profiles for all IBRD borrowers. The disbursement period was
determined after taking into account the advanced status of project design, the early setting up of the
CPPI with a PPF advance, and the commitment and willingness of agencies to be involved in the
project at all levels of government. Detailed loan disbursement tables are also given in Annexes 6.1
and 6.2.
4.104        The project is expected to be completed by October 31, 2000, and the loan closing
date would be June 30, 2001. Withdrawal applications will be fully documented, except for
expenditures against contracts valued at no more than US$50,000 equivalent, that will be made on the
basis of certified statements of expenditures (SOEs) detailing the individual transactions. The
documentation to support these expenditures will be retained by the CPPI and the NPAF for at least
one year after receipt by the Bank of the audit report for the year in which the last disbursement is
made. This documentation will be made available for review by the auditors (see sections below on
Financial Reporting and Auditing) and the Bank upon request.
4.105        To facilitate the disbursement of funds, a Special Account will be established in a
commercial bank acceptable to the Bank for the CPPI implemented TA components and the NPAF.
The authorized allocation to the Special Account will be $2 million, representing about four months
of average expenditures under the Loan. The account will be opened in the name of the Borrower and
will be administered by the authorized representative of the CPPI on behalf of the Borrower. The
CPPI will send on a monthly basis or more frequently, if necessary, to the Bank requests for
replenishment of the Special Account for the amounts transferred. In addition, monthly bank
statements of the special account that have been reconciled by the Borrower should accompany all
replenishment requests.

G.      Project Accounting, Financial Reporting, and Auditing
4.106            The CPPI will establish appropriate accounting systems to ensure timely and accurate
accounting of all transactions under project. The staffing of the CPPI with a financial adviser will
enable the project implementation agency to maintain all project accounts in accordance with
internationally accepted accounting practices. The Borrower will ensure that all the necessary
arrangements for proper accounting of receipts and payments (and replenishment) are complete before
the start of the Special Account's operations. The proposed accounting and financial reporting
procedures are outlined in Annex 6.2.
4.107            The annual financial statements and reports on the Project (including the Special
Account) would be audited in accordance with generally accepted auditing standards by independent
auditors (preferably an international accounting firm) acceptable to the Bank. The annual audit report
is to be submitted to the Bank by CPPI within four months after the end of each fiscal year.
H.      Project Supervision
4.108            As shown in Annex 10, the Supervision Plan for this project is designed on the basis
of three mutually supporting arrangements, including World Bank Headquarters and Resident Mission
staff, and a General Consultant Firm (GCF). This plan not only includes regular supervision
activities, but also incorporates assistance for the project start-up phase, a Project Launch Workshop,
18/ALL EXPENDITURES, EXCLUDING TAXES AND DUTIES.



68                                                       Environmental Management Project
and at least yearly Project Review workshops, to bring together the SCPM; SCMC Chairpersons;
CPPI Director, MEPNR and World Bank staff to learn from experiences and to make adjustments to
the program as required. Donors responsible for implementing components of EFP will also be
invited to attend.
4.109        Bank supervision missions would take place three times per year, with Resident
mission staff providing on-going support in between these missions.
4.110        The General Consultant Firm is expected to begin its work following Board
presentation. The GCF would employ one full time foreign project manager with technical expertise
in one of the subject areas of the project, at least for the first two years of its contract. This expert
would train and assist not only the CPPI Director as overall project manager, but would also work
with other foreign specialist advisors from the GCF in training and assisting the subcomponent project
managers during project start-up and implementation. The detailed knowledge of the project that the
GCF would gain during project start-up will greatly enhance their ability and effectiveness in
providing continuing advise to the Russian SCPMs for supervision of the project as a whole. These
consultants also will draw up an overall training plan for the project which will be updated at regular
intervals. As shown in Annex 10, it is expected that the GCF would provide approximately 100
consultant months of start-up and supervision support to the Project over its four-year life. The GCF
would be responsible for independently auditing the quality of the work being performed by other
Russian and foreign experts on the various subcomponents, as well as for evaluating project
performance and progress in meeting its designated objectives and planned schedules. They would
prepare tri-yearly supervision reports for evaluation by MEPNR and the Bank, and would assist the
CPPI in preparing the final Implementation Completion Report.
I.    Environmental Assessment
4.111        The project is consistent with Bank policy and follows accepted Bank EA procedures;
it has been assigned a "B" rating for EA purposes. In conformity with Bank practice detailed
environmental guidelines and procedures have been drawn up for subproject environmental appraisal
including: (i) environmental selection criteria evaluation; (ii) environmental assessment; and (iii)
environmental liability auditing as required. In addition, sub-borrowers from the NPAF will be
responsible for ensuring that the proposed investment complies with all applicable environmental
assessment rules, regulations, and procedures of the Russian Federation. Efforts are currently
underway to harmonize the Russian Federation and World Bank Environmental Assessment guidelines.
This should help to minimize compliance difficulties and speed the approval process. Annex 4.5
provides details on the proposed guidelines and procedures to be established by the NPAF
Management to meet both Russia Government and World Bank EA requirements.
J.    Benefits and Risks
1.    Project Benefits
4.112        The specific benefits that are expected to accrue as a result of this project are as
follows:
(1)   the establishment of national databases and standardized methodologies and systems
for the collection and verification of environmental health and hazardous waste
generation data for policymakers at the national and regional levels;
(2)   the upgrading and adoption of overall environmental action plans based on



The Project                                                                        69
environmental health priorities at the national level and in the Upper Volga and Urals
regions, and the first phase implementation of these plans to test and demonstrate their
application;
(3)   the development and Phase I implementation and adoption of specific action plans
for water quality and resource management in the Upper Volga, Urals and North
Caucasus Regions, and sectoral environmental action and Phase I implementation
plans for:  (a) iron and steel; (b) non-ferrous metals; (c) oil refining and
petrochemicals; (d) basic organic and inorganic chemicals; and (e) agriculture with
particular attention to intensive livestock operations;
(4)   the formulation and adoption of improved regulatory systems and enforcement
mechanisms for water quality and water resource management in the Upper Volga
and North Caucasus regions; for hazardous waste management in the Upper Volga
region and for environmental assessment at the national level;
(5)   the dissemination of the experience and the results of project financed activities to
other regions in the Russian Federation through a national outreach program;
(6)   the establishment and management of a national facility to fund investment in
pollution abatement and improved resource utilization and recovery. The effectiveness
of this Facility will be judged on the basis of the amount of private loan and equity
funds it is able to leverage for pollution abatement, the extent of pollution reduction
achieved through its investments and its ability to address pollution priorities identified
under items (2) and (3) above;
(7)   the improved organization, management and training of staff of environmental
regulatory agencies at the federal level and in the Upper Volga, the Urals, and the
North Caucasus regions; and
(8)   facilitating donor participation in the environmental sector in Russia through the
establishment of a Center for Project Preparation and Implementation and donor
involvement in project workshops.
2.    Project Risks
4.113        The main risks that the project may face include: (1) the fragmented decision making
process for environmental management at the federal and regional levels; (2) the shortage of
experienced and qualified Russian specialists in environmental economics and law; (3) the poor
environmental regulation enforcement capability of the legal system in Russia; (4) conflicting mandates
and lack of cooperation between government agencies and even between departments within the same
agency; (5) possible delays in project implementation due to a lack of familiarity with Bank procedures
and the requirements of project management in general; (6) difficulties in arranging local cost
financing for pollution abatement; and (7) potential adverse impacts of high inflation on projects
financed by the NPAF. The project will build on initiatives already being introduced by the
Government in order to address these issues. Firstly, at the federal level, the project will be under
the direction of the recently formed Federal Commission on the Environment (with MEPNR acting
as the Secretariat of this Commnission), which has been set up to improve inter-agency cooperation and
decision making for environmental management. The regional project areas were selected on the basis
of already existing arrangements for promoting inter-agency cooperation for environmental



70                                                         Environmental Management Project
management and their governments strong commitments to environmental quality improvement
(usually because of strong local community pressures). The project proposes an early start to a
comprehensive training program to improve skills in environmental policy and regulation, as well as
in the technical areas of environmental epidemiology, hazardous waste, water quality management and
project management. The use of twinning arrangements with foreign institutes will ensure practical
application in the development of these skills. Trained personnel will also be delegated to work at
regional, as well federal level to transfer their skills. In the participating regions, cooperative
arrangements for pollution abatement at major polluting enterprises will be negotiated with appropriate
incentive arrangements for compliance with agreed permit schedules.
4.114         To minimize risks of project implementation delays, framework agreements have been
reached with federal and regional agencies that will be responsible for project implementation laying
out in detail the responsibilities and arrangements for component implementation. These agreements
have been initialed by the respective parties and will be formalized as binding resolutions after Board
approval. The early setting up and staffing of the CPPI and recruitment of a Project Management
Advisory Firm to assist the CPPI with the setting up of procurement, financial and information
management systems has been designated to reduce risks of implementation delays. The NPAF will
be especially equipped to assist borrowers to put together packages for project financing including a
full array of financing arrangements from local and foreign sources. One of the criteria on which the
NPAF operations will be judged will be its ability to leverage other sources of financing. IPPAs
would enable enterprises to develop high quality projects to the stage where they meet the requirement
for NPAF project financing. The pollution abatement projects that will be targeted by the NPAF have
a number of inherent characteristics that will protect them from the impacts of high inflation, namely:
(i) recovery of valuable resources from waste streams with zero or negative costs; and (ii) end
products that have a high economic value in world markets and are exportable.






STAFF APPRAISAL REPORT

ENVIRONNTAL MANAGEMENT PROJECT
V. AGREEMENTS REACHED AND RECOMMENDATION
5.1          During negotiations, agreements were reached with the Borrower that it would carry
out, or cause to carry out, the following actions:
(a)    adopt legal and regulatory decisions to enable NPAF sub-loans and IPPA to be onlent
to the sub-borrowers (para. 4.55 );
(b)    utilize the proceeds of the NPAF Account in accordance with the agreement with the
Bank (para 4.56);
(c)    ensure that NPAF would operate pursuant to the Operating Instructions satisfactory
to the Bank (para. 4.57 ); and
(d)   the CPPI would maintain functions, staffing, including consultants, and other
resources satisfactory to the Bank (para.4.74 ).
5.2          For loan effectiveness, the following conditions will apply:
(a)    adoption by the borrower of a resolution and other actions acceptable to the Bank
necessary to implement the NPAF component (Para 4.55); and
(b)   the hiring of a core team of consultants for the NPAF (Para 4.58).
5.3          There are three conditions of disbursement for the NPAF Component:
(a)    an arrangement with a bank, for administration of the sub-loans and management of
the NPAF account, are to be entered into by the Borrower, in a manner acceptable
to the Bank (Para 4.56);
(b)   the Operating Instructions for the NPAF are to be adopted by the Borrower in a
manner acceptable to the Bank (Para 4.57); and
(c)    each NPAF sub-loan or IPPA has been approved in accordance with the requirements
agreed with the Bank (paras. 4.64 and 4.67).
5.4          Subject to agreement on the above, the Environmental Management Project is suitable
for a Bank loan of $110 million equivalent to the Russian Federation at the Bank's standard variable
interest rate, for a period of 17 years, including a grace period of 5 years.
