Annex 2                                                                             73
STAEl APPRAISAL REPORT
RUSS&N FEDERATION
ENVIRONMENTAL MANAGEMENT PROJECr
ANNEX 2
DESCRIPTION OF THE COMPONENTS OF THE ENVIRONMENTAL FRAMEWORK
PROGRAM NOT FUNDED BY THE WORLD BANK
A.    Human Resources and Training Subcomponent
1.    Russian professionals have had little opportunity to develop analytical, managerial and
entrepreneurial skills, having previously worked in what were rigidly structured and segmented
governmental entities. There is a need to encourage cross-disciplinary thinking, creative speculation,
and intellectual risk taking in dealing with policy and strategy issues in the environmental and
resources management field. To develop such skills, and to break down the resistance to debating
issues outside the expert's field, role playing and case studies will be used. The focus will be on
encouraging policy debate and examining strategy alternatives in an atmosphere of intellectual
openness. The objective of this subcomponent is to develop suitable training courses and to train
trainers, as well as to develop the capacity of Russian institutions to continue the ongoing training
courses.
2.    The level of education in the physical and biological sciences is impressive in the FSU; and
there is a valuable reservoir of technical talent within the MEPNR and its regional committees.
However, based on the training needs assessment, four subject areas have been identified to receive
priority:
*     policy, programming and decision-making at the federal and regional levels;
*     operational proficiency and managerial capabilities within the Ministry;
*     environmental assessment and enforcement capabilities of the Ministry and its
territorial bodies; and
*     public education, participation and conflict resolution.
3.    An important part of the training program is to build an informal network of Environmental
Management Training Centers throughout the Russian Federation. The Centers would develop, adapt
and deliver short-term training activities (from three-day to two-week sessions) for the following
audiences: all levels of governments, representatives of industry, private consulting firms, and NGOs.
The first center would be based in Moscow. The Moscow center would initially support other centers
located in several different geographic regions. Ultimately, all of the centers would be independent
and self-supporting, sharing information, workshop materials and training experiences. In addition to
delivering training sessions in Moscow, the Moscow Center would coordinate the dissemination of
training materials, provide technical assistance, and sponsor national conferences for representatives
of the other training centers. As the Academy of Management in Moscow has the best training
facilities and Moscow State University has access to the greatest diversity of expertise, these
institutions seem to be the best initial candidates for forming a consortium, in which one or the other
has lead responsibility.



74                                                       Environmental Management Project
4.    The Human Resources and Training Subcomponent will assist in the setting up of these
training centers and in developing the training programs and delivery mechanisms. It will also fund
the start-up costs of the centers while they become self-supporting.
B.    Regional and Local Demonstration Pilot Projects
5.    Four local demonstration institutional and policy development pilot projects are proposed under
the framework program in the following regions and cities:
*     Khabarovsk Krai
*     Nizhnii Tagil
*     Novokuznetsk
*     Murmansk Oblast
6.    Khabarovsk Krai Demonstration Pilot Project. Khabarovsk Krai in Eastern Siberia is
extremely well-endowed with many natural resources. Prominent among these are nonferrous metals,
as well as forest resources. The Government expects a considerable increase in tin, gold and silver
mining and refining activity, with active involvement by foreign companies. Since this industry has
enormous potential for major environmental degradation, the following three activities are proposed
for the local pilot program:
*     Support for the Krai in developing environmental regulations and legislation by
establishing twinning relationships with a similar local government in an OECD
country;
*     Improving the enforcement capability of Khabarovsk's Committee of Environment
through the training of environmental inspectors in the techniques and principles of
environmental enforcement; and
3     Development of a comprehensive sectoral environmental action plan (SEAP) for the
Krai mining sector.
7.    Nizhnii Tagil Demonstration Pilot Program. Nizhnii Tagil is an industrial city of 438,000
people located on the eastern slopes of the Ural Mountains, about 150 km north of the oblast capital
of Ekaterenburg. Founded in 1722 on the site of a large copper deposit (now exhausted), it has long
been a major center for mining, metallurgy, and manufacturing. A demonstration project in Nizhnii
Tagil would take advantage of the comparative advantages offered by Ekaterenburg and other sites in
Sverdlovsk Oblast. Ekaterenburg has a strong institutional infrastructure, that can support training and
other activities requiring local staff and financing. It is expected that as the project develops,
opportunities will be identified for identifying smaller activities within the overall framework.
Four principal activities have been identified:
*     Environmental planning and priority setting,including the development of a local
environmental action plan (LEAP) for the city of Nizhnii Tagil;
*     Environmental education, involving parents, teachers and citizens in a community-
wide curriculum development process for primary and secondary school children,
which could eventually be adopted at the National level.



Annex 2                                                                             75
*     Environmental technology transfer and cooperation, through the application of
specially targeted environmental audits, demonstration of low-cost innovative
technologies and improved management practices, setting up of an international
business center in Ekaterinburg to improve access to information on low waste
technologies and practices, and training.
*     Strengthen environmental institutional arrangements in Nizhnii Tagil to improve
environmental management capabilities of the city authorities.
8.    Novokuznetsk Demonstration Pilot Project. Novokuznetzk is located in southeast of Western
Siberia, and is situated in the southern part of Kemerovo Oblast, at the center of the Kuzbass
urbanized region that covers about 12,000 square kilometers. The population in this area is
approximately 1.2 million, of which about 600,000 live in Novokuznetsk. Novokuznetsk is
characterized by large coal deposits that are shaft-mined (city center) and open pit-mined (north of
the city). The city has 120 industrial enterprises, of which metallurgy accounts for about 68 percent
and coal mining about 13 percent of production. Novokuznetsk has been named one of the most
heavily polluted cities in the Russian Federation.
9.    An integrated approach to addressing environmental problems has been adopted in
Novokuznetsk as reflected in the documentation prepared for a local environmental action program
(LEAP). The LEAP provides details of: (1) environmental problems by media and sources; (2) public
health impacts; (3) a proposed 10-year program of investment to reduce pollutant emissions and
loadings by enterprise (public and private); (4) operations and plants to be shut down and
reconstructed; (5) an initial cost-benefit analysis to justify the proposed investment pattern; and (6)
an initial calculation of how environmental investments accounted for in the plan can be supported
with enterprise internal cash flows and government (public) funding. A set of appendixes has also been
provided that contains additional technical information, including engineering diagrams and
specifications related to specific enterprise investments that should be reviewed by foreign technical
specialists, including environmental and process engineers.
10.   Two principal activities are proposed:
*     Strengthening of the Local Environmental Action Program. Novokuznetsk authorities
have already made considerable progress toward developing a local environmental
action plan supported by all social and economic groups, and with assistance from the
Weldon Foundation has linked up through a sister city arrangement with the City of
Pittsburgh in the United States. However, the local authorities are well aware that the
Action Plan only partially reflects the environmental policy and institutional options
that need to be addressed, and have requested bilateral assistance in filling these gaps.
*     Local and Overseas Training. Training will be provided for: (a) specialists located
at the various institutes in Novo Kuznetsk which already provide environmental
training; (b) municipal planning and administrative staff; (c) personnel of polluting
enterprises; and (d) NGO personnel. The objectives of this subcomponent are to
strengthen the environmental skills and performance of key local government decision
makers, to raise awareness of environmental issues and enable them to solve the
technical problems in a cost effective way.



76                                                         Environmental Management Project
11.   Mumansk Oblast Demonstration Pilot Program. Mumansk Oblast on the Kola Peninsula
covers an area of 145,000 kmn2 adjacent to Norway and Finland. The total population is 1,098,000
with 472,000 in the capital city of Mammansk. Industry represents 80 percent of the GNP of the
region with 40 percent directly relating to the exploitation of natural resources, especially minerals
and timber. Extensive pollution is caused by two large nickel/copper smelting complexes in Nikel -
Zapolyarny and Monchegovsk, with severe health and ecological impacts. The activities proposed
under this project can be grouped as follows:
*     Improvement of the environmental institutional arrangements at the Oblast level
including: environmental information systems, emission permitting and enforcement
systems; policy formulation and implementation; organizational arrangements;
development of least cost investment options for pollution control;
*     Preparation of a forest master plan, which will include sustainable forest
management practices and production methods; forest protection and multiple use;
institutional strengthening of forest organizations; and small scale forest industries.
*     Improved environmental management practices in selected enterprises through the
preparation of environmental management plans, preparation of feasibilities studies
investment planning and preparation of investment proposals. In screening these
projects, Ministry officials would try to identify projects that (1) are financially viable,
(2) have financial and engineering feasibility studies already underway, (3) will have
a cost-effective, material impact on pollution abatement; and (4) might be able to
attract foreign partners and foreign financial support.
C.    Air Quality Management Component
12.   Overview. Air quality management (AQM) in Russia is oriented toward control of stationery
sources. Oversight of the process is provided centrally by MEPNR and responsibilities for
implementation of the system are delegated to the oblasts, rions, and cities. Since 60 percent of the
pollution fee revenue is kept by the local Committee of Environmental Protection, their role in air
quality management is very strong and they appear to operate with considerable autonomy.
13.   The Russian system of air quality management consists of the following key features: use of
air quality norms (standards) to set targets for the ambient concentration of over 1000 pollutants; air
quality monitoring and emission inventories to help establish emission limits for these pollutants;
approvals for land; and establishment of maximum permissible emission (MPE) rates for enterprises
and of sanitary protection zones (SPZs) around these enterprises where concentrations of one or more
of these pollutants may be exceeded; and development of an ecological passport that defines emission
limits and pollution fees for emitting pollutants, or consuming natural resources. The legal foundation
for implementing this system is evolving, but the basic analytical framework for AQM has been in
use for some time.
14.   Problems and solutions. The current Russian AQM system seeks to ensure that emissions of
pollutants from an enterprise do not cause the maximum pollutant concentrations (MPCs) to be
exceeded outside of the SPZ, around that enterprise. The system focuses on individual sources and
their immediate surroundings, rather than on the collective impacts of all of the sources of a given
pollutant in the area, and this focus on individual sources can inhibit identification of the least-cost
solutions to an area's air quality problems.



Annex 2                                                                              77
15.   Russia's AQM program currently is too complex and cumbersome to enable sources to react
to changes in the world marketplace. It needs to be simplified and streamnlined so that sources can
adapt to changing markets and attempt innovative modifications to products and production methods.
Industrial enterprises could then attain the norms in their most cost-effective manner. The system also
needs to be able to deal effectively with uncooperative managers of industrial concerns
16.   There are several specific aspects of the Russian AQM system that should be improved if the
system is to work efficiently and be more cost effective. These are: (1) reduce the large number of
pollutants (over 1000) for which norms have been developed; 2) simplify the "synergy" concept that
attempts to consider the combined effect of pollutants on health; 3) establish a "de minimis" concept
for regulating emissions or making process changes; 4) modify the legal system to facilitate
enforcement of the control strategies and provide for redress by the regulator, sources or citizens; 5)
update the outmoded technology used for ambient and source measurements and the data systems in
which measurement information are stored; (6) refocus the current system to enable Russian regulators
to manage the collective impacts of all of the sources in an area; and (7) address the transport of
pollutants into neighboring countries and global air issues.
17.   Pilot program. Many cities in Russia have significant air pollution problems, with pollutant
concentrations that are over 10 times the MPC level. These high levels are generally caused by
concentrations of large industrial enterprises (for instance, metallurgical plants, petrochemicals, and
oil refining). The MEPNR have identified a number of highly polluted areas that are of particular
interest for siting a pilot program to upgrade the AQM system.
18.   Volgograd was selected for several reasons: (1) the area contains a wide and diverse mixture
of industrial sources and although the urbanized area is long and narrow (stretching 70 kilometers
along the Volga River), there are several distinct clusters of sources that can be used either separately
or together to demonstrate potential changes to the AQM system; (2) the area is relatively accessible
by rail and air; (3) the local government, including the Mayor's office and the City's Environmental
Services Administration are anxious to cooperate; (4) the industries are actively seeking joint ventures
and other forms of financial support for restructuring antiquated production process; (5) the City
Environmental Services Administration appears to be quite capable and their leader is dynamic and
enthusiastic about the program; (6) while Volgograd's air quality problems are not the most severe
in Russia, they are serious, especially in the summer; and (7) there is a strong NGO community in
Volgograd that is linked to local environmental management through representation on both elected
governing bodies and advisory committees.
19.   Russian Air Management Program (RAMP). The US EPA Office of Air Quality Planning
and Standards has agreed to manage a Russian air management program (RAMP) for Volgograd to
demonstrate the potential changes to the Russian system of air quality management for the MEPNR
and to assist them in making those changes Russia-wide. All components of the AQM system will be
demonstrated in the pilot area (for example, ambient monitoring, emissions measurement, emissions
inventory methodology, data management, air quality modeling, and control strategy development at
the least cost to the enterprises in the area).
20.   Oversight of the RAMP will be through the formation of a RAMP management team. The
management team will consist of the following: US EPA Program Manager (coordinating US EPA
staff and contractors); MEPNR Program Manager (coordinating Ministry staff, other Russian Federal
Government groups, and contractors employed by the Ministry); Director of the Scientific Research
Institute of Atmospheric Air Protection (SRIAAP) in St. Petersburg; and Chairperson of Volgograd
City Environmental Services Administration (ESA), (representing the city government, local NGOs,



78                                                       Environmental Management Project
the Volgograd Scientific Ecological Soviet, the Volgograd Oblast Committee for Ecology and Nature
Protection, contractors employed by the City for this study, and local enterprises).
21.   The management team will be made up of AQM experts and will be responsible for managing
the program and making specific policy recommendations. The various RAMP teams also would
include substantial involvement of US EPA technical and air policy staff (Office of Air Quality
Planning and Standards),  consultants (both Russian and foreign), and Russian environmental
engineers, scientists, economists and planners representing MEPNR and the Volgograd Oblast and
City staffs.
22.   The objectives of RAMP are to: characterize air quality in the Volgograd area using modern
monitoring methodologies; improve AQM principles, policies, and compliance methods; improve the
Russian Federation's technical approach to solving air quality problems and develop a preliminary
least-cost control strategy in Volgograd as a demonstration; and work with the MEPNR and key
oblasts to make similar improvements throughout Russia.
23.   RAMP is a comprehensive project that will continue for four to five years, and is structured
in the following phases:
*     Phase I. Evaluate existing air quality and emissions information and identify priorities
for developing environmental action plans. This should include a preliminary
evaluation of ambient and source measurement methods and the emission inventory.
*     Phase II. Beginning with Volgograd, strengthen the Russia-wide air quality
management capabilities and institutions. This would include: (1) assisting in
rationalizing the Russian system of air quality norms, reviewing and improving the
emission inventory methods, source test methods, and emission factors; (2) reviewing
and revising atmospheric dispersion modeling, air quality monitoring, and siting and
quality assurance methods, and developing a long-term plan for creating an interactive
data storage system; (3) improving and revising the system for regulating new and
existing sources and source modifications; (4) working with local, Federal and oblast
officials Russia-wide to disseminate information and help make changes to the Russian
system; and (5) developing methods for assessing and managing pollutant transport
and transboundary/global air pollution problems through local and regional control
programs.
*     Phase III. Upgrade Volgograd's air pollution monitoring, assessment and planning
capabilities and information collection and processing. This would include: (1) the
upgrade of laboratory and field measurement capabilities and monitoring procedures
for ambient air and source emissions; and (2) the source testing, saturation
monitoring, and receptor and airshed modeling.
D.    Water Quality and Water Resource Management Component
24.   The Russian Federation has developed an extensive system for water quality management at
the central and regional levels including a national monitoring system of over 2200 stations with
regional available laboratory services, a regular and systematic program with detailed methodologies
for river basin water balances and preparation of master plans, and central and regional institutional



Annex 2                                                                               79
infrastructure for waste discharge regulation and permitting. Nevertheless, this system has not been
successful at preventing a serious decline in water quality in the country's rivers, lakes, and
reservoirs, and major ecological damage in some areas.
25.   Issues. A number of areas have been identified where methodological, procedural, and
infrastructure improvements in the regulatory and discharge permitting system would have a significant
impact, including developing cost-effective local monitoring systems to support permitting and
enforcement; strengthening local laboratory services; developing an effective national quality assurance
program; and establishing a more appropriate and flexible system of standards. However, the critical
issues are the lack of effective incentives to ensure compliance, of awareness of the value of water,
and of what will be required to improve the situation; and lack of commitment on the part of the
public and local officials and enterprise managers to take action. Where these latter constraints do not
exist and there is local commitment to change, there has already been notable progress.
26.    The existing monitoring system, for example, was developed for a different set of goals and
conditions from those that exist today. The present system was designed to provide a basis for river
basin water balances and overall indication of the trends in water quantity and quality, not to support
local management of water quantity or quality. There is great reliance on an apparently very weak
system of self-monitoring.
27.   The operation of local infrastructure of water supply, distribution, and treatment has been less
effective than would be possible with current design and control system practice, and effective
maintenance. The inability to adequately protect water sources has become an important issue as
inappropriate land uses and economic activities have expanded in the watershed areas of major water
supply sources. The current institutional, regulatory, and financial system appears unable to cope with
this serious trend.
28.   The sector is also profoundly affected by the political and economic changes that are taking
place. There is increased uncertainty about the legal framework, caused by frequent changes in
leadership and laws that makes effective regulation and management nearly impossible. More
profound, however, are the changes in thinking and perception that must be developed and
accommodated in the current system of water quality and quantity management being brought about
by the shifting roles of the central Ministries, Republics, and Oblasts especially with the emergence
of local autonomy and decision-making, the introduction of private property and the privatization of
industry, the introduction of more realistic prices, the introduction of water charges, and the effort
to enforce the "polluter pays" principal. These changes, for example, require fundamental changes in
many of the assumptions that underlay the methods and processes used in water resources planning
and management. The present system both from a methodological and institutional point of view has
tended to fragment responsibility for water quantity and quality management and to create barriers to
the effective introduction of environmental considerations in decision-making.
29.    Water resources planning. The Committee for Water Resources Management is responsible
for preparation of the federal plans for management and protection of water resources, and the river
basin plans. On the basis of these plans, local water agencies develop water management guidelines
for smaller rivers and their basins. All these planning efforts concem both water quantity and quality.
At present all plans are made for the horizon of 2010, and in accordance with the former practice they
are supposed to be updated every five years. In the FSU, similar plans served as a basis for the
centrally decided (subject to negotiations) resource allocation among different republics. Allocation
quotas have been defined for three water availability scenarios-dry, normal and wet years.



80                                                       Environmental Management Project
30.   The past planning approaches and methodology are currently being adjusted to the new
economic and political situation. The work is being done mostly by SOYUZVODPROJECT, which
is a research arm of the Committee for Water Resources Management. The ongoing efforts address
three principal issues: (1) what are the best ways for incorporation of the appropriate environmental
and ecological criteria; 2) how to allocate water among over 80 territorial bodies of the Federation
who in several cases are already involved in bilateral or multi-lateral negotiations over the use of
shared water resources, and (3) how to combine so far dominating central allocation principles with
the introduction of economic incentives (water as an economic good). Although allocation by price
mechanisms is not a viable proposition under the existing economic situation, it is very likely that local
water markets will be emerging (trade in water armong autonomous republics, oblasts, or between
different categories of water users, for example, irrigation and municipalities).
31.   Project priorities, strategies and objectives. A framework for the water quality management
component of the Environmental Management Project is outlined in Table A3.3.1. The priorities for
environmental management in the water sector identified by MEPNR and the Committee of Water
Resources Management include:
*     Ensure the availability and supply of safe drinking water;
3     Restore and maintain the ecological health of rivers and other water ; and
*     Promote water conservation and strengthen integrated water quality and quantity
management.
32.   The strategy and objectives (Annex 3.3 ) reflect the issues identified above and discussed at
length with the key agencies in the sector. Hence the strategy for ensuring the availability and supply
of safe drinking water focuses on protecting sources of supply, including reducing pollution loads,
expanding the sources of supply, improving the effectiveness of drinking water treatment. The third
priority focuses on water conservation and strengthening integrated water resource management, and
the corresponding strategy will focus on economic incentives, institutional strengthening, and building
greater public awareness.
33.   Pilot Projects. The specific objectives outlined in Annex 3.3 will be addressed under the
Program through pilot projects implemented at the oblast or watershed level in different regions. These
projects will provide an opportunity to develop, test, adapt, and demonstrate new methods and
procedures, train personnel and develop training materials, and prepare and implement integrated
water quality management action plans. The pilot projects will also address both immediate and long-
term needs. Criteria for identification and selection of pilot projects and a preliminary list of regions
where the pilot projects could be located, were agreed with the MEPNR during pre-appraisal. The
criteria strongly focus on determining the interest and commitment of local people and organizations
to participate in and support the project, local problems and priorities, the availability of data and
information to support a pilot project, and the importance and relevance of the problems to the
framework in Table A3.3. 1. The following were finally selected for inclusion in the pilot program.
*     Komi Republic, including the cities of Syktyvkar and Vorkuta;
*     Kostroma and Yaroslav oblasts in the Upper Volga River basin, including the cities
of Kostrama, Yaroslavl and Rybinsk, and the village of Borok;
*     Tver, Smolensk, and Moscow Oblasts in the Upper Volga and Moscow River basins,
including the city of Konakovo, and the Ivankovskaya and Istranskaya Reservoirs;
*     Rostov Oblast and the Lower Don River basin; and
*     Sverdlovsk Oblast, including the cities of Nizhnii Tagil and Ekaterenburg.



Annex 2                                                                              81
34.   A list of 14 activities is given in Table A2. 1. While some of the activities deal with specific
problems that recur in many areas and municipalities, a strong desire was expressed in every region
for the preparation of a coherent and integrated water management action plan; for efforts to
strengthen and improve management, particularly the development of new tools and strategies for
permitting and enforcement; for the demonstration of new, low-cost technology; and for the
implementation of new management actions as a part of the pilot projects. A clear understanding of
the need to examine and strengthen the inter-relations among quality and quantity issues was widely
felt. Both preventive and curative actions were stressed by local officials.
35.   These findings are reflected in the proposal for the preparation of an integrated action plan
in every region (Table A2.1). Since there are important differences and variations in individual
proposals that reflect local conditions and opportunities, they will collectively represent a large body
of experience that can be useful to many other regions and areas of Russia and the Republics of the
FSU.
36.   The widely recognized need to focus on improving the effectiveness of managing small
watersheds, and to introduce new technologies that can be used by individual enterprises, farms, and
villages to protect and manage their water and land resources is reflected in the activities that focus
on the management of small watersheds and the demonstration of practical, low-cost technologies (one
in the Lower Don River basin, and one in the watersheds that are the source of drinking water for
Moscow City).
37.   A single pilot activity is proposed to improve monitoring, although it was observed that
present systems need restructuring and improvement in all the regions visited. The proposed pilot will
focus on the Moscow water supply watershed system in the Upper Volga and Moscow River basins.
Under the pilot, new monitoring concepts in relation to network design, equipment, and operation will
be formulated to serve the needs for emergency warning, trend analysis, and water resources
management. The objective is to develop and test new cost-effective and affordable approaches to
strengthen and improve the existing system in support of these three purposes.
38.   The need to upgrade present laboratories and field sampling methods, equipment and capability
was also identified and a pilot activity with Moscow Drinking Water Service System has been included
to this effect. A quality assurance program essential to the delivery of reliable comparable, and usable
environmental data will also be implemented under this activity.
39.   Four of the remaining seven pilot projects deal specifically with municipal water supply issues;
and the last three deal with strengthening permitting systems, improving industrial pre-treatment
controls and industrial environmental audits. Two of the drinking water pilot projects (located in
Sverdlovsk Oblast), will focus on building effective local and oblast institutions for drinking water
quality management, and develop action plans to improve and protect sources of water supply. A third
pilot (located in Rybinsk) will focus on water conservation; while the fourth will address special
problems and needs of small municipalities in dealing with their technical mess.
E.    Hazardous Waste Management Component -
Hazardous Waste Environmental Management Studies
40.   There are three subcomponent of the Hazardous Waste Management Component, two of which
are being funded under EMP and are described in Annex 3.4.



82                                                         Environmental Management Project
41.   The third subcomponent project included within the Hazardous Waste Component involves
undertaking up to six waste related environmental management studies within specific enterprises, each
considered representative of key industrial sectors. These studies are intended to be financed by donor
participants in the Framework Program.
42.   These enterprise specific projects have been jointly identified and assessed by MEPNR and
the IBRD missions. In each case, the work will be directed to comprehensive environmental
management studies, utilizing waste audit techniques as demonstrations. The enterprises have been
selected on the basis that they are in need of assistance in relation to current and potential
environmental impacts related to hazardous waste, they are representative and viable enterprises in the
long term, and their management appears committed to improvement of current practice.
43.   In addition, they represent candidates for clean technology and resource recovery investments
which can enhance productivity, profitability and export potential through application of waste
minimization and resource recovery technology in implementing improved waste management
practices. Therefore, these studies are designed to assist the particular enterprises involved in
qualifying for financing from the National Pollution Abatement Facility (NPAF), whenever
appropriate. The scope of the study work undertaken will include the identification of key investments
for technological change and/or infrastructure additions, evaluation of the economic and environmental
enhancements that these may offer, and their documentation in the form of creditable business plans
and financing proposals.
44.   This subcomponent project addresses the MEPNRs priority to carry out projects in selected
enterprises and industrial sectors in various parts of the country to enable the transfer and adaptation
of modern approaches and technologies especially designed to reduce public exposure to hazardous
waste, minimize creation of new waste, and reduce the volume of existing stored waste.
45.   The individual studies and implementation plans will provide accurate data on waste quantities
and characteristics; waste storage, transport, processing, and disposal practices; and the environment
in which waste generation and transport takes place. This information will also be useful for the core
Hazardous Waste Management Component subcomponent projects involving the development of a
national industrial waste data management system, and the development of regulatory management
capability at the regional level.
46.   These Environmental Management Studies will also provide information on the process
technologies and raw materials used in producing products and which generate waste.  The
multidisciplinary skills of enterprise employees, Institute and industry association experts, as well as
the emerging Russian environmental consulting community and foreign experts, will be brought to
bear to identify changes in current operating practices, as well as options for applying pollution
prevention techniques and technologies to improve the effectiveness and efficiency of waste
management practices. Building ongoing capability for performing environmental management studies
in the Russian Federation is an integral part of this project. Means for building this capability in
carrying out the project, as well as ways to sustain an ongoing capability in the Russian Federation
are important benefits to be derived from it.
47.   The following provides a brief overview of the six enterprise specific projects identified within
this subcomponent project:



Annex 2                                                                               83
1. PRESSA Publishing House - Moscow
48.    This enterprise is one of the largest printing operations in Europe, producing a complete range
of publications from newspapers, books to specialty printing products. It employs a wide range of
technology, much of it involving significant air and waste water emissions. These include discharges
of caustic materials, volatile and soluble organic solvents, and large volumes of heavy metals,
particularly lead, chromium and zinc. It recognizes that to avoid increasing pollution for assessments
and to be in a position to service broader markets, substantial changes in practice will be required.
A number of initiatives are proposed for evaluation, based on current practice in western countries.
These include ink replacements, expanded use of electronic printing technology, metals and organic
material recovery from air and waste water, and employment of "closed loop" water management
systems.
2. Tula Cartridge Works - Tula
49.   This enterprise has been a major defense industry that is now a joint stock company and which
has converted approximately 80 percent of its production capacity from munitions to consumer and
industrial products, such as vacuum cleaners, tableware, valves, chains, bellows, and photographic
equipment. It is a major generator of metal finishing wastes, notably those from electroplating, as are
a number of other similar enterprises in the area. Recognizing both the environmental impact, of
these waste discharges and the potential to develop a service capability for the region's industry, they
have identified the possibility to develop a commercial treatment facility that would service a sizeable
regional market, which would recover heavy metals from these wastes.
3. Lakokraska Company - Yaroslavl
50.   This enterprise is the largest paint and coatings producer in Russia, being the major supplier
of consumer and industrial paints and pigments. Traditionally, the technology employed has been
based on metals bearing materials and solvent based formulations, many of which are falling out of
use in the west for environmental reasons and associated regulatory restrictions. The change over of
this basic industry to the use of more environmentally friendly technologies such as powder (dry) and
water based paints would enhance this enterprises' export potential as well as that of its industrial
customers.
4. Jaroslavlpetroliumstintez Oil Refinery Group - Yaroslavl
51.   This industrial group operates several large oil refinery complexes in Yaroslavl and represents
the major refined products supplier for the region, as well as a significant exporter. This operation
is directing attention to resource recovery of substantial volumes of hydrocarbon residuals currently
stored in an extensive lagoon system, which represents a significant environmental hazard. The
specific study would focus on developing an integrated system of managing these and other waste
streams with an emphasis on being able to utilize them for refined product production, or as waste
derived fuel, potentially in the local centralized heating plants.
5. Cherepovetz Integrated Steel Complex - Cherepovetz
52.   This enterprise is the largest integrated steel works in European Russia, with approximately
13 million tons of installed capacity. It employs a range of technology including older open hearth
furnaces and an extensive coking operation. While the most common impact of the operation relates
to air emissions, something that is largely associated with the status of this area as priority for



84                                                        Environmental Management Project
environmental concern, the complex also produces a wide range of liquid and solid wastes that are
subject to land disposal and surface water discharge. The proposed study would be directed at
providing an overall environmental management plan which would provide for improved waste water
treatment, water recirculation and waste management practice upgrading.  All are considered
fundamental to maintaining the long term viability of the complex.
6. Novomosvosk Chemical Complex - Tula Oblast
53.   A group of four large organic and inorganic chemical enterprises is centered in this region.
They have been identified as an environmental priority by regional and national authorities. Specific
concerns include surface and ground water contamination, air impacts, and damage to national heritage
resources. The proposed study work would focus on developing environmentally sound alternatives
to current practices, particularly deep well injection, large scale use of waste water lagoons, and
landfilling of hazardous solid waste. Specific initiatives that could be examined include organic
chemical removal and reuse of process water, materials recovery from spent catalysts, and improved
thermal treatment and waste derived fuel usage.
F.    Biodiversity Conservation and Natural Resource Management Component
54.   Russia has relatively abundant renewable and non-renewable resources. In the past, to attain
development targets (for instance, industrial growth rates or an increase in foreign exchange earnings),
these resources were exploited under the assumption that there was a limitless supply . Natural
resource management (NRM) was never a consideration, and the environmental consequences were
not assessed in a systematic way. This is now changing.
55.   Public pressure is forcing emphasis on NRM and conservation of biodiversity. However, in
the search for new sources of growth and comparative advantage, there is strong pressure for federal,
regional and local governments to open access and to accelerate the use rates of natural resources even
to the extent of resource depletion and environmental degradation. This is being done in response to
short-term economic pressure.
56.   The objective of the proposed program is to strengthen Government capacity to manage its
natural resources, including biodiversity in a sustainable way. The project will assist Government in
three main areas.
57.   These activities will assist Government to assess trade-offs between development and natural
resource use at the national and regional levels, establish institutional arrangements for improved
NRM, identify relevant policies and investments to minimize environmental degradation while
improving welfare in the economy, and assess the extent to which sustainability of development in
many rural areas in the feasible objective. The program of assistance consists of these subcomponents:
(a) Biodiversity Strategic Overview and Action Plan; (b) Nature Protection Program; and (c) Lake
Baikal Program.
1. Biodiversity Strategic Overview and Action Plan (US$2.6 million)
58.   The conservation and management of biodiversity has become central to the Russian
Federation policy-making process. There is an increasing awareness among decision-makers that
economic reforms have (and will continue to have) a significant influence on how natural resources
crucial to biodiversity are allocated and used. In addition, decision-makers have also recognized that



Annex 2                                                                                 85
the systematic evaluation of biodiversity at the national level will require a wider and more explicit
national consensus, (e.g., objectives and instruments).
59.    The formulation of a National Biodiversity Strategy (NBS) in its role as both a document and
as a process, represents the manner in which this consensus ought to be reached. The strategy will
touch on issues and concerns linked to nation-wide policies, investments and institutions; assess the
state of those natural resources that are central to conservation and management; and present a strategy
and action plan. As a process, the elaboration of the strategy will assist in the development of the
necessary institutional arrangements (e.g., organizations, laws and regulations) to reflect the cross-
sectoral and multi-dimensional nature of biodiversity management at the national, regional and local
levels.
60.    The strategy will take into account the sectoral and inter-sectoral lirkages, and will present
an assessment focusing on the technical, economic, social and institutional aspects of the development
process. A topology of situations will be developed and analyzed for a wide spectrum of areas, from
those designated as protected to those that are heavily used and have major significance for
biodiversity. Special emphasis will be given to NRM and its impacts on biodiversity within the
development context of the Russian Federation. The Biodiversity Action Plan will establish and will
outline actions to be taken in the short, medium and long term.
61.    Biodiversity Strategy. The National Biodiversity Strategy (NBS) will have a program of
actions that will include:
*      a detailed assessment of how socio-economic policies affect the conservation of
management of biodiversity at the international, national, regional and local levels;
*      an institutional framework to adequately formulate a sound legal foundation, and to
allocate, use (in relation to carrying capacity), manage, and regulate natural resource
use;
*      an identification and evaluation of the principal elements of the strategy, specifying
the main criteria for policy and investment selection;
*      a proposal for the reconciliation of the needs of local communities with the needs to
protect the environment; and
*      a detailed action plan.
62.    Biodiversity Data Base System. The preparation of the National Biodiversity Strategy, as well
as the whole host of programs suggested in the Action Plan will require adequate information and
data. Today there is plenty of information available on the state of natural resources, which because
of an ineffective institutional structure, is not accessible to policy makers. This is due to at least two
factors: absence of networks whereby the scientific community can participate in decision-making,
and lack of adequate reporting systems that are suitable and user friendly for policy makers. In this
respect, the Government has requested assistance to indicate the necessary mechanisms for a database
system on biodiversity. It is expected that the data system will continue performing its role after the
biodiversity strategy is prepared within the 18 months of this program.
63.    Economic Evaluation of Biodiversity. Given the scarcity of public and private funds, there
is no doubt that biodiversity programs will be scrutinized very thoroughly at the national and regional



86                                                          Environmental Management Project
levels. Central to this scrutiny is the financial and economic valuation of biodiversity investment and
policy reforms. These will be subject to benefit-cost analysis and the Government has requested
assistance to prepare guidelines on this matter.
64.    Workshop on Biodiversity Economics. The Government has recognized the need to put
together a workshop dealing specifically with the economic aspects of biodiversity conservation and
management. A central aspect of that workshop will be to deal with the impact of policy and
investment decision making on biodiversity and establish the basis for future action plan and
investment reviews. In addition, the workshop will be instrumental in providing the first step in
formulation of the national biodiversity strategy. The workshop will bring together scientists, policy-
makers and donors and establish the key elements of the future policy dialogue in the area of
biodiversity and economic development. The terms of reference for the biodiversity strategy and the
original outline will be submitted to this workshop for review and approval. In this regard, the
workshop will also serve as the first public form for the working group on biodiversity that will be
in charge of defying the national strategy.
2. Nature Protection Sub-Component
65.    The principal objectives of this subcomponent are to:
*      Strengthen the institutional arrangements by which protected areas are governed and
administered through the review and reform of the administrative and organizational
structures and processes, i.e., an Institutional Support Program. This necessarily has
to include the development of a strategic framework for strengthening the entire
protected area network in Russia. In addition, it would identify "gaps" in the system
and thereby initiate the basis for the future development of Russia's protected area
systems.
i      Develop an ongoing training program and facilities for protected area administrators
and managers through which the skills and knowledge necessary for effective and
efficient protected area planning and management will be upgraded and simultaneously
dispersed throughout Russia.
*      Sustain the existing federal protected areas through the immediate provision of
carefully selected requirements that are essential for natural resource protection (i.e.,
an Immediate Operations Assistance Program).
66.   Institutional Program.  This sub-component will assist the Government in a legislative,
regulatory and policy review as well as an organizational and efficiency review of the system of
protected areas. Specifically to:
*      assist the Ministry to draft the supporting regulatory and field management polices to
implement the new legislation. The study would identify the inconsistencies, areas
in which policies are at cross purposes or contradictory, and design new policy
instruments to fill the identified gaps (including mechanisms to finance the on-going
management of the system and develop coordinated strategic programs for
environmental education and interpretation);



Annex 2                                                                                   87
*      conduct an appraisal of the actual roles of the existing protected area designations
based on principles of landscape ecology and conservation biology, and develop a
desired vision for all protected areas as part of an identified system as above;
*      undertake a review of existing administrative and organizational structures and
processes, including staffing, particularly at the site level, to improve upon their
efficiency and effectiveness.
67.    Training Program.  The development of training facilities and appropriate curricula in
protected area planning and management will result in tangible, transferable, and long-term benefits.
Several tasks will be implemented including:
*      the development of a training program comprised of several modules to enhance, and
disperse the skills and knowledge that are essential for improving the planning and
management of protected areas. This will require an initial study which should
address in detail, matters such as:  specific training needs (at entry, skill and
professional development levels), numbers and qualifications and sources of
instructions personnel, logistical requirements, instruction methods, numbers and
positions of personnel to attend, internal costs, times of training, and training
evaluation procedures:
support for priority training needs; and
the establishment of training centers at locations to be determined, either in a
Regional, or national context following the assessment of needs from the study above.
68.    Immediate Assistance Program. The immediate operations assistance program will enable
a rapid improvement in the protection and operation of the existing protected areas by alleviating key
constraints to their effective management and underpin the system of reserves in the short term. To
accomplish this, a range of immediate investments is required to upgrade their current management
capability. These investments include:
*      Provision of essential means of transport (boats, patrol and heavy goods vehicles),
communications (computer fax/modems including software, E-mail and radio
systems), office equipment and resource protection tools that are crucial for effective
operations and management. Currently, these essential management resources are
either non-existent, inadequate or broken down.
*      Provision of computer equipment and software is essential for improving information
management and decision-making, and thus for the effective management of protected
areas. A wealth of information exists on many of the protected areas. Much of this
information, however, is currently unwieldy. Equipment is lacking and a number of
sites suffer from a shortage of electricity for lack of access to transmission lines or
lack of functioning diesel generators. This activity constrains operational efficiency
and scope. Procurement of generators and fuel supply will be provided.
*      Provision and/or upgrading of key infrastructure, such as small headquarters
buildings, is required for protected area management and operations. At present,
almost without exception, the existing infrastructure is severely deficient in terms of
meeting the basic standards and requirements for effective operations and



88                                                          Environmental Management Project
management.   Consequently, it is critically important to invest in essential
infrastructure in the most needy protected areas.
69.    All the activities in the immediate assistance sub-component have been reviewed with senior
Russian protected area administrators and managers and have been subjected to field verification. The
criteria used for evaluating the requirements of the protected areas included an assessment of
pressures, biodiversity values, management effectiveness, the state of existing infrastructure, transport
and communications, and income generation capabilities.
3. Lake Baikal Program
70.    While the Strategic Overview formulates the general policy, the Regional Program enables its
acceptance and provides an invaluable trial of its implementation by working through inter-regional,
regional and immediate financial assistance components.
71.    Lake Baikal of itself provides compelling reasons for assistance, while fulfilling the criteria
for the regional pilot natural resource management action plan. It has an outstanding representation
of species, many of which are endemic and threatened, contains 20 percent of the world's fresh-water,
includes three Zapovedniki (two of which are biosphere Reserves) and two National Parks, has a
significant industrial, urban and tourist sector and has suffered the consequences of deleterious
regional agricultural and forestry management. It is of such vital international importance that it
requires immediate support even under the Immediate Assistance Program described above. The
project will address institutional, ethnic and social impacts of biodiversity in the two regions of Irkutsk
and Chita and the Republic of Buriatia. It represents an unique opportunity to test alternative
organizational arrangements at the regional and local levels.
72.   It provides an initial model of integrated natural resources management with the integration
of social, economic, institutional, and sectorial issues.  This is central to any future nature
protection/environment project and is particularly relevant in view of the inter-agency and inter-
ministerial coordination that will be required. It will be a testing ground for the identification, design
and evaluation of the regulatory environment confronting biodiversity and nature protection activities.
Regulation at the regional level is evolving and many of the implementation mechanisms are either
ad hoc or relatively new. The principal vehicle for integration will be land-use programs and it is of
critical importance to gauge how effective such a vehicle will prove, especially in relation to the
accompanying economic instruments.
73.    The Lake Baikal Program considers three major areas of intervention: inter-regional, regional
(individual oblast/republic) level, and immediate assistance for sustainable development. Each has
multiple components which are presented below.
74.   Interventions on the Inter-Regional Level. The project includes five interventions to improve
natural resource management at the inter-regional level. These are:
0      Natural Resource Management Strategy. Formulation and implementation of a
strategy for the region, considering the ecological, economic, social and institutional
aspects in managing of natural resources. The strategy will pay attention to resource-
based problems, i.e., using natural resources as the unit of analysis, and to the
sectorial and cross-sectorial issues, constraints and options. Use will be made of
existing studies and experiences (e.g., land use planning). Policies, investments and
institutional reforms will be selected within the format of an action plan that would



Annex 2                                                                                            89
benefit all regions involved.  The strategy will be central to the attainment of
sustainable development, particularly as it relates to land and water based
ecosystems'9'.
*      Assessment of Sources of Growth and Comparative Advantage.  Given the
development imperatives facing the regions and the challenges associated with the
conservation and management of natural resources, an assessment of the real
comparative advantage of the Lake Baikal region will be undertaken. The main
difference with traditional assessments in this area will be its emphasis on the proper
valuation of natural resources involved in potentially viable economic activities (e.g.,
the full opportunity cost of each resource involved). The assessment will also be
instrumental in defining a system of regional accounts that will include the full set of
values of natural resources and goods. Options and alternatives will be evaluated and
disseminated through workshops and seminars. Existing public investment and
expenditure plans will be revised in light of the main conclusions of this assessment.
*      Information Systems and Monitoring and Evaluation.  Proper management of
natural resources requires adequate information and data for decision making.
Experience demonstrates that the "quality of entry" of many projects and programs
is at present very low and thus, their probability of success rather slim. While there
appears to be no shortage of data and information about the natural systems prevailing
in the Lake Baikal region (including information on relevant economic/social
indicators of development performance), there are significant institutional constraints;
i.e., the way in which data flow to decision-makers (quality, timing, format). The
project will assist in overcoming those constraints.
e      Environmental Assessment:  Guidelines and Procedures.  There are different
environmental standards applied across different regions, with enforcement
mechanisms and procedures which are clearly inadequate. Significant differentials in
environmental standards create notable changes in resource allocation and the rate at
which renewable and non-renewable resources are being consumed. Specifically,
some areas could become pollution heavens or the center of major depletion of such
resources as soil and forests. Because of the potentially damaging effects of resource
misallocations, e.g., expansion of polluting industries where the standards are more
lax and of the inevitable ecological and economic linkages across regions (through
common watersheds and river basins, shared underground water aquifers, air pollution
and acid rain) a major harmonization of environmental standards is needed. In
addition, environmental guidelines must be issued and applied at the level of
development planning where investments, policies and institutional reforms are chosen
to attain sustainable developmentm'
*      Institutional Development, Education  and  Training.   The development and
enhancement of institutions is greatly needed if natural resource management is to be
improved.   Increased institutional absorptive capacity will take several forms,
including the development of legal frameworks and institutions, e.g., property rights,
19/ This project will pay particular attention to fisheries development and management and to wildlife management and hunting. These are
two aras of significant ecological and economical importance in the Lake Baikal region.
20/ The project will give special attention to mining methods and land reclamation issues as a result of mining operations. The guidelines and
procedures should explicitly include these reclamation activities given the proximity of profitable mines to the Lake Baikal shores.



90                                                         Environmental Management Project
rules and regulations, the establishment of the necessary organizational arrangements
for the successful implementation of development activities and the creation of the
necessary mechanisms for the participation and consultation of communities, NGOs
and indigenous people. Central to this process is the enhancement of human capital
at all levels, through education and training programs. These programs should be
tailored for different groups and needs. In some cases, public awareness-related
discussions will be recommended. In other cases, needs may be associated to the
comprehensive development of curricula on the subject matter of natural resource
management -- from primary school to the end of the university education.
75.   Interventions at the Regional Level. The interventions presented below will be implemented
in Irkutsk, Chita and the Republic of Buritia. The framework and implementation approaches of these
interventions will be similar for each region. However, the detailed design and the setting of
implementation priorities will respond to the conditions in each region. In all cases, the main
objective will be to implement the proposed pilot operations, test approaches, consolidate and
disseminate (i.e., through an adequate extension service) those natural resource interventions that are
effective and sustainable, and to establish the foundation for future expansions into more sizable
investments and institutional development programs. The project includes three pilot programs of
critical regional importance. These programs are assigned to the following areas:
*     Fire Forest Prevention. Forest fires have proven to be one of the major problems
in forest management during the summer (harvest) season. It has been estimated that
4.5 million hectares are destroyed every year in Siberia. Fire hazard is the number
one problem in the Baikal region and, consequently, the prevention of forest fires has
become a top regional priority. The pilot operations will promote emergency
investments into fire prevention and fire fighting in high priority areas. A local fire
specialist will be identified and supported (training and operation) during the course
of project implementation.
There are several other forestry related initiatives that this project is prepared to
finance. These initiatives will include: as institutional improvements; policy analysis
and change; and technology improvements. In the area of technology improvement
technical assistance will be allocated to areas such as wood production, harvesting,
transport and processing. The up-grading of information systems will also be
considered.
*     Watershed Management and Sustainable Development. The regional governments
have given special attention to the development of watershed management programs
that are characterized by environmental fragility, poverty, or both. The development
challenges are particularly acute in several areas, and ecological sustainable
interventions need to be found. The pilot programs will cover several development
and management dimensions, including agriculture, forestry, irrigation, and livestock
development. On the resource management side, these programs will pay particular
attention to soil and water management, pasture improvement, changes in agricultural
practices, soil erosion control and sedimentation management, and the like. The
selection of the watersheds has been such that the replicability effects may be
maximized in the future.
*     Ecotourism Development. The regional governments have all given high priority to
ecotourism. This priority has been justified on ecological, economic and social



Annex 2                                                                               91
grounds. While some forms of ecotourism are taking place near the Lake Baikal, it
remains to be demonstrated that this activity is profitable and sustainable in the longer
term. Funds for project preparation have already been allocated, and it is expected
that the major question surrounding a significant expansion of ecotourism activities
will be addressed in detail to avoid misallocation of scarce resources or the creation
of ecological and social stress in the Lake Baikal region.
0     Information Systems for Selected Project Components.  Information and
monitoring and evaluation systems will be designed for a selected number of activities
included in the protected areas and Lake Baikal sub-components. With regard to the
Lake Baikal Program, the emphasis will be on watershed management, forestry
inventories, and fisheries management. The scope and content of those efforts will
be directly proportional to their pilot scale. Expansion of that scale will be considered
as a second stage in the process of project implementations.
*     Immediate Assistance for Sustainable Development. A special component will be
created to benefit natural resource management related activities, particularly those
directly linked to traditional systems, communities, women groups, NGOs and
indigenous groups. These activities will strengthen NRM at all levels. The allocation
of financial resources available from this component will be carried out following five
main categories. These are:
*     Community Development Activities. To promote such activities as "backyard
farming", livestock raising or harvesting of minor forest products, e.g.,
berries, nuts, honey. All of them are influencing the management of such
resources as forest, land and vegetation.
*     NGOs' Development and Conservation Programs.  Examples of these
activities are tree planting programs, fisheries management and aquaculture,
and soil conservation.
*     NRM  Activities and Indigenous People. Enhancement of NRM  in areas
where most indigenous people live, based on traditional and indigenous
approaches and practices in NRM. Examples include the preservation and
effective utilization of medicinal plants and livestock raising and rearing in
areas where soil erosion rates are relatively high.
*     Policy and Applied Research in NRM. Funds will be allocated into the
research community, i.e., natural science research as well as social science
research, to bring practical and proven methods and procedures into public
policy making.
*     Short Term  Training.   International Consulting  and Private Sector
Development. Funds will be allocated for short term training courses or
consultants in such areas as Benefit-Cost Analysis of Environmental Goods
and Services, and consultation on specialized issues regarding public
expenditures and investment reviews.
76.   While it is not possible to specify all the necessary criteria for selection of projects to be
funded by the component, several of these are worth noting: the funds are to be allocated to tasks and



92                                                         Environmental Management Project
activities that are definitely linked to natural resources and environmental management; the funds
should be approved, based on a written statement of a task or a project for the Baikal region. A copy
of a written statement of tasks to be financed from the component must be supplied to the Bank; the
use of consultants (local and international) must be explicitly acknowledged; the funds should not be
used disproportionately for a few activities.
77.   However, because of the experimental nature of the Immediate Assistance for Sustainable
Development component, several activities have been already earmarked for implementation, such as
emergency financing in the areas of fishing and wildlife management; research on medicinal plants
that will, in turn, enhance the Indo-Tibetan Medicine Program in the Republic of Buritia; and
environmental-ecological extension servicing the watersheds; short and medium term credit for
agriculture and land management related purposes; investment cum technical assistance program for
backyard farming "cottage" industries in the Ust Barguizin area; and community based programs
managed or executed by NGOs.
G.    Cultural and Natural Heritage Conservation and Management
78.   The Conservation and Management of Cultural and Natural Heritage (MCNH) are an
important national objective of GOR, and the ability to attain that objective depends significantly on
economic social and environmental policies/reforms in other sectors of the economy. It is this set of
linkages that justifies the incorporation of a MCNH component within the Overall Framework TA
Program. Other framework activities with potentially significant linkages with the MCNH are: air
quality management, water quality management, hazardous waste management and policy and
regulatory support.
79.   In the midst of the great changes that are taking place in Russia today, cultural and natural
heritage is increasingly threatened by lack of adequate funding, human resources and management.
Several policies oriented towards growth and development have profound effects on the government's
and community's ability to preserve the cultural and natural heritage of the country. These effects are
transmitted through important changes in land use, air and water quality, budget allocation at the
national and regional levels, and the like. It is, therefore, opportune to assist the government in the
preparation of a national and regional framework and in the implementation of a set of priority
activities to ensure conservation and a better utilization of cultural and natural heritage.
80.   The proposed MCNH  component will consider cultural and natural heritage in the
environmental management context. The program will emphasize major linkages between policies,
investments and institutional reforms to enhance environmental and natural resource management and
the conservation and management of cultural assets. Within this context, initial activities will review
the current situation of cultural heritage and identify the major issues confronting cultural and natural
heritage conservation. Based on this analysis, the MCNH component will assess the main policy
options and their implications and provide a preliminary assessment of financial and technical
assistance needs. It would aim at strengthening existing programs, initiating new pilot activities, and
laying the foundations for later reforms.
81.   The MCNH component will include three major areas: (i) Policy and Regulatory Support;
(ii) Institution Building and Strengthening of Management and Implementation Mechanisms; and (iii)
a National Fund for MCNH.
82.   Policy and Regulatory Support. This activity will assess present policies that affect the
conservation and maintenance of cultural and natural heritage assets.  Chief among these are



Annex 2                                                                                      93
environmental policies in regard to air, water and natural resource protection, the siting of industry,
and waste disposal. Examples of other policies with direct impacts on heritage are land use in urban
and rural areas, building control, budgetary and fiscal policies with regard to resources allocated to
research institutes, museums, historic structures, and archaeological, natural and sacred sites, and
institutional roles and relationships.
83.    It is clear that in Russia today there are key policy and institutional areas where adjustments
are needed in order to create a climate conducive to adequate protection of the heritage: (i) the legal
and regulatory framnework; (ii) the management of heritage assets; (iii) institutional coordination; and
(iv) management of public finances. The component project will aim to make phased improvements
in each of these areas.
84.    The legal system should provide a set of rules that govern the protection of cultural and natural
property and property rights and their exchange. Some of the areas for legislative review and revision
include land use planning and control, building standards, municipal zoning laws, protected areas and
nature reserves which include cultural heritage assets, and regulations concerning the export of and
trade in antiquities.
85.    InstitutionBuilding and Strengthening of Management and Implementation. Consistent with
the main thrust of the framnework program, this activity will emphasize management as a central
function in the conservation of cultural and natural heritage. Management in this instance will be
mainly concerned with the allocation, use, maintenance, control, regulation, and sustainability of
cultural assets. To strengthen the management fuinctions and component will assist in the formulation
of a national strategy; assessment of existing programs and needs at the national, regional and local
levels; harmonization of existing preservation and management standards; and development of
informnation systems and monitoring and evaluation.
86.    In addition, the component would support pilot projects and training activities. The pilot
projects would be chosen to represent thematic and structural typologies. Among these would be:
(i) the management of archaeological sites, including site interpretation and presentation; (ii) the
conservation of historic wooden structures; (iii) climate control and collection management in
museums; and (iv) guidelines for the reuse of important historic structures.
87.    The analysis of investment and TA needs would lead to the preparation of selected studies and
training courses. These would include:
*      studies to design a nation-wide inventory of cultural heritage, aiming at maximum
efficiency and user-friendliness;
*      training programs for Russian staff in site management;
*      studies to modernize and strengthen the financial capabilities of Russian institutions
responsible for safeguarding her-itage; and
*      studies and training to strengthen local goverrnments and public administration to plan
for heritage conservation.
88.    National Fund for the MCNH.  The fund will constitute the instrument through which
inmportant cultural heritage and natural conservation and management activities will be financed.



94                                                         Environmental Management Project
These activities may be pilot operations, the implementation of inter-regional programs and inter-
regional cooperation, or the financing of community-based activities.
89.   The rules and regulations defining the allocation, use, control, and audit of funds would be
consistent with funds set up under other World Bank projects.
