G.    Foreign Consultant Qualifications
33.    The Foreign Consultant's team  including subcontractors would include expertise in
environmental epidemiology and statistics, community and pediatric health issues, environmental and
health economics, occupational health, survey design and implementation, database management, and
geographical information systems.
34.    Since environmental conditions and policy choices differ greatly from  those that are
characteristic in OECD countries, substantial experience in industrializing countries, or in formerly
socialist countries will be important. Practical experience in carrying out policy-oriented studies of
environmental and other health issues in countries with incomplete data of variable quality will also
be important. The inclusion of local and other Russian-speaking consultants will be particularly
valuable for all aspects of the project, and is a requirement of the contract.



STAFF APPRAISAL REPORT
RUssN FEDERATION
ENVIRONNIENTAL MANAGEMENT PRtoECr
ANNEX 3.3
WATER QUALITY AND WATER RESOURCES MANAGEMENT
COMPONENT DESCRIPTION
A.    Background
1.    There is substantial agreement among the principal water management agencies of the Russian
Federation on the nature of current water quality management problems and on the priorities for future
action. This is reflected in the framework outlined in Table 3.3.1 which links these priorities, to
specific strategies and objectives. The priorities for environmental management in the water sector
identified by the MEPNR and the other key water sector agencies are to:
*     Ensure the availability and supply of safe drinking water
*     Restore and maintain the ecological health of rivers and other water bodies
*     Promote water conservation and strengthen integrated water quality and quantity
management
2.    The strategy for ensuring the availability of safe drinking water focuses on managing
watersheds to protect sources of potable water supply including reducing pollution loads, identifying
alternative sources of supply, improving the effectiveness of drinking water treatment and distribution,
and adjusting priorities to reflect health risks. The strategy to restore and maintain the ecological
health of rivers and other water bodies focuses on improving the present system of ambient water
quality standards, increasing the effectiveness of the regulatory system, building the capacity to carry
out water quality assessments that support management needs, and increasing the effectiveness of
waste treatment. The third priority focuses on water conservation and strengthening integrated water
resource management, and the corresponding strategy will focus on the introduction of economic
incentives, institutional strengthening, and building greater public awareness. Despite the need for
specific actions in response to current problems of pollution and water quality degradation, most local
authorities recognize the need to develop an integrated and comprehensive approach to improved
management.
3.    The framework outlined in Table 3.3.1 not only provides an effective mechanism to develop
specific high priority projects, but also provides a convenient and effective means for the MEPNR to
formulate projects and programs, and to coordinate the activities of donors with an interest in carrying
out investment programs supporting its efforts to build capacity and strengthen the institutions in the
sector.



Annex 3.3                                                                            121
B.    Assistance Strategy
4.     The current transition in the Russian Federation to a new economic system that relies less on
the former command and control approach to environmental management and more on local initiative
and authority carried out within a broad federal policy framework has understandably resulted in
confusion and uncertainty. Local authorities have repeatedly stressed the need to strengthen four
areas:
*     the need to clarify the legal framework within which they will be required to operate.
The emergence of new federal laws on water and the environment do not fully satisfy
this need because they do not deal with the full range of changes needed in the
processes, methodologies, and procedures for such vital areas as priority setting,
permitting, standards, and enforcement;
*     the need to develop prioritized investment programs, particularly in the area of
municipal water and wastewater system improvements, in the context of
comprehensive and integrated water management plans. In the past there has been a
notable lack of participation by all the local affected parties when preparing plans, and
these plans often did not reflect a least cost approach nor the economic considerations
appropriate to a market type economy. The current budget crisis has made local
authorities acutely aware that the devolution of authority will bring with it new
financial burdens as well, and that these must be accompanied by a greater focus on
cost;
*     the need to translate plans into management actions. Local authorities are very aware
of the weaknesses of the former system of management and have expressed a strong
desire to know more about the experience of the OECD countries and to develop new
and more effective approaches and procedures that can work in their areas;
*     the need to focus on the problems of small watersheds and municipalities. The ability
of local and regional authorities to control activities across large river basins and
watersheds is extremely limited, not only because of the diversity of these activities
and the limited effectiveness of present regulatory systems, but also because they lack
practical and low-cost technologies and the mechanisms to disseminate them at the
local and enterprise levels. The devolution has left small municipalities with large
responsibilities and burdens for infrastructure, but the former system in which
technical capacity was centralized leaves these small municipalities with little or no
capacity to work out priorities and programs to deal with the most pressing
environmental and public health issues.
5.     Hence the initial focus of this Component will be on strengthening environmental and water
management rather than on investment in infrastructure.  The need for the rehabilitation,
modernization, and expansion of existing infrastructure, particularly water and waste water treatment
is evident. However, it is likely to be more cost-effective to concentrate initially on strengthening
existing management, introducing new technology and approaches, including economic incentives, and
identify priority projects and investment programs than to commit large, scarce resources to costly
infrastructure and uncertain improvements.
6.     Subcomponent projects specifically designed to meet immediate and long term needs in each
region will each address a limited subset of the objectives outlined in Table 3.3.1, and will be



122                                                      Environmental Management Project
implemented at the oblast or watershed level in different regions. The sub-component projects will
provide specific opportunities to develop, test, adapt, and demonstrate new methods and procedures,
train personnel and develop training materials, prepare, and implement portions of, integrated water
quality management action plans, and address both immediate and long-term needs.
7.    The Component will provide technical assistance for training, technology transfer, and the
development, testing, and demonstration of new processes, procedures, guidelines, and methodologies;
test the application of new economic tools and management mechanisms and instruments to improve
enforcement and compliance; provide training, particularly to create greater understanding of modern
practices and provide opportunities to collaborate with foreign experts and to study and adapt
experience in OECD countries; provide for procurement of equipment; provide for the implementation
of demonstration projects and activities; and provide resources to develop and implement an outreach
program to coordinate and disseminate project findings, recommendations, and lessons.
C.    Objectives
8.    The objectives of the Water Quality and Water Resources Management Component of the
Environment Management Project are to assist MEPNR and officials of the environment and water
management agencies at the oblast level:
(i)   to integrate and improve the effectiveness of present environmental management plans,
infrastructure, institutions, economic instruments and regulatory arrangements with
respect to water quantity and quality, particularly in relation to urban water and waste
water services;
(ii)   to acquire and adapt modern approaches and new technology as well as recent
experience in the OECD countries; and
(iii)  to expand the pipeline of high priority projects consistent with the ongoing economic
transition.
D.    The Role of Other Donors and Bilateral Agencies
9.    Other bilateral and multilateral international agencies and donors are supporting or planning
to support improved environmental and water management and environmental restoration in the
Russian Federation. The framework of priorities, objectives, and subcomponent projects established
under this project is expected to assist the MEPNR and other water sector agencies to coordinate this
assistance, and to provide clearer priorities for donor programming.
10.   Several bilateral donors including the Environmental Protection Agency (EPA) and the Agency
for International Development (AID) of the U.S. Government, and Environment Canada have directly
assisted the Bank and the Ministry in preparing this project. Several other donors including the
Governments of the Netherlands, Germany and Finland have expressed interest in supporting some
of the sub-component project activities.
E.    Approach
11.   While local environmental and water management authorities have traditionally had a strong
administrative role in environment and water management, particularly in the permitting, planning and
operation of water management facilities, and water allocation functions, the increased emphasis on



Annex 3.3                                                                         123
decentralization is creating the opportunity for greater regional and local initiative and leadership in
developing new approaches to more effective management. The high degree of interdependence and
potential for effective coordination among different agencies at the regional level is very evident in
their response to this project and represents an opportunity to develop an integrated approach. Hence,
the approach adopted for the Water Quality Management Component is to focus on the execution of
site specific sub-component projects in selected priority regions utilizing the existing local network
among environment and water management institutions to provide management oversight and to
facilitate implementation.
12.   EMP Program. The Water Quality and Water Resources Management Component focuses
on developing and testing solutions to the major water quality management problems in three key
regions: (a) the Upper Volga River basin; (b) the Urals region; and (c) the North Caucasus region.
The subcomponent in each region will consist of an integrated planning and regulatory reform
program aimed at improving safe drinking water supplies, developing a prioritized investment and
action program, and addressing key sectoral policy issues through pilot project activities at the oblast
level. Each subcomponent will also support existing regional organizations and improved regional
inter-agency management of priority issues. In addition, the component will support a Federal
Program to provide mechanisms within the Project to disseminate the results of pilot projects to others
who are not directly participating in project activities, and to create the opportunity for those working
on various pilot projects to meet periodically to exchange experiences. This federal program will be
executed by the MEPNR's Department of Surface Water Protection.
13.   Upper Volga River Basin (Annex 3.3.1). This is a vast region of forests and farms with its
modest population concentrated in an number of large industrial cities and important tourist areas
located in the riverain environment of the Volga River. The river is the main source of water supply
for the region's population, is an important fishery, navigation and transportation link, and source of
hydroelectric power. The subcomponent will support the newly formed Inter-regional Council of the
Upper Volga formed by the five concerned Oblasts with the goal of improving and restoring the
ecological condition of the Upper Volga River. The objectives of the subcomponent include the
identification of strategies and priority investment programs to improve the quality and efficiency
water supply systems in selected cities, asses alternative region economic policy options to
management water use and quality, and develop regulatory reform programs for industrial and
municipal water management.;
14.   North Caucasus Region (Annex 3.3.2). This subcomponent will focus on the problems of
integrated water management and conservation in the Lower Don River basin (from the Tsimlyansk
reservoir to the Azov sea) in the Rostov Oblast.   The Don River is one of the largest rivers in
european Russia. It has been intensively developed for irrigation, hydropower, and water supply.
The water supply and waste water management problems of the large cities of this region, including
Rostov, include supply deficits characterized by intermittent service, microbiological contamination
due to the lack of treatment capacity and inadequate maintenance of the distribution systems. Reduced
flows brought about by the more intensive irrigation water use, including reservoir storage, have led
to increased salinity in the Azov sea, and pollution in its highly urbanized and industrialized lower
basin have resulted in major fisheries and ecological damage. The objectives of the subcomponent
include the development of prototype monitoring systems, demonstration of small watershed
management practices, development of strategies and prioritized investment programs for improving
water supply and municipal wastewater treatment, and the development of integrated strategies for
regional water management and conservation focusing in particular on improvement in water supply
availability and restoration of the riverain ecosystem in the Lower Don River.



124                                                      Environmental Management Project
15.   The Urals Region (Annex 3.3.3). This subcomponent will be located in the major industrial
city of Ekaterinburg, and will focus on the problems of integrated management of the raw and treated
water supply of Ekaterinburg. The large concentration and rapid growth of industry and population
combined with periodic droughts, especially over the last 50 years, has led to the development of
complex water management systems comprising numerous reservoirs and inter-regional transfers.
Public heath officials believe that environmental conditions in the oblast and surrounding areas, which
have greatly effected the quality of drinking water supplies, have been a primary factor in the reported
higher than average incidence of disease and worsening morbidity rates. The objectives of this
subcomponent include assessment of the threat to public health from drinking water supplies and
sources of supply for Ekaterinburg; evaluation and prioritization of current infrastructure needs and
the institutional and technical framework for supplying potable water and protecting its quality;
recommend physical, operational, legal, financial, and institutional improvements; demonstrate specific
measures to reduce threats to public health stemming from drinking water; and strengthen local/Oblast
institutions for drinking water management.
F.    Project Organization
16.   The Government has designated the Ministry of Environment Protection and Natural Resources
(MEPNR) as the lead agency for implementation and coordination of EMP activities, and management
of the EMP loan as well as other donor financed activities within the EMP framework. The MEPNR
has established an inter-agency Component Management Committee to supervise implementation of
the Water Quality and Water Resources Management Component that will be chaired by the director
of the MEPNR's Department of Surface Water Protection.
17.   The MEPNR will delegate responsibility for day-to-day technical and administrative functions
for implementation of the regional Water Quality and Water Resource Management Subcomponents
to a Sub-component Management Committee (SCMC) and a Sub-component Program Manager
(SCPM). The activities of the SCMC and SCPM will be supported in the following areas by the CPPI
(Annex 7.4) liaison; procurement; disbursement; reporting, accounting, and audition; information
exchange; and related policies and procedures.
18.   The SCPM will hire a team of experienced Russian technical staff (with the number varying
depending on the size and complexity of the program) and administrative and support staff to form
a Subcomponent Implementation Team (SIT). The SIT staff will be recruited and appointed in
accordance with guidelines and personnel policies established by the CPPI (see also paragraph 4.86).
SIT staff will provide technical management and support for implementation of the SCMC
subcomponent; This team will be equipped with reproduction, communication, computer, and
essential general technical office equipment to support implementation of the subcomponent, and will
provide direct support for Russian and foreign advisors and consultants, including space, equipment,
and administrative support such as travel and meeting arrangements, interpreters, and translation
services as described in the final subcomponent TOR.
G.    Schedule
19.   An overall period of four years is assumed for the implementation of the Water Quality and
Water Resources Management Component, divided into two phases as outlined below.
20.   Phase I - Start-up Activities. Activities during this initial Phase will establish the institutional
framework for implementation of the Water Quality and Resources Management Component at the
regional level. The EMP loan would finance the establishment and staffing of the SCPM, provide the



Annex 3.3                                                                         125
specialized training needed by the SCPMs, the staff of SITs and the members of the SCMCs. The
General Consultant firm will assist the CPPI in its work. Activities will include:
*     build a national level program team including the SCPMs, staff of the MEPNR, CPPI,
and other key agencies; develop a common concept of the purpose, approach and aims
of the project, establish milestones and specifics goals, and develop regular
mechanisms for communication and coordination;
*     provide training to the SCPMs in CPPI policies and procedures, project management,
work planning and preparation of tender documents for consulting services; develop
mechanisms and approaches for effective working relationships with the CPPI,
donors, and other government agencies at the federal, regional, and local levels;
*     establish the regional SIT Offices including the recruitment of staff, procurement of
equipment; provide training for SIT staff;
*     build a regional level project team consisting of the SCPM, SIT, as well as Russian
and foreign experts.
21.   Phase l(b) - Inception and Work Planning. Activities during this phase, which follows
immediately on the subcomponent team building workshop, would develop a detailed work plan and
plan of implementation for each subcomponent project. Analysis and planning would focus on
sources, availability of data, methodologies, detailed discussion of expected outputs, results and their
relation to the objectives. The work plan includes the description, sequencing, and scheduling of work
tasks, data requirements, and analytical methods in sufficient detail to prepare the contractors TORs
and monitor progress of the work. The implementation plan would include a determination of all
inputs required to complete the subcomponent project including contracts to provide local and foreign
technical assistance, equipment, etc. Local technical resources to be used to implement the pilot
project would be identified and evaluated. The implementation plan would include a schedule of
subcontracts and all required Terms of Reference.
22.   Phase 11 - Implementation of Subcomponents (3.5 years). This phase would consist of the
implementation of each of the subcomponents. The component would provide consultants (local and
foreign) to assist the SCPM and SIT to carry out studies, surveys, and analysis; equipment; training
including workshops and study tours; and demonstration projects and activities.
23.   Phase 1 - Federal Program of Outreach and Coordination (3.5 years). This phase of the
project would develop a mechanism to network the various subcomponent projects to insure the
exchange of experience between team working on the same type of pilot project, and a mechanism
such as a annual or semi-annual seminar or workshop to link subcomponent project teams to oblasts
and municipalities that are not participating in the project. It would run in parallel with the
implementation of each regional subcomponent.

STAFF APPRAISAL REPORT
RUSSAN FEDERATION
ENVIRONMENTAL MANAGEMENT PRoECr
ANNEX 3.3.1
UPPER VOLGA RIVER BASIN INTEGRATED REGIONAL ENVIRONMENTAL
MANAGEMENT POLICIES AND STRATEGIES
SUBCOMPONENT DESCRIPTION
A.    Background
1.    The Upper Volga River extends 1263 km from its source in the Valdai Hills northwest of
Moscow to the confluence of the Oka river. The total watershed area is 229,000 sq.km. From its
headwaters the river flows through a series of small reservoirs among which is the Ivankovskoya
which is one of the principal sources of drinking water for Moscow. The 448 km long river stretch
between Rybinsk and Nizhni Novgorod has been replaced by the Gorky reservoir. Filled in 1955-1957
with usable capacity 3,6 cu.km, it became the last element in the Upper Volga cascade system (mean
annual discharge 53 cu.km). The total installed capacity of the 4 hydropower stations of the Upper
Volga cascade is about 900 MW (Ivankovo 30, Uglich 11, Rybinsk 330, Gorky 520). Although the
reservoirs that form the cascade are multipurpose, the interests of power generation have taken
precedence over other uses in the past. The river is also intensively used for navigation, predominantly
for the transport of oil products, coal and building materials.
2.    Unfortunately, the transformation of the Upper Volga for these purposes has resulted in a
number of important negative conditions from the standpoint of the environment and water
management. Groundwater tables have risen dramatically causing the expansion of swamps in the
basin. The large surfaces of shallow reservoir water, which become quite warm during summer, have
greatly increased evaporation. The existence of stagnant, shallow embayments along the edges of
storage reservoirs has led to the substantial accumulation of pollutants. Pollution of bottom sediments
is a serious problem especially in the northern part of the Rybinsk reservoir where waste from the
industrial complex at Cherpavets has done great damage to water quality and the aquatic ecosphere
of the upper Volga reservoirs. Eutrophication, manifested by luxuriant growth of aquatic and semi-
aquatic vegetation is now a prominent feature in summer with consequent degradation of water quality.
The rising groundwater tables, as well as the intermittent existence of shallow surface waters, have
significantly altered vegetation in immediate shore areas. In some cases, the rise of groundwater tables
has proved to be detrimental to forests which cover about 50 percent of the Upper Volga basin area.
Ice generally remains two weeks longer on the large reservoirs than it did on the original river.
3.    The total annual withdrawals of water in the Upper Volga basin are about 10.2 cu.km.,
including 2.7 cu.km. transferred to the Moscow water supply system. The total wastewater discharged
to inland water bodies is difficult to estimate but, for example, in Yaroslavl Oblast alone about
400,000 cu.m. are discharged annually, out of which about 75 percent are untreated or inadequately
treated. It is interesting to note that in this oblast about 90 percent of wastewater is discharged directly
to the Volga proper (or its reservoirs). Proportional to their flows, smaller rivers also receive
considerable pollution load and their quality is considerably below standards required for drinking
water sources. Except for small communities supplied by groundwater, the majority of the population



128                                                      Environmental Management Project
is served by surface water. The typical substances detected in the surface water bodies (Volga, its
reservoirs and tributaries) are oil products, copper, ammonia, nitrate, organic substances, phenols and
others, some exceeding by several times the maximum acceptable concentration (MAC) values.
4.    In the Upper Volga, as in most areas throughout Russia, there is a critical need for improved
drinking water treatment and supply systems. Each of the cities visited use the Volga River as their
source of supply and all recognized the need for upgrading of their treatment technology to include
carbon absorption and ozonation. In addition to the need for new technology and design assistance
there is a need to upgrade current operations through improved operation and maintenance, laboratory
capability, and overall management including water conservation.
5.    Regional Efforts to Take Action.  In response to a growing regional concern for and
recognition of these issues, the five oblasts which constitute the Upper Volga River basin - Vologda,
Ivanovo, Kostroma, Yaroslavl, and Tver - have formed the Inter-Oblast Environmental Council of
Upper Volga with the goal of improving and restoring the ecological condition of the Upper Volga
River. The Council consists of six voting members including the Chairman of the respective Oblast
Environmental Committees and the Chairman of the Council. Representatives of regional NGOs and
environmental organizations, academic and technical organizations in the region, and others may
participate in the work of the Council and have the right of an advisory vote. The current Chairman
is the Director of the Institute of Inland Water Biology in Borok. The members have agreed to form
a Regional Ecological Fund to support the Council's activities financed by contributions from the
respective Oblast ecological funds.
6.    The competence of the Council includes formulating and making recommendations on urgent
actions for improvement of ecological conditions in the Upper Volga; determining mechanisms to
finance programs and actions including the determination of Oblast contributions; adopting joint
decisions on disputed intra-regional issues; making recommendations on permanent or temporary
stoppage of industrial discharges; organizing and carrying out regional ecological studies; organizing
international environmental cooperation; and adopting an annual budget including allocations of the
Upper Volga Ecological Fund to various activities.
7.    Recognizing the deteriorating ecological condition of the river, and the complex character of
the causes, the Council has outlined a program to carry out an urgent assessment of the ecological
situation to identify the direction and causes of current trends, to identify priority issues, impacts and
problems, to develop scientifically sound basin environmental policy alternatives, to consider regional
strategic and near-term goals, and to adopt and implement a regional action plan for the restoration
of the ecological health of the river basin.
B.    Objectives
8.    The aim of this sub-project is to support the work of the Council and the respective Oblasts,
municipalities, and enterprises in the region to develop and implement an integrated regional
environmental management policy framework and action plan for the Upper Volga River basin, to
assess the main problems and factors affecting the safety and quality of drinking water in the Upper
Volga region, and identify strategy to improve drinking water supply and wastewater management
systems. The broader, long-term sup-project compliments the regional scientific program of the
Council by developing a near and mid-term policy and investment priority that are intended to lead
to immediate improvements. There are six specific objectives of the sub-project.



Annex 3.3.1                                                                            129
(1)    An assessment of the current ecological situation in the Upper Volga River basin,
including an appropriate classification of different ecological and functional zones,
based on the identification and assessment of risk and hazard areas (from both an
environmental and public health perspective).
(2)    An assessment of available data, identification of important gaps, completion of
surveys to fill these gaps, and development of maps, data bases, and models to
support policy analysis and plan formulation and evaluation.
(3)    An analytical description of the regional hydrological and ecological systems including
functions (water and land uses) and sources of pollution to identify issues, assess
impacts and determine priorities.
(4)    Identify strategies to improve the overall performance and efficiency of the drinking
water supply system in selected cities of the Upper Volga River Basin, and to assess
priorities for action to ensure safe and reliable supply for domestic and industrial use.
(5)    Assess alternative regional economic policy options including water charges, other
economic incentives, and water utility management and financial strategies to
encourage water conservation and source protection, and waste reduction management.
(6)    Alternative integrated regional environmental policies and strategies including water
quality standards, enhanced monitoring systems, industrial and municipal pollution
prevention and control strategies, economic incentives, investments, regulatory and
institutional reforms, and information dissemination mechanisms.
C.    Scope of Work
9.     The pilot project would consist of the following principal tasks:
Task I        Develop an analytical description of the regional ecological system
la.    Compile and assess the suitability of existing data to describe the status and
trends of different ecological zones, including water bodies.
lb.    Develop and carry out surveys to fill important data gaps.
Ic.    Compile, develop or adapt data bases, maps and models, etc. to support
analysis of current ecological trends and causes.
Id.    Assess the adequacy of the current monitoring systems for management and
emergency response and formulate a cost-effective expansion and remodeling
of the current monitoring system including sampling, analysis, and
dissemination of data (coordinating in particular with the findings and
recommendations of pilot project B.3).



130                                                       Environmental Management Project
Task 2.      Regional inventory and assessment of industrial sources of pollution as
well as navigation, agriculture and animal husbandry.
2a.   Inventory the industrial sources of waste including stockpiles from past
production and wastes from current production.
2b.    Describe the present technology, economic condition, waste stream, and waste
management practices of the major sources.
2c.    Assess the current impacts on surface and groundwater, and different
ecological zones of these waste sources, and assess health risks.
2d.    Evaluate alternative strategies to improve waste management at the priority
or high risk waste sites, including waste minimization and reduction,
considering health and ecological risks.
2e.   Identify and evaluate the nature and potential (technical and economic) for
technological changes to reduce and minimize waste, and cost-effective and
ecologically sound technologies for waste management
Task 3       Assessment of domestic and mixed drinidug water and waste water
systems including municipal and rural systems
3a.    Carry out a regional inventory and assessment of rural and municipal drinking
water supplies and sources identifying public health risks and trends.
3b.   Inventory the status of rural and municipal waste water management systems;
assess the impacts of waste water discharges.
3c.    Assess the present institutional and financial constraints and conditions for
effective management, operation, and investment in municipal water and
waste water services.
3d.    Determine priorities and evaluate least cost regional and municipal level
options including water conservation, pricing and other economic incentives,
and varying balances between rehabilitation, modernization, and phased
introduction of technology and facility expansion.
Task 4.      Assess the status of treated and delivered water quality and rank factors
affecting drinking water safety in water supply systems in selected cities
of the Upper Volga region.
4a.   Compile and analyze all existing data on treated drinking water quality, water
quality within the distribution system and at the tap, and public health data
related to water borne diseases and health risks.
4b.   Identify data gaps and inconsistencies and carry out limited, targeted
monitoring sites as required to fill key data gaps.



Annex 3.3.1                                                                         131
4c.    Develop a rapid water supply system audit procedures and conduct audits of
selected drinking water supply systems.
4c.   Identify and rank in terms of importance, impact, and risk, the key factors
and problems affecting drinking water safety and quality in the Upper Volga
region on the basis of the sample audits and data analysis in Task 1.
Task 5.      Develop programs to improve the capacity to manage drinking water
systems in the region.
5a.   Identify needs for capacity building, particularly to improve the performance
and effectiveness of operations, maintenance, and system management.
Sb.   Develop and implement operator training program, develop operation and
maintenance manuals, operation training materials and courses for operator
certification program.
Task 6       Analysis of regulatory options and controls.
6a.   Review the current system of permitting, compliance and enforcement for
regulating water pollution in the region at both the municipal and regional
level.
6b.   Assess existing industrial waste regulatory and control procedures, and
enforcement and compliance practices, and develop new procedures and
practices as required including such elements as classification, registration,
licensing, and monitoring.
6c.   Assess existing data, computer systems, and analytical methods and models
used to establish discharge limits, track and evaluate self-monitoring reports,
evaluate compliance, and coordinate and schedule inspections.
6d.   Develop new analytical procedures for permitting, compliance monitoring,
new enforcement strategies that could be considered in Task 6.
Task 7.      Assess cost recovery and rmancing issues.
7a.   Develop methodology and conduct a review of existing system of water
charges and metering.
7b.   Assess the financial condition of selected drinking water systems in terms of
revenue required to meet full operations, maintenance,and replacement needs,
and support needed investments.
7c.    Make recommendations for changes in system to provide incentives for water
conservation and efficient water use.



132                                                        Environmental Management Project
Task 8.      Analysis of economic policy options and financing mechanisms for water
management.
8a.    Analyze the practicality and potential effectiveness of alternative economic
policies and incentive options to increase water use efficiency, promote
conservation and encourage water source protection.
8b.   Identify and evaluate alternatives of raising adequate revenues for water
source protection measures and improvements in the drinking water and waste
water infrastructure, including sound operation and management.
8c.   Identify and prioritized investment needs for rehabilitation, modernization,
and expansion of existing facilities for drinking water supply, evaluate the
need for new water treatment technology.
8d.    Recommend an economic policy framework and strategy, and financing
mechanisms, for the Upper Volga region that serves the goals and objectives
developed in Task 5a.
Task 9       Screen alternative integrated regional environmental policies, investment
strategies, and institutional arrangements and reforms
9a.    Develop a framework of strategic goals and objectives and a screening model
to assess alternative regional environmental policies and strategies paying
particular attention to defining explicit criteria for selecting priority objectives
and problems.
9b.    Generate alternative least-cost options and measures to address priority
objectives, problems and issues considering in particular the balance between
economic incentives, regulatory controls, pollution prevention, and the phased
introduction of new technology.
9c.    Formulate alternative near-term and long-term environmental management
strategies.
9d.    Develop a regional consensus on priorities and strategies, and adopt a regional
environmental management plan.
10.   Outputs.
(1)    Plans for upgrading the regional monitoring system
(2)    Standardized drinking water system audits procedures
(3)    Operator training program, operational and maintenance manuals
(4)    A regional strategy and plan for environmental management
(5)    Regional priorities and strategy for minimizing industrial pollution and improving
industrial and municipal waste water management
(6)    A regional plan for upgrading and expanding drinking water supply systems and
protecting drinking water sources, including prioritized investment plan.



Annex 3.3.1                                                                        133
11.   Plan of Implementation. The Inter-Oblast Environmental Council of the Upper Volga in
collaboration with the respective oblast environment committees would be responsible for supervising
the implementing this subcomponent. Regional expertise including experts from the scientific institutes
and universities located in the region would form a Subcomponent Implementation Team to carry out
the work, and would be supplemented as necessary by specialized expertise at other Russian institutes,
as well as by foreign consultants. Study tours and collaboration with foreign specialists will be used
to review experience in the OECD countries with the policy, institutional, and technical issues and
options to be considered in this region.



STAFF APPRAISAL REPORT
RUSsIAN FEDERAnON
ENVIRONMmITAL MANAGEMENT PROJECr
ANNEx 3.3.2
THE NORTH CAUCASUS REGION INTEGRATED WATER RESOURCES
CONSERVATION AND MANAGEMENT
SUBCOMPONENT DESCRIPTION
A. Introduction
1.    Project activities will be concentrated in the Lower Don River basin from the Tsimlyansk
reservoir to the Azov sea. The Don River is one of the largest rivers in European Russia. It has been
intensively developed for irrigation, hydropower, navigation, and water supply. Reduced flows
brought about by small rivers impoundment and siltation and more intensive water use, including
irrigation and reservoir storage, have led to increased salinity in the Azov sea, which combined with
pollution from sources in the highly urbanized and industrialized lower basin, has resulted in major
fisheries and ecological damage.
B.    Background
2.    Over the last 30 years, construction of a system of storage reservoirs on the Don River and
its tributaries has radically changed the hydrological regime of the river, especially in its lower reach
below the Tsimlyansk reservoir (24 km3 total capacity). Development of inland navigation (Volga-
Don canal) and irrigation water supply were the main purposes of these developments which almost
completely eliminated downstream floods. The mean spring runoff (April and May) recorded in
Razdorskaya cross-section about 60 km upstream of the Taganrog Bay where the Don flows into the
Azov Sea, was reduced from 16.6 km3 (mean value for 1881-1951) to 6.3 km3 (mean value for 1952-
1985).
3.    As a result of these developments and the consequent change of flooding regime, the natural
spawning grounds for fluvial anadromous fish have been drastically reduced in the lower Don River,
its delta and in the Azov Sea. Fish productivity in the area, considered earlier to be one of the highest
in the world, dropped from over 300,000 tons in the 1950s to present levels of about 10,000 - 30,000
tons/year. Pollution of water resources by poorly controlled wastewater discharges from industry,
municipalities and agriculture have also contributed to the reduction of fish population, as well as to
the degradation of drinking water supplies endangering public health.
4.    The Rostov Oblast Environment Committee has reported that in samples from the 20 public
water intakes located on the rivers Don, Mius, Seversky Donets, 17 do not comply with Federal
standards "GOST. Drinking Water" on bacteriological and chemical parameters. During the last 8-10
years in summer period vibrio similar to cholera were detected in the samples taken from drinking
water intakes. Pathogens of acute intestinal infections were detected in drinking water distribution
system in Shakhti City, Bagaevsky and Semikarakorsky districts Rostov Oblast. In 1991-93 eight
cases of group infection deceases directly caused by poor drinking water supply were registered in



Annex 3.3.2                                                                          135
Rostov Oblast (cities of Volgodonsk and Belaya Kalitva, Aksai and Zernograd districts). The total
number of people who became ill in those cases was about 700.
5.     While a large proportion of the total pollution load in the lower Don River basin comes from
activities in the Rostov Oblast, a substantial load also comes from mining, agriculture and industrial
activities in Ukraine through the Seversky Donets River which is the main tributary of the lower Don
River. The Seversky Donets pollution load commonly contains significant quantities of phenols,
chlorides, sulfates, heavy metals, pesticides and chemical fertilizers which pose serious risks to the
stream population in Russia, especially to the city of Kamensk-Shachtinsky located on the Seversky
Donets river. This city of about 70,000 people is growing and water is in short supply. The Seversky
Donets begins in Russia (Belgorod Oblast), flows through Ukraine, re-enters Russia and joins the
lower Don River about 150 km below the Tsimlyansk reservoir (about 150 km upstream of the city
of Rostov).
6.     The proposed subcomponent project area would cover the Lower Don water resources system,
from Tsimlyansk reservoir to the Taganrog Bay. This system includes the lower Don tributaries,
especially the Seversky Donets and Manych rivers. Although the project area is limited to the Lower
Don system and Rostov Oblast, it would address related problems in the upstream parts of the basin.
7.     Water Quality Monitoring. In the Lower Don Basin several agencies are active monitoring
water quality. These agencies include the Federal Survey for Hydrometeorology and Environmental
Monitoring responsible for measurement of stream flow, ambient water quality, precipitation, and
aquatic biota; State Committee for Sanitary and Epidemiological Surveillance responsible for
microbiology in drinking water systems, sanitary quality of recreational and ambient waters; the
municipal "vodokanals" responsible for water quality upstream  from  major water intakes and
monitoring of drinking water in the distribution systems; Committee for Water Resources Management
responsible for water allocation and water-related data; Ministry of Environmental Protection and
Natural Resources responsible for point-source discharge monitoring; Committee of Geology and
Mineral Resources responsible for monitoring of the ground water quantity and quality.
8.     Several agencies have, for a long time, been producing water-quality data on major ions,
nutrients, oxygen, pH, turbidity, BOD5, and coliform bacteria. A limited amount of analyses for a few
metals, organochlorine and organophosphorus pesticides, and phenols are currently available, but
many of the detection levels are above concentrations of drinking water and environmental concern.
Existing laboratory analytical capabilities are often out-dated and poorly supplied with chemicals and
disposable supplies; shortages of computers, software, and trained personnel have resulted in few
existing computer-data systems, and minimal sharing of data between agencies. Agencies are not using
consistent sample collection protocols within their agencies and between agencies. Quality control
samples are being collected but the results are difficult to find and there is no splitting of samples
between agencies. The quantification of pollution in point sources is often poor or unknown. Little
is known about concentrations of pollutants in sediments, tissues of aquatic biota, and the flow and
quality of ground water and its impact on surface water. Only the Hydrochemical Institute in Rostov
has interpreted the ambient water-quality data such as compilation of national ambient water quality
summaries on an annual basis. Data do not appear to be used to make water quality management
decisions (e.g. stream sections which exceed standards might receive greater attention for permitting).
All of the federal agencies are currently finding it extremely difficult to continue current monitoring
activities because of a severe lack of operational funds.
9.     Small River Watershed problems in the Don Basin. Within the Rostov Oblast there are a
large number of small rivers which are tributaries to the Don and other main rivers of the area or



136                                                         Environmental Management Project
which flow directly to the Azov Sea. The Oblast is intensively farmed with the primary crops being
wheat and corn. There are also a number of minor crops including sunflower, barley, sugar beets and
legumes. There is also a significant amount of pasture for grazing and hay production. In addition,
there are a number of large concentrated livestock and poultry enterprises. The majority of the farms
in the oblast are still large collective farms with a few private farms. The number of private farms is
growing, however the total is still very small. The collective farms are generally very large, ranging
from 10,000 to 20,000 hectares. The topography of the area is flat to gently rolling with some large
breaks near some of the streams and rivers. Past agricultural practices have focused entirely upon
production with little attention given to off-site impacts. Only recently have farmers begun to consider
the use of minimum tillage practices. Approximately 10-15 percent of the land is fallow each year.
This land is generally plowed in the fall and worked in the spring and summer for planting of winter
wheat in the fall. Fertilization is provided by use of animal waste and supplemented by up to 100
kg/ha of chemical fertilizer. Chemical fertilization and pesticides has decreased over the past several
years due to increased costs and lack of availability.
10.   Intensive industrial and agricultural use of water and land resources in small and medium size
river basins of the Lower Don resulted in their unsatisfactory condition characterized by decreased
water flow, increased water and soil pollution, and progressive siltation of rivers. The natural water
regime of most of the rivers is disturbed and reproduction conditions of most of the fish, animal and
vegetation species have been changed dramatically. Regulations on water protection zones are
typically not followed. Degradation of small rivers is stimulated by pesticides, nutrients, and
suspended solids loading, heavy metals and intensive eutrophication. Some rivers are transformed into
a cascade of ponds and reservoirs constructed by the collective farms. These ponds normally do not
have outlet facilities preventing continuous flow to the streams. They are used by the collective farms
for livestock watering, some irrigation as well as fishing and recreation.
11.   Recently, a number of projects for restoration and protection of small rivers were initiated and
either partially or fully implemented. However, restoration measures did not result in any significant
improvement of the ecological situation due to the lack of an integrated approach at the design stage,
insufficient data on natural bodies exposed to anthropogenic impact, lack of routine monitoring data
on the processes occurring within watersheds and streams. Sometimes, only fragments of the projects
were implemented which did not comply with demands of an integrated basin approach.
12.    Oblast water resource and environmental officials have indicated a strong desire to undertake
a pilot project in a small watershed to develop a comprehensive plan for both water and land
management. Such a project would also include demonstrations of erosion control, pesticides and
nutrient management, and household and animal waste management practices to minimize adverse
impacts to the water resources of the watershed. It is suggested to implement the pilot project in the
Kagalnik Azovsky River Basin, in the 12 km long reach of the river from the village of Kagalnitzkaya
to the mouth of the Mechetka River, including the area of the village and collective farm Kalinin. The
Kagalnik River Basin occupies the area of 5,040 km2, the length of the river is 162 km, total length
of the tributaries longer than 10 km is 432 km.
13.    The river receives mixed flow; the ratio between snow melting, ground and rain water varies
with years of different flow conditions; spring flood flow accounts for 70-80 percent of the annual
flow. River water resources within the basin in the year of average flow conditions approximate 76.4
million m3/year; in the year of 75 percent probability - 20.9 million m3; in a year of 95 percent
probability - 7.26 million m3. There are 10 reservoirs in the basin with a total volume of 21.0 million
m3 and 187 ponds with a total volume of 26.2 million m3. Water resources are used for agricultural
water supply irrigation; total volume of water withdrawn is about 40 million m3/year, including 20.15



Annex 3.3.2                                                                          137
million m3 of surface waters. The Kagalnik River is of fishery value and is a spawning area for
transitory and semitransitory fish.
14.   Intensive use, violation of the water protection zones and impoundment of streams resulted
in serious deterioration of hydrological and sanitary conditions in the basin. A considerable amount
of restoration measures are needed. In fact, some work on dredging of the river channels, elimination
of poorly designed dams and management of water protection zones have been carried out in the basin
over the last five years. In 1993, the Don BVO and SevKavNIIVKh started cooperative work on
development of a testing site to improve the system of water resources management.
C.    Objectives
15.   The objective of this subcomponent project is to develop an integrated water resources
management system for the Lower Don River basin, addressing in particular the following issues:
(1)    Operational assessment (evaluation) of the hydrological, hydrophysical, hydrochemical
and ecological characteristics and conditions of the project area, including information
on storage reservoirs, main water uses including environmentally sensitive areas and
in-stream uses, sources of water pollution and environmental degradation, and water
conservation measures
(2)    Assessment of effects on ecological conditions and trends in the project area stemming
from existing water resources use and management activities including existing and
planned investments undertaken for the utilization and protection of water resources
(3)    Development of an integrated system for water resources conservation, operational
management and long-term planning in the Lower Don River basin, addressing in
particular the conflicting uses of water quality protection, fishery management, flood
control, irrigation and industrial water use, drinking water supply, nature
conservation, navigation and hydropower production
(4)    Assess the current water-quality conditions and monitoring activities, to fill critical
data gaps, and to design a modern multipurpose and integrated water quantity/quality
monitoring program in the Lower Don River basin, upstream of water intakes for
municipal drinking water supply. The assessment will include the analysis of current
surface and ground-water data; physical, chemical, and biological data; as well as the
collection of specific data to fill data gaps
(5)    Develop a water quality management plan for a selected demonstration and pilot small
watershed. The plan would consider all impacts on the aquatic ecosystem of the
watershed from both point and nonpoint sources, with particular emphasis on the need
to control erosion and sedimentation pesticides and nutrients and livestock wastes.
16.   This subcomponent project would address also relevant water policy issues, including legal,
institutional, regulatory, economic and financial aspects of integrated water quantity and quality
management. In the project area, the problems of inter-oblast and international cooperation in the area
of water resources management are of particular significance.



138                                                        Environmental Management Project
D.    Scope of Work
17.   The subcomponent project would consist of five principal tasks:
Task I        Assessment of adequacy of existing water resource data for operational
management and strategic planning, and development of prototype water
quantity and quality monitoring system, including information on principal
water uses in the basin.
la.    Assemble existing water data files. Store all existing water (quantity and
quality, surface and ground water) data in one computer data base. The water
quality data would include physical, chemical, aquatic biological, and habitat
data and any associated QA/QC data. The relational data storage system
would include a data dictionary inclusive of all analyses; location, date and
time of sampling; agency collecting data; medium (soil, water, stream bed
sediment, tissue); method of analysis; and associated quality control data.
The data base will include compliance monitoring of point-source effluents
when available. Existing digital line data will also be stored for land use,
land cover, hydrography, geology, precipitation, transportation, and other
ancillary data sets that may be used for interpreting water quality data, such
as population.
lb.    Upgrade current water data collection. Upgrade existing Russian protocols
for measuring, sampling, preserving, filtering, analyzing, recording, and
sharing of water quantity and quality data. Provide laboratories and field
sampling teams with the critical sampling and laboratory equipment needed
to follow the revised protocols. Write a quality assurance plan to identify
quality control measurements needed to quantify accuracy and precision of
data. Train personnel working with samples, analyses, and data to use the
protocols and follow the quality assurance program. Commence a data-
sharing program to include the exchange of computer-stored data. Prepare
a data summary to include number of samples, analyses, sites  sampled,
number of times standards were exceeded, and a description of the data as
appropriate, possibly including seasonal trends, long-term trends, and
causative factors for all data collected in the project area.
Ic.    Identity data gaps and collect selected data. Data gaps will be identified that
would significantly contribute to developing a more accurate conceptual model
of water resources. Such data might include quantification of pollution
sources, analysis of steambed sediments and tissues for metals and organic
compounds, synoptic measurement of point sources and ambient conditions,
ground-water quality and its seasonal variability in specific areas to
understand surface-ground water interactions, quality of precipitation, etc.
The collection of some of the data will include development of new protocols,
provision of training, and acquisition of analytical equipment When possible,
the collection and analysis of samples and the sharing of data will include
participation of as many agencies as possible.
Id.   Assess current water-quality conditions. The assessment of water and related
data will provide managers with a current conceptual model of water-quality



Annex 3.3.2                                                                          139
conditions in the Lower Don River basin upstream of the municipal water
intakes. This conceptual model will include, where possible, all parts of the
hydrologic cycle, such as precipitation, snow melt, overland runoff, ground-
water infiltration, ground-water discharge, well pumpage, reservoir
regulation, diversion of surface water for water supplies and industries,
discharge of point sources, and evaporation. The conceptual model will
include, where possible, spacial variability; seasonal variability; long-term
trends; daily, seasonal, and annual loads; and the source, cause, transport,
fate, and effect of contaminants in the basins; when the data do not allow
quantification of source, cause, transport, fate, and effects, hypotheses will
be identified to provide guidance for designing a new monitoring program.
Oral presentations of results will be made to all interested agencies on a
regular basis. These results, when there is sufficient data to support actions,
will recommend management actions, potentially including a revision of
standards, greater treatment of waste sources, or regulation of reservoir
operation.
le.    Identifying management needs. Management responsibilities, needs, and the
interaction of all participating agencies need to be identified While the
assessment will provide a conceptual model of current water-quality
conditions, the design of a new water monitoring plan needs to consider
current and anticipated needs and uses of water for at least the next 10 years.
Such needs will include population change, water use, management of land
and water use, and political and social changes. Managers of water resources
will be asked to identify management alternatives, such as conservation, new
storage reservoirs, use of ground water, restriction of water use, and
increased irrigation needs.
If.    Design new prototype water-quality monitoring program. The new design
will include the identified water-resource management needs and management
alternatives, the current conceptual model and hypotheses identified in the
assessment, and will be constrained by costs, analytical capability, and our
knowledge of how to collect, analyze and interpret data. The following such
tasks will be identified in the design: coordination of all agency activities;
objectives of water-quality monitoring program in relation to management
needs; design a coordinated monitoring plan incorporating different agency
data-collected programs and joint needs of the participating agencies,
including sampling sites, and protocols, sample preservation, analyses, and
quality assurance plan; implementation plan to include each agencies
responsibility in the plan, including storing and sharing of data; interpretation
of the data to meet management needs and advise agencies of needed changes;
communication of the results to managers, the local, oblast and federal
governments, and the public; and evaluation of the effectiveness of the
monitoring program and revision of the program on a regular basis.



140                                                        Environmental Management Project
Task 2        Analysis of water resources conservation and management policy.
2a.    Organize a workshop to review and evaluate the current legislative/regulatory
arrangements affecting water use and withdrawal and waste water discharges,
including ambient water quality and effluent discharge standards and economic
and other incentives and rules in force in the Lower Don River basin.
Review the existing water balance and the present technical factors which
influence and determine water use. Review water management objectives in
the basin with a special focus on aquatic ecology, fishery management and
mutual relationships among the lower Don river and tributaries, river delta,
and the Taganrog Bay. Agree on Task 2 work program and division of work.
2b.    Develop a system of new policy incentives for water conservation (especially
in irrigation and inland navigation water uses), including regulatory measures,
institutional arrangements and economic incentives.   Utilizing  latest
methodologies develop procedures for the improvement of inter-oblast and
international collaborative arrangements in the region. Organize a workshop
for a thorough discussion of the recommendations with all parties concerned.
2c.    Test policy improvements, including new enforcement tools and monitoring
procedures developed under Task 1 activities.
Task 3        Demonstration of Small Watershed Management Practices.
3a.    Organization and site selection. Meetings with oblast, local officials and
organizations and evaluate potential watershed areas, establish management
and organization for the project. Finalize the selection of one or possibly two
watersheds to conduct pilot study. Establish specific goals for the watershed
area. Compile maps and other information on the areas and consider the
possible development of a geographic information system (GIS) for the
watershed.
3b.   Inventory and Assessment. Evaluate current land use practices including
cropping patterns,tillage and fertilization practices, livestock grazing, etc.
Develop an inventory of municipal and industrial point source dischargers.
Develop an inventory of concentrated livestock and poultry enterprises.
Inventory farmstead practices including septic tank and housewaste
management, pesticide and fertilizer storage and handling and animal waste
management. Inventory existing ponds and other structures on the river
system. Evaluate existing monitoring systems and data and document current
conditions of rivers and streams within the watershed. Prepare a report
outlining the current practices and problems in the watershed and the impacts
upon the water quality and aquatic ecosystem of the rivers.
3c.    Watershed Plan.  Develop overall watershed plan for controlling point
sources of pollution (reconnaissance plan only). Develop a nonpoint source
management plan that includes both structural and management measures for
controlling sediments, nutrients and pesticides. Develop management plan for
controlling impacts from any large concentrated animal feeding enterprises.



Annex 3.3.2                                                                            141
Develop a plan for cleaning and restoring the natural conditions of the
streams.
3d.    Monitoring Plan. Prepare recommended monitoring plan for watershed area
including monitoring runoff from farmsteads, cropland, and concentrated
livestock and poultry enterprises. Initiate monitoring as appropriate.
3e.    Demonstration in Selected Areas of the Watershed. Construct retention
ponds, terraces, waterways, filter strips, tree planting, etc. Demonstrate
management measures such as reduced tillage, pesticide/nutrient management,
improved grazing practices, animal waste management and farmstead
practices. Conduct field days to showcase the various measures and practices.
3f.    Technical Manuals/Educational Materials. Develop technical manual for
assessing impacts and developing control plans for small watershed areas
Develop educational/training materials for use in training collective and
individual farmers and for general education of the public.
3g.    Policy and Institutional Issues.  Investigate existing policies, legal
constraints, institutions, etc. Develop recommendations for possible reforms.
Test possible changes in local areas as part of demonstrations. Prepare
recommendations for policy, legal and/or institutional reforms.
Task 4        Water resources conservation and development of decision support system
for operational control of the Lower Don system.
4a.    Assess water use patterns and needs (concentrating on major water uses and
users), including analysis of the potential for introduction of water
conservation practices and measures; determine environmental and other flow
requirements during different periods of the year, and evaluate alternative
storage reservoir. operating policies and rules in relation to these flow
requirements to identify opportunities for improved performance and
constraints.
4b.    Develop an inventory (data bank) of major wastewater discharges (industrial,
municipal, agricultural and others); review solid and liquid toxic (hazardous)
waste disposal sites and their impact on surface and groundwater quality.
4c.    Organize workshop for a thorough discussion of present system operation
procedures, including demonstration of computerized decision supporting tools
currently in use. Discuss decision criteria with the representatives of federal
government, local authorities and principal water users.
4d.    Develop a computerized decision support system for operational control of the
Lower Don system, with special attention given to reservoir inflow
forecasting, water quality dynamics, demand management, water supply
reliabilities, reservoir control, flow and quality control in the oblast boundary
profiles and on the international rivers.



142                                                       Environmental Management Project
4e.   Install and test new decision support system operated initially in parallel with
the current operational procedures.  Organize workshop for all-Russia
demonstration and dissemination of task results.
Task 5       Long-term planning.
Sa.   Organize workshop for a thorough discussion of future socio-economic
development scenarios of the oblast and the neighboring regions.
Sb.    Develop alternative scenarios of future water use and wastewater discharge
patterns. Utilizing latest methodologies and computer techniques, develop
long-term plan of sustainable water use and protection in the lower Don basin.
The degree of detail should be compatible with the principles of strategic
planning.
Sc.    Assess future requirements for institutional and financial arrangements for
water resource management in the lower Don River basin including planning,
operation, regulation, monitoring, mobilization of financial resources for
investment and operation, and investment programming and allocation of
financial resources; review recent experience in central and eastern Europe
and the OECD countries with different approaches to river basin water
resources management.
E.    Outputs
18.   (1)    The integrated water quantity and quality monitoring system to be used for upgrading
operational management decisions in the Lower Don water resources system.
(2)    Legal, regulatory, institutional and financial policy improvements, including
introduction of economic incentives, leading to more efficient and environmentally-
sound water resources management in the Lower Don Basin.
(3)    Computerized decision support system for operational control of the Lower Don water
management operations. Application of new operational rules would lead to the
restoration of natural flood regime in the Lower Don basin, and consequently to the
gradual rebirth of natural fishery conditions.
(4)    Long-term water resources development and management plan for the Lower Don
basin.
(5)    Recommendations for restructuring river basin water resources management including
required legal reforms, institutional strengthening, capacity building and training
needs.
(6)    For Task 1, revised protocols used in the collection of samples, analysis of samples,
and storage of water quality data.
(7)    Revised quality assurance program for all agencies to use.
(8)    Trained personal to follow revised protocols and quality assurance plan.



Annex 3.3.2                                                                          143
(9)    New data types filling existing data gaps.
(10)   Report on current water-quality conditions and recommendations to water resource
managers to improve basin water quality.
(11)   Design of a new water-quality monitoring program.
(12)   For Task 3, report on existing conditions of the watershed including maps, etc.
(13)   Resource management plan including plans for municipal and industrial point sources,
confined animal feeding and nonpoint sources. (Note: The municipal and industrial
plans would only be reconnaissance level plans).
(14)   Demonstration Projects/Field Days, etc.
(15)   Training/Educational Materials.
(16)   Recommendations for policy/institutional reforms.
F.    Plan of Implementation
19.   The Don River Basin Agency (Don BVO), in collaboration with the Rostov Oblast Committee
for the Protection of Nature, would be responsible for implementation of tasks 2, 4 and 5 of this
subcomponent project. These tasks would be carried out by working groups involving Russian
specialists from such institutions as the North-Caucasus Branch of the Russian Institute for Integrated
Utilization and Protection of Water Resources, the Azov Sea Research Institute of Fishery Problems,
Rostov University, Hydrochemical Institute and others. This project would be coordinated with the
international Black Sea program and the project results would be directly used for development of the
Black Sea recommendations. Working relationships would be established with the non-governmental
environmental organizations. The activity shall include foreign and local training components,
consultations of foreign specialists, and supply of necessary equipment specified tentatively in the
project cost estimates.
20.   Task 1 will be carried out jointly by the Federal Survey for Hydrometeorology and
Environmental Monitoring, State Committee for Sanitary and Epidemiological Surveillance, the
concerned "vodokanals", Ministry of Environmental Protection and Natural Resources, Committee
of Geology and Mineral Resources, and Don River Basin Agency. This task will require each of the
agencies currently collecting water-quality data to review their sampling and laboratory analysis
methods and ensure they meet the revised protocols. Training will be needed within the region to
acquaint field and laboratory personnel with revised protocols and quality assurance plan. Much of
the work in the task would be undertaken by those agencies currently collecting and analyzing samples
and data and their consultants with close collaboration of foreign technical consults. It would be
expected that some computers for data storage data analysis, and Geographic Information System
(GIS), and equipment for sample collection and laboratory analyses will be provided for agencies
responsible for the assessment of data and the collection and analysis of water samples respectively.
In addition, it is likely that travel abroad will be necessary for some personnel to observe first hand
data analysis techniques and special training available in the USA and Western European countries.
This task will take place over approximately a three year period.



144                                                      Environmental Management Project
21.   Task 3 would be carried out by the Don River Basin Agency with support from the Oblast
Committee of Environmental Protection, the Committee of Agriculture, the Committee of Land Use,
the Northern Caucasus Branch of Russian Federation Research Institute of Integrated Use and
Protection of Natural Resources, regional and local officials and one or more collective farms. It is
very important that agricultural officials from the oblast and local area as well as farmers be involved
in the project from the beginning to get their input to the project and to assure their acceptance of the
project and the various land management practices which they will eventually be expected to
implement. This pilot would require the use of consultants or individuals with experience in
comprehensive watershed planning and in the development and implementation of agricultural
demonstration projects. Much of the work in carrying out the project would be undertaken by oblast
and river basin authority staff and their consultants in close collaboration with outside technical
consultation. It would be expected that some equipment for both monitoring (among others, mobile
environmental laboratories), construction of demonstration and for training and workshops will be
necessary. In addition, it is likely that travel abroad will be necessary for some oblast and
regional/local officials and local specialists to observe first hand some of the practices being utilized
in the USA or Western Europe countries. This subcomponent project would take place over
approximately a three year period.



STAFF APPRAISAL REPORT
RUSSUN FEERATION
ENVIRONMENFAL MANAGEMENT PROJECr
ANNEX 3.3.3
THE URALS REGIONAL INTEGRATED WATER RESOURCES MANAGEMENT FOR
THE EKATERINBURG WATER-SUPPLY SYSTEM
SUBCOMPONENT DESCRIPTION
A.    Background
1.    Water supply for the 1.5 million residents of Ekaterinburg comes from the Isset, Chusovaya,
and Ufa rivers. Six reservoirs provide raw water storage and a maximum safe yield of approximately
800,000 cubic meters per day. The current potable water demand is 600,000 cubic meters per day.
Many of the 200 industries in the area are served by the municipal water supply system, with a
significant portion of the treated water being used for non-potable purposes. Distribution system
leakage is reported by local officials to be 10 to 12 percent, with about 70 percent of the delivered
water going to residential use and 30 percent to industrial and commercial customers. The water
distribution system includes 150,000 cubic meters of treated water storage capacity, 8,300 water
meters, and 1,145 kilometers of distribution mains. There are 800 kilometers of sewers in the
wastewater collection system.
2.    The municipal water and wastewater systems are managed by the Ekaterinburg Vodokanal,
with support from local engineering consultants and contractors. There are two water treatment plants
with design capacities of 400,000 and 200,000 cubic meters per day. The larger (western) plant is
being expanded to 600,000 cubic meters per day. Two wastewater plants provide secondary
treatment. Design capacities are 550,000 and 180,000 cubic meters per day. Actual flows average
520,000 and 195,000 cubic meters per day, respectively. Wastewater flows exceed potable water
demands due primarily to six large industries that withdraw water directly from local rivers and
discharge wastewaters to the sewer system.
3.    According to the Sanitation and Epidemiological Service, approximately 50 percent of drinking
water samples fail to meet bacteria standards. Potential sources of contamination include inadequate
treatment, storage tanks, leaking mains, and cross connections. Some 30 to 40 percent of samples do
not meet one or more chemical standard. Analytical improvements and additional sampling are needed
to confirm these chemical results. One reason for the degraded drinking water quality is the lack of
sufficient revenues for proper water treatment and maintenance of the distribution system. Industrial
water rates are 180 rubles per cubic meter and residential rates are 2 rubles, 25 kopecs. At present,
rate increases are not feasible due to economic conditions and public perceptions and attitudes.
4.    At least three other agencies are actively involved in water supply management. Hydromet
collects weather and ambient water quality data (56 water quality stations in the Sverdlovsk Oblast are
sampled 1 to 2 times a month). The Committee on Water Resources is responsible for water source
development, reservoir operation, watershed protection, and river/reservoir monitoring. The Oblast
Environmental Committee is responsible for environmental protection, discharge monitoring, and
regulation. The large number of water management agencies make coordination, communication,
avoiding duplication, and maintaining adequate expertise and facilities difficult.  Monitoring,
laboratory facilities, and data management illustrate this problem. At least five organizations are
involved in defining monitoring needs, collecting samples, conducting laboratory analyses, and
evaluating the results.



146                                                           Environmental Management Project
5.     Major concerns among public health, environmental, and water supply agencies include: (1)
risks to public health and the environment from inadequate drinking water and wastewater treatment;
(2) inadequate source protection, water treatment, and distribution system design and maintenance that
results in a failure to consistently deliver acceptable water service; (3) lack of sufficient revenues and
public support to maintain and improve the water infrastructure and operations; (4) need for improved
technical capabilities, legislation, and economic incentives for optimum water allocations and reservoir
operations (e.g., water conservation, recycling, and reduced industrial use of potable water for non-
potable purposes); (5) increasing importance of watershed management, regulation, enforcement, and
pollution control in protecting water sources from existing and future impacts; and (6) need for
additional monitoring and laboratory capabilities (or efficiencies) that will provide data for effective
system operation and enforcement actions.
B.    Objectives
